THE RED PAGE.
Recent Reading.
Osbern and Ursynk, the new play by “John
Oliver Hobbes,” holds the pleasant futilities of a
clever London woman whose harvest is home and
whose gleanings are grainless stalks. Three acts
of contending emotions culminate in three deaths
and a verse of “ Dies Irae.” For the rest there
are Yeatsian lines—
The wood is volute with your paleness, sweet Arlette—
and Rabelaisian sentiments —
For indeed a man possesses most surely such good as
he can put into his mouth and swallow. The rest is all
appearance, phantasm, and the means to certain damna-
tion--
and nothing to keep or count.
G. W. Steevens’s war-letters From Capetown to
Ladysmith were hardly entitled to the dignity of
a Book. The “light-weight” feature of the
author’s work—that lack of solid force, possibly
resulting from a lack of vital force, which especially
differentiates Steevens from Forbes and Dr.
Russell —becomes more prominent than previously.
Steevens is the most brilliant of impressionists,
and in his account of the night after Elandslaagte,
for example, his picture-talent is well employed.
But the account of Elandslaagte itself is ineffective.
What is wanted is a plain, strong, Alisonish state-
ment of the where, why, and how of the battle,
and Steevens gives only glimpses in phrases.
Vernon Blackburn’s notice of the dead author is
loose and amorphous.
Steevens’s assertion of the E iglis'.i abuse of the
white flag is reprinted in “ The Story of Nichol-
son’s Nek ”
And then —and then again, that cursed white flag !
It is some consolation that for a long time the soldiers
refused to heed it. Careless now of life, they were sitting
up well behind their breastworks, altering their sights,
aiming coolly by the half-minute together. At the nadir
of their humiliation they could still sting—as that new-
come Boer found who, desiring one Englishman to his
bag before the end, thrust up his incautious head to see
where they were, and got a bullet through it.
What would be said of the Boers if they did this
sort of thing ?
The contrast between plain thinking and high
shrieking is especially marked in connection with
the Boer war. If any of the Jingoes were calm
enough to consider a priori arguments against
their error, the consensus of world-famous writers
on the historic plane would surely root a doubt.
The opinions of men like Spencer and Bryce can
be sneered at only by the ignorant. And in the
Feb. North American Review the German his-
torian Mommsen is found declaring that, on the
part of Britain, “this war is not only, as every
War is, a calamity, but also an infamy,” and char-
acterising it as “the repetition of Jameson’s Raid
by the English Government, dictated by banking
and mining speculations.” Which may be taken
as the opinion of every thinker uninfluenced by
patriotic bias or Rhodesian finance.
Ibsen’s new play, When We who are Dead
Awaken, does not seem likely to add to his
reputation. No English version is yet available ;
but Tolstoy, who read it in German, says “it is
simply a delirium, devoid of life, character, and
dramatic action.” And does Tolstoy’s judgment
count ? Yes ; a little—it depends on his point of
view. These lines explain Ibsen’s title and the
idea which he developes
Irene. We only see what is past beyond recall, when—
Rubeck. When? —
Irene. When we who are dead awaken.
Rubeck. Yes—and what do we see then ?
Irene. We see that we have never lived.
The chief characters are a man (Rubeck) and his
wife (Maja) who are tired of each other —and
another man (Ulfheim) —and another woman
(Irene). Each seeks sis own ends; and all go
hunting “on the mountains.” An avalanche
devours two ; two escape.
Rubeck appears as a polished man of the world with
high-sounding phrases in his mouth, but in his heart
ruthless selfishness. He is possessed of the evil spirit,
and leads Irene on to her destruction and his own.
Ulfheim, though devoid of all polish and refinement, is
yet able to recognise the danger on the mountain of
temptation, and therefore to save himself and his com-
panion from the gathering storm.
The meaning does not appear from this bald
synopsis: that fascinating game of Hunt the
Symbol may he played later on.
The pith of J. B. Atla/’s Celebrated Trials
is a good account of the Tichborne case—which
should have been perused by that Victorian Judge
who sent alleged-murderer Jones to the scaffold
protesting innocence with his last breath. The
Tichborne case proves that there is absolutely no
limit to the average person’s powers of identifica-
tion, A fixed idea is a treble bandage over the
eyes. From poor Lady Tichborne, who lost a
romantic young gentleman of 10-stone, and em-
braced in his place a 16-stone butcher who spelt
with his cleaver —down the whole list of Orton’s
witnesses—you see chiefly one thing : that who-
ever is.prepared to be deceived can be deceived till
Coleridge, Q.C., cross-examines—and afterwards.
And still, in Australia, we have devotees who wish
to set lunatic Creswell in the shoes of the man
drowned in the Bella !
A. P. Martin is “at it again” in The Literary
Year-Book for 1900. If there is one thing more
certain than another, it is that a man who has not
seen Australia for eighteen years is absolutely
disqualified from uttering ex-cathedra judgments
on Australian literature ; but, “ having introduced
Gordon to the English public,” Mr. Martin keeps
on—and London seemingly accepts him at his own
valuation. If lie would only write of the little
things lie knows about it would matter less ; but
when lie utters generalities like—
. . . taking a broad survey of Australia on the eve of
her federating into one great commonwealth, I point to
Mr. Henry Lawson and his rival, Mr. A. B. Paterson, as
the only two writers in whom the new generation will
feel any living 'interest
Tut ! tut 1
America, where the sun uses, as ..ad Kipling’s
“ new ” book From Sea to Sea for nearly
a year; England gets it later, and Australia
later still. It is becoming almost a fashion to
publish first in America, where readers and copy-
right wait for no man. From Sea to Sea is a
reprint of Kipling’s early travel-sketches—“ Let-
ters of Marque,” and so on; and uncommonly
good travel-sketches they are. Nowhere are the
writer’s clean stroke, his virile force, better seen ;
but perhaps two thick volumes of travel-sketches
at a time are too much —the pamphlet reprints
seemed to taste better. And lias n’t the mature
Kipling cut the viciousness out of the originals—
particularly on the American side !
The Black Man and the 1 Var,
It is the habit of the Australian J ingo parson,
following his brother in England, to defend Britain
for making war on the Bqers by pointing out how
badly the Boers have treated the natives. It is
“a war for humanity,” we are told. But there is
another side to the case, and the London Morning
Leader states it as follows :
Mr. Rhodes once complained with exceeding
bitterness that they think more of the natives at
home than the whole of South Africa.” In those
days the Churches were ranged against him. But
the times have changed, and Mr. Hugh Price
Hughes comes forward to plead in one breath for
Mr. Rhodes and the “niggers” who were once
such a thorn in his side. The Methodist Times
has discovered yet another pretext for destroying
the independence of the Transvaal.
Some apologists hold that we are waging war to
rescue the “ helots ” of Johannesburg from intoler-
able oppression, while Mr. Hays Hammond, of
the Consolidated Goldfields, has told us that it is
to dower them with two millions and a quarter in
increased dividends. Some ivill have it that we
are but repelling Boer aggression, while others say
that our armies are now busied in asserting British
prestige and Paramountcy. Mr. Hughes has an
entirely novel theory. He assures us that “ Eng-
land is now fighting against the slavery of the
African race as truly as the Northern States fought
against it in the Civil War. ” Whether we know
it or not, we are avenging the wrongs of the
Kaffirs whom the Dutch have so long oppressed—
for Mr. Hughes can hardly suppose that actual
slavery prevails in the Transvaal. It is a comfort-
able theory, though we doubt whether it would
justify the diplomatic provocations and the cam-
paign of lies that brought the war about. But is
it true ? Will the natives be the gainers when the
Union Jack floats over the Rand ?
* * * *
Before we consider the case of the Transvaal it
may be well to take a summary glance at the pre-
sent position of things in our own colonies and
possessions :
1. In the strip of East African coast—a
British Protectorate—which faces Zanzi-
bar, the full “legal status of slavery” is
maintained, and fugitive slaves have even
been handed back to their owners by
British officials.
2. In Zanzibar and Pemba the manumission
of slaves, presided over by Sir Arthur
Hardingb, is proceeding slowly, and many
thousands are still in bondage.
. 3. In Natal the corvee system prevails, and
all natives not employed by whites may
be impressed to labour for six months of
the year on the roads.
4. In Bechuanaland, after a recent minor
rebellion, natives were parcelled out among
the Cape farmers and indentured to them
as virtual slaves for a term of years.
5. Under the Chartered Company in Rhodesia
the chiefs are required, under compulsion,
to furnish batches of young natives to
work in the mines, and the ingenious plan
of taxing the Kaffir in money rather than
in kind lias been adopted so that he may
be forced to earn the pittance which the
prospectors are willing to pay him.
6. In Kimberley what is known as the “ com-
pound ” system prevails. All natives who
work in the diamond fields are required to
“reside” under lock and key, day and
night, in certain compounds, which re-
semble spacious prisons. So stringent is
the system that even the sick are treated
only within the prison yard. On no pre-
text whatever is a native allowed to leave
his compound.
After, all, the hands of the Northern States were
a little cleaner than our own when they championed
the cause of the slave.
w • *
It would .be easy to make an ex parte defence of
the Dutch, but we prefer instead to accept the
deliberate judgment of the only impartial writer
on South Africa known to us—Mr. Bryce. He
tells us that the laws of the Boer Republics are
indeed harsher than those of the English colonies
—though of course they in no way sanction slavery
—but, on the other hand, “ one often hears that
the Dutch get on better with their black servants
than the English do.” The attitude of both races
to the blacks he describes as “ contemptuous, un-
friendly, and even suspicious,” and elsewhere he
speaks of the ‘ ‘ deep and widespread aversion to
the colored people.” The only “ atrocity ” which
he mentions, though he gives it as an exceptional
case, was the work of Englishmen.
A white farmer—an Englishman, not a
Boer—flogged his Kaffir servant so severely
that the latter died ; and when the culprit
was put on his trial and acquitted by a white
jury, his white neighbors escorted him home
with a band of music.
We hear much of the Boer practice of flogging
natives with sjamboks. Mr. Bryce tells us that
in Rhodesia it is proposed to compel the natives
to work in the mines by means of the lash, “ de-
mands which,” he well says, “seem fitter for the
mouths of Spaniards in the sixteenth century than
for Englishmen in the nineteenth.” But there
are three broad facts which seem to show that the
Boers do not oil the whole very grievously oppress
the natives: —
1. The black population of the Transvaal has
increased under their rule from about
20,000 to nearly 700,000.
2. That they respect the honor of the native
women is proved by the fact that no race of
half-breeds is found in their territories.
S. -The blacks have not, as we were told they
would, taken advantage of this war to
wreak vengeance on their oppressors.
On the whole question we cannot perhaps do
better than say with Mr. Bryce that our country-
men “ have done so many things to be deplored,
that it does not lie with them to cast stones at the
Boers. . . . Only two sets of Europeans are free
from reproach, the imperial officials —and the
clergy.” If there has been anything to choose
between the attitude of Boers and Englishmen
towards the Kaffirs, the rather more humane be-
haviour of our countrymen is due, as Mr. Bryce
has told us, to the fact that, in days when England
was still proud that she had led the way in the
abolition of slavery, humanitarian feeling at home
did for a time ekfercise a fairly effective control
over our own colonists. But the party which Sir
Arthur Hardinge, our pro-Consul in Zanzibar,
once described in a Blue-book as “the anti-slavery
faction,” is not so strong to-day as it was when
Bright and Gladstone still lived.
We agree, therefore, with Mr. Hughes in.de-
ploring the Boer treatment of the native for the
same reason that we condemn the behaviour of
own colonists.. But to his contention that the
blacks will gain by the substitution of British for
Boer rule there is a clearer answer.
The Rand financiers have told us very plainly
what they mean to do when, as the price of
gallant lives and broken hearts, they rule as abso-
lutely in Johannesburg as they do in Kimberley.
At a meeting of the Consolidated "Goldfields Com-
of South Africa held in London on 14 Nov., the
consulting engineer boldly announced that under
English rule he hoped to cut down the wages of
the Kaffirs by one-half, and a director who fol-
lowed declared that they would compel the natives
to work for them. Here are some passages from
their speeches:—
Mr. Hays Hammond was tolerably frank:
“There are,” said Mr. Hammond, “in
South Africa millions of Kaffirs, and it does
seem preposterous that we are not able to
obtain 70,000 or 80,000 Kaffirs to work upon
the mines. . . . With good government there
should be an abundance of labor, and with an
abundance of labor there ivill be no difficulty in
cutting down wages, because it is preposterous
to pay a Kaffir the present wages. He would
be quite as well satisfied— in fact, he would
work longei—if you gave him half the amount.
(Laughter.) His wages are altogether dispro-
portionate to his requirements.” (Renewed
laughter.)
But the voice of the philanthropist spoke even
more clearly through Mr. Rudd
If they could only get one-half the natives
to work three months of the year it would
work wonders. He was not pleading for the
mines, or urging the views of capitalists, but
from the point of view of progress, agricul-
ture, public works, mines, and the general
prosperity of the country. They should try
some cogent form of inducement or practically
\ compel the native, through taxation or in some
other way, to contribute his quota to the good
of the community, and to a certain extent he
would then have to work. He was not advo-
cating slavery. As in everything else, there
were the use and abuse of labor, and there was
constantly the deliberate misuse of the word
slavery by those who wanted to raise it as a
bogey. If under the cry of civilisation we in
Egypt lately mowed down 10,000 or 20,000
Dervishes with Maxims, surely it cannot be
considered a hardship to compel the natives in
South Africa to give three months in the year to
doalittle honest work. We have in power to-day
a strong Government, but there is a morbid
sentimentality among a large section of the
community on the question of the natives, and
Government require the support of the
majority of their countrymen. Mr. Rhodes
started what was known as the Glen Gray
Act, tending to the regulation of native
labor. He knew that Sir Alfred Milner,
whom they all trusted so well, had travelled
- throughout the country, and was therefore
able now to form a pretty good opinion
with regard to the natives, and he con-
sidered that if Mr. Rhodes and Sir Alfred
Milner could put their heads together the
effect would be that they would be able to
propose something practical in this direction
which would make for the general welfare and
prosperity of the country.
As a study in complicated cant this would be
difficult to beat. In St. James’s Hall Mr. Hughes
preaches a crusade against slavery. In the City
Mr. Rudd chuckles at the prospect of forced
native labor won by the statesmanship of Mr.
Rhodes and Sir Alfred Milner. Yet Mr. Rudd
and Mr. Hughes are both defending the same war.
* * * *
What Mk. Froude said of the Dutch treat-
ment of Natives.
“If we compare the success of the Dutch in
the management of uncivilised tribes with our
own, in all parts of the world, it will he found
that although their rule is stricter than ours
and to appearance harsher, they have had
fewer native wars than we have had. There
has been less violence and bloodshed, and the
natives living under them have not been less
happy or less industrious.” — Oceana, p. 37.
* • * *
An Authoritative Confirmation.
In a pamphlet entitled Blacks and Whites in
South Africa, the secretary of the Aborigines Pro-
tection Society (Mr. H. R. Fox Bourne) thus
sums up the position :
“Unless it is accompanied by very great
and comprehensive changes in British policy
towards natives, the contemplated overthrow
of Boer rule can bring them no benefit.”
Wilfrid Blunt, at all events, is a Born Irrecon-
cilable. His “Satan Absolved” seems often
hysterical enough ; but it contains the root of the
matter. For example—
Hast Thou not heard the chanting ? Nay, Thou dost not
hear,
Or Thou hadst loosed Thy hand like lightning in the clear
To smite their ribald lips with palsy, these false priests,
These Lords who boast Thine aid at their high civic feasts,
The ignoble shouting crowds, the prophets of their Press,
Pouring their daily flood of bald self-righteousness,
Their poets who write big of the “ White Burden.”
Trash !
The White Man’s Burden, Lord, is the burden of his cash.
—There. Thou hast heard the truth. Thy world, Lord
God of Heaven,
Lieth in the hands of thieves who pillage morn and even.
And Thou still sleepest on !
Not fiction, but religion, makes the best-selling
books. Spurgeon’s sermons are credited with a
sale of one hundred millions !. A writer in
Lond. Puritan declares that the number of these
sermons sold since 1855 exceeds th 6 number of
Bibles circulated since the beginning of the cen-
tury—and the British and Foreign Society alone
prints five tons of Bibles daily.
Verses by P. b. Marston.
, LOVE LIES A-DYING.
Come in gently, and speak low,
Love lies a-dying ;
By his death-bed, standing so,
Hush, hush your crying.
Once his eyes were full of light,
Who now lies a-dying ;
Round about him falls the night,
Hush, hush your crying.
Ghostly winds begin to blow,
Love lies a-dying ;
Hark where distant waters flow,
Hush, hush your crying.
Prom a Land of Lost Delight-
Now he lies a-dying—
Visions come to haunt his sight,
Hush, hush your crying.
h rom a land he used to know—
Jjove lies a-dying—
Ghosts of dead songs come and go,
Hush, hush your crying.
Perished hopes like lilies white—
Now he lies a-dying—
Leave beside him, in Death’s night,
Hush, hush your crying.
Round about him, to and fro—
Nov/ ho lies a-dying—
Phantom feet move soft and slow,
Hush, hush your crying.
Sharply once did sorrow bite,—
O Love lies a-dying!— '
Tears and blood sprang warm and bright
Hush, hush your crying.
Pain is done now; strength is low,—
Love lies a-dying,—
Let him gently languish so,
Hush, hush your crying.
AT LOVES GRAVE
Now we stand above Love’s grave,
Shall we weep,—
We who saw and would not save?
Let him sleep.
Shall we sing his requiem ?
Ah, for what ?
Better stand hero, cold and dumb;
Vex him not.
He was young and strong and fair,
Myrtle-crowned ;
Now no myrtle wreathes his hair,
Cypress bound.
Did we slay him ? Nay, not we ;
We but said,
“ Doubts and bitter words must be.”
He is dead !
Of these doubts and words he died.
Hush; keep still!
Late regreta would but deride.
One calm will,
Perfect peace and perfect faith—
Had these been,
He had never chanced on death,
Never seen
Darkness of the under night
Where he lies,
No song on his lips, no light
In his eyes.
Leave him where he lies alone,
Void of care;
Only carve upon his stone —
“ Love was fair.”
LOVE'S RESURRECTION.
I made a grave for dead Love to lie in ;
And I dug it deep in a grassy place,
With trees above it for winds to sigh in,
Or birds to make song in through blithe Spring
days.
Then I stretched myself on the grave in sorrow,
Remembering Love, and how fair was he;
Yesterday poisoned the thought of the morrow—
Blank morrow in which no Love should be.
And I cried, “ O Love, thou'wert full of splendor
And pomp and dominion but yesterday,
Thy voice was kind and thine eyes were tender j
But all, now, all, has been taken away.
“ Love, canst thou hear?” But the wind moaned
only,— .
Only the grass on Love’s grave was stirred ;
In the trees above sang, faint and lonely,
One sad, little, bright-eyed, unmated bird.
The twilight fell as I lay and wept there;
And dew dropped silently, wetting my face;
Alone my weary vigil i kept there,
Till the moon arose in its placid grace :
And the air was charged with her benediction.
And my desolate heart was soothed and nllea
With a spirit of some divine prediction,—
And suddenly all my weeping was stilled.
Then I beheld, in the moonlight tender, # .
A presence more bright than the moon s pai
fire j ,
For there stood Love, with increase of splendor,
And my heart was made one with my heai
desire.
[Philip Bourke Marston, born 1850, died 1887,
lished during his short life several volumes of]
marked by delicate fancy and poignant feeling.
blind bv the result of an early accident; and. as
educated wholly by ear, so the whole of his verse .
dictated. A complete collection is edited £>y
Chandler Moulton.]

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mortal Sin.
"... Pish !
He’s a good fellow, and ’twill all be well.”
Fitz Gerald’s “ Omar,”
A pail* of terror-stricken baby eyes,
That blankly stare at me in dread surprise :
There are no chances to prevaricate,
Caught in the act by an avenging fate !
Was not that currant-bush forbidden fruit?
Alas ! the reckless criminal is mute.
Did I not give particular command ?
But there ! It seems she cannot understand.
Poor baby ! what a guilty, hopeless glance !
I cannot keep my solemn countenance
As I behold her in her woeful plight,
But laugh Ha ha 1 Ha ha /—and kiss the mite.
She stood before the throne of God on high,
A quaking soul beneath her Maker s eye ;
And the despair of certain, hopeless doom
Cast even Heaven’s brightness into gloom.
The knowledge of unpardonable crime,
Of sullied innocence and wasted time,
Of reckless disregard for human law
Burnt in her consciousness ; and faint with awe
She trembled on the brink of livid Hell,
Just waiting for the sentence fierce and fell.
And God beheld her, steeped in mortal sin,
And laughed Ha hal Ha ha /—and thrust her in !
N.Q.
VON KOTZE.
Love and Intuition.
She bent very close in the dark till her hair
Brushed over his cheek.
’Twas as silk, and the scent of it perfumed the air;
But the Voice of the Past, it had whispered
“ Beware ! ”
Had he hearkened it speak.
Close, closer she crept, and her arm wound him
round
Till, searched through and sunned
By a perilous sweetness, his reason uncrowned
In the dust tumbled down ; in her witchery found
A brilliance that stunned.
But he paused yet a little ; he heard from afar
The Voice of the Past;
“Her blood and thy blood, they are daggered for
war;
S )6 was bom from the first not to make thee, but
mar,”
And his soul stood aghast.
“ Her hands they are red with the blood of thy
sires;
Ancestrally, she
Drank as incense the fumes that coiled up from
their pyres,
Their moans were more sweet than the sweetest of
lyres
Low-toned o’er the sea.
“ Canst thou touch her and kiss her who wrought
thee this wrong,
Who dimmed thy race-star ?
Her soul and thy soul are asurge and athrong
With foe-forces that warred through the centuries
long,
And for ever shall war.
His fingers unclasped her wreathed fingers; she
felt
The ice at their tips.
Then, firing, she sought with new ardours to melt
The frost in his veins, cooing softly, and knelt,
And so moist were her lips,
lied and moist in the dark, that, enslaved by her
charms,
He stooped then and there,
And locked in her beautiful prison of arms
He laughed at the Past, the instinctive alarms
That bade him beware.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
“ Ancient ” : Had extensive dental opera-
tion by Sydney doctor under gas and ether. Had
very bad dreams. Thought I was sailing a boat
in a race through the swing opening in Pyrmont
bridge. Man standing on bridge put his arm
round my throat and held the boat back whilst
*he other beggars sailed away from me. My
struggles whilst choking, as I thought, were some-
thing awful, and when I came-to it was amongst
the wreckage of a beautifully-finished dental chair.
Dentist took it like a lamb and never billed me
for a penn’orth of damage.
It was midnight when Smiggs crept out,
and after listening, cautiously proceeded to his
right-hand-neighbor’s yard, opened the gate and
disappeared. Soon afterwards a soft thud broke
the stillness ; then another, and another. Bagster,
who dwelt next door to Smiggs on the left-hand
side heard and located the sound. Something
was being dropped into Smiggs’s yard. He made
his way to where the sound was.. As he stood
there listening, something fell at his feet; it was
a chunk of wood. Then he knew—Smiggs was
stealing his neighbor’s fuel. Should he raise an
alarm ? No ! Perish the thought! Besides, he
was out of wood himself. Picking up each piece
as it fell, he transferred it over the fence to his
own yard, taking care to drop it where it would
make the least noise. Then hearing Smiggs
climbing up the fence Bagster fled silently with
the last piece of timber under his arm, . and
vanished like a baseless aspersion in the night.
And Smiggs, after an hour’s hard wood-stealing,
dropped into a yard that was as bare of timber as
when he started.

Special Notice
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The. Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“Correspondence” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Perse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, MAY 5, 1900.
The Sydney Wharf Resumptions.
A few days ago almost three-fourths of the
members of N.S.W. Legislative Assembly
petitioned the Lyne Government to resume
the wharves and wharf frontages of Sydney
harbor, and Premier Lyne practically pro-
mised that it should be done. This is one
of the many beneficial results of the Plague.
The same subject has been discussed in a
languid fashion for years, but, until the up-
rising of the bubonic rat along the infected
shores of Darling Harbor, very few people
took a serious interest in the matter. Now
the Government is urged by an overwhelm-
ing majority of the Assembly to buy back
the germy banks and beaches of Darling
Harbor “as the only guarantee against
the present disastrous Plague getting a per-
manent hold on Sydney.” The ONLYguaran-
tee, be it observed. The petitioners admit
that they have no hope of making the private
wharf-owner clean and reasonable. They have
no expectation that he will cease to slay his
fellow-men in order to save cleaning ex-
penses, by permitting the sodden garbage of
ages to fester underneath his property. In
this time of trouble even the bitterest oppon-
ents of State enterprise appeal to State
enterprise as the only thing that can save
them. They always do at such times and in
such circumstances. The fine old Tory
fossils of the Bank of New Zealand, after
objecting to “State interference” all the
days of their lives, went down on their knees
to Digger Dick Seddon to pray for State
interference as soon as private enterprise
had brought them to ruin. So did the fine
oid conservatives who ran the Q.N.B. on the
rocks. The caving banks of Sydney, in ’93,
promptly leaned on the broad, aggravated
bosom of the Dibbs Government for assist-
;ince —and so on ad infinitum. No doubt
many of the people who now call on the
State to abolish the private wharf-owner
because his dirtiness is incurable will revile
State enterprise and boost up the old doc-
trine of private capitalism as enthusiastically
as ever—when the Plague scare is over. But
for the present State Socialism is a harbour
of refuge and a tower of strength.
• • * *
Within two days after Premier Lyne’s
assurance that he intended taking over the
private wharves and wharf frontages of
Sydney, interviews with the owners of these
properties began to appear in the papers.
The owners, as a rule, didn’t speak in a
spirit of hostility—far from it. But they
mentioned with all their might the vast
sums private enterprise had spent on these
dirty wharves, and the immense value of the
“ private interests ” that had grown up, and
the truly immense amounts the resumption
would cost. They didn’t urge the Ministry
to drop the idea and save these immense
amounts. Rather they seemed inclined to
advocate that the Ministry should go right
on and face the expense. In a general
way they are probably willing that the State
should take their property for the public
good at 50 or 100 per cent, above its value,
if the State can’t be induced to give 850 per
cent, above value—after the style of the
State land resumption scandals and swindles
of the olden time. At all events all feel-
ing to the contrary has been carefully con-
cealed up to date.
* * * •
If the Lyne Government intends to pre-
serve any semblance of a reputation for
honesty, and the Sydney wharf resumptions
are not to be one more job added to many
previous malodorous resumption jobs, it will
be necessary to grievously disturb the equa-
nimity of these owners. The old idea of
resuming the property in a large, off-
hand fashion, and leaving the value
to be fixed by an arbitration court, is
played out. N.S.W. has “been there” be-
fore. The arbitration court, as a rule,
has no personal knowledge, or doesn’t use
its personal knowledge, of property values.
It is guided by the evidence of experts of
high standing in the city, and experts of the
very highest standing are only too often
persons who tell the most blushless yarns for
a fee—and not a very large fee either. Not
so very long ago an Australian property
expert, in a land resumption case where the
Government was the victim, attested that
the value of certain property that the State
had taken over was so much ; and when it
was pointed out to him that he had quite
recently declared to the same Government
that the value of the same property for taxa-
tion purposes was a mere fraction of this
sum, he didn’t even turn a hair. He not
only admitted quite cheerfully that he had
valued the land, when the State was to
collect taxes on it, at far less than he valued
it at when the State was to buy it, but he
apparently failed to see any sin or disgrace
in the proceeding. The court was so
accustomed to such things that it apparently
saw nothing specially objectionable about it
either. And it is questionable if there are
many well-known land-experts who don’t
act in exactly the same manner—for a
moderate fee.
* ■* * *
This time the resumption business should
be done on plain business lines. So far as
concerns the values of the frontages, apart
from improvements, the Government and
the owners agreed on them some years ago
when the land-tax valuations were made,
and values haven’t increased since then,
as proved by Coghlan’s Statistics and
many other authorities. In almost every
case, if not in every case, the figure
finally agreed upon for land-tax pur-
poses was more than what the owners de-
clared to be the real value ; so if the State
resumes the land and pays this sum (which is
more than the owners’ estimate of the worth
of their property) for it, it is quite impos-
sible that the owners can be aggrieved. Or,
better still, the value of the lands and
buildings and all improvements can
be ascertained from the municipal valu-
ation ; this would be a still more reliable
gpide, seeing that it includes the entire
property. The municipal valuation shows
the worth of everything that has to be re-
sumed, and if Parliament enacts that the
whole property is to be bought on the muni-
cipal valuation, with (say) 10 per cent, at
most added for disturbance, no one can have
any just right to complain.
* * * *
Of course the plea advanced will be that it
is a vile fraud on the proprietor to resume
his property on the municipal valuation see-
ing that it was not made for resumption pur-
poses. The Bulletin, for one, fails to see
where this argument comes in. What is
wanted is a fair valuation that will do for any
purpose—not one made for resumption pur-
poses and cooked accordingly. The munici-
pal valuation must be reasonably correct ;
no wharf - owner would be such a
swindler as to defraud the muni-
cipal treasury by getting his valuation
reduced below the true figure. If he has
been such a swindler, he should certainly
pay for his own swindle. When the valua-
tion was made he didn’t know', of course,
that circumstances would compel him to sell,
and get a specially high valuation made ac-
cordingly ; neither did the State know that
circumstances would compel it to buy, and
get a specially low' valuation made accord-
ingly—so one party is no more aggrieved
than the other. The municipal valuation
is that of an independent third party —a
retrospective arbitrator and the owner
accepted it. What reasonable person could
ask more than an independent valuation
which the owner has, in every case, accepted
as quite high enough —except when he said
it was too high 1 And if there is a doubt,
the State is quite as much entitled to get the
benefit of it as the private owner. More-
over, in this special case, the State is more
entitled to it, for it did not force the trans-
action—the transaction is largely forced upon
it by the wharf-owner. It is to a great
extent his own personal dirtiness, foulness,
neglect, and want of consideration for his
fellows, that make the transaction compul-
sory in the public interest. He has driven
the public to buy him out, and, having done
so, it is very liberal treatment if he gets the
value which he has all along declared in
his dealings with the public to be either
enough or a great deal too much. And in
strict justice he shouldn’t be compensated
for disturbance seeing that he has compelled
the State to disturb him. Instead he should
pay the State for expenses incurred in the
disturbing process.
• * • •
But if the State does not take this com-
mon-sense view, and it decides to settle the
value of the resumed property by arbitration
—that process by which the public always
pays from times to eight tifnes a fair
value—there is at least one precaution that
it must take—unless the Lynk Government
has an absolute genius for fooling away its
opportunity. In the Wharves and Fore-
shores Resumption Act it should at least be
set forth as a cast-iron enactment that, when
the value of any man’s property is fixed, for
resumption purposes, at more than the
municipal valuation, that value should be
declared to be the municipal valuation for
the last 20 years, or as much thereof
as covers the period of his owner-
ship. And from the purchase money
the State should deduct and hand
over to the municipality the amount of
municipal taxes which the owner, by his
own showing, has diddled the munici-
pality out of, through the undervaluation
of his property, during these years, to-
gether with compound interest thereon at 5
per cent.—said money to be added to the
municipal sinking funds in reduction of the
city debt. This is a strictly just and fair
and honest idea—in fact, it is giving a large
concession to the landholder. Sydney pro-
perty in 1900 is just about the same value as
in 1880. If any landholder can convince the
Arbitration Court that his property is worth
£lO,OOO, and it has been municipally valued
at only £5OOO, then he practically demon-
strates that he has swindled the city out of half
its lawful taxes for 20 years, and he should
pay up with interest. In fact, he proves
that he has swindled the city out of more
than half, for the property that was worth
£lO,OOO in 1880, before the boom, and in
1900, after it, was probably worth £20,000
in some intervening years, and if the State
says nothing about that it is treating the
owner liberally. And the land-tax that the
State has been diddled out of should be
similarly recovered—with compound in-
terest and a heavy fine for making false re-
turns. But these are complicated methods,
and it is vastly better to take the municipal
valuation and stick to it.
* • * *
Whichever way the resumption value is
arrived at, the money should not be raised
by the usual process of floating a loan in
London. Borrowing in London means often
a heavy discount, always a heavy loss in
stamp duty, expenses, exchange on remit-
tance of interest, loss of income-tax on in-
terest, and other items. The State should
simply pay the resumed landlords by giving
them State securities at 3 or per cent.,
interest thereon payable in Sydney and no-
where else, and subject to income-tax as a
matter of course. And it should also be
enacted that separate accounts be kept of
the interest payable on this purchase money
and on all other sums expended in wharf-
building, reclamation, and similar improve-
ments, and of all revenue derived from
the property. And that the profit, when
there is one, shall not go into ordinary
revenue, but be applied in repayment of the
borrowed money, until it is all repaid and
the State has its wharves and water-frontages
absolutely free from debt.
* * * *
Ex-Premier Reid rose the other day to
say that he was against the proposed re-
sumption scheme because, in his experience,
the State was always swindled over such re-
sumptions. That was another way of saying
that Reid and his predecessors lacked the
brains and the honesty to save the State
from being swindled. Premier Lyne has
a chance to make a name for himself as
the first N.S.W. Premier who conducted a
big land-resumption scheme without allow-
ing the State to be- milked.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
One a.m. Verdicts.
Judge Gibson sentenced a man named Ensob to
death at Broken Hill lately—sentenced him at
something after 1 o’clock in the morning. The
case was called at 2 o’clock p.m.; it was 10 o’clock
when the weary, overwrought prisoner—he made
his first appearance in court in this case —had his
only chance to speak in his defence. Of course he
wandered, and blundered, and got himself tangled
in irrelevancies, and lost himself in trifles, and left
out things which the jury were waiting to hear
about. After that the Crown counsel, who had
been travelling three nights and days, said what
he could for the man, and then the Judge rapidly
got through his address. The jury, which had
been sitting nightly till 10 o’clock, was sent out at
midnight—and the Court waited for the twelve
to come back. At 1 o’clock they filed in
with a verdict of guilty, to which they added
a strong recommendation to mercy. But when
they were asked (at 1 o’clock in the morning) on
what grounds they based their recommendation,
the foreman had no answer ready ; so the Judge
told them it was no good. They wanted to go
out again to set their reasons in order ; but though
they were not actually refused, proceedings were
pushed on (it was nearing 2 o'clock), and
the prisoner was sentenced. It was made clear
then that his Honor did not appreciate the recom-
mendation ; brushing it aside, be told the man in
the dock that he (the J udge) could hold out no
hope whatever of mercy. And now the question
is whether the Judge or the jury is to prevail?
There is, unhappily, no provision in Australian
law by which a jury may find a prisoner guilty in
the first, or second, or third degree, as there
should be ; all that a jury can do when it does
not bebeve a prisoner deserves the severest
penalty is to recommend him to mercy, or, going
still further, to strongly recommend him. If
those recommendations are flouted, then juries
will be more than ever indisposed to convict in
cases where the offence is not of the very worst
sort. In this particular case the reasons for the
strong recommendation to mercy are obvious
enough; if it hadn’t been one o’clock in the
morning, and if the jury hadn’t known that Judge
and counsel and witnesses and the rest were wait-
ing out of bed for them, they would probably
have had a dozen reasons ready. .In fact, every
man could, no doubt, have given his own reasons
off-hand ; but no one was authorised to say what
were the jury’s official reasons. After it has
been decided (as in the circumstances it unques-
tionably ought to be) that this particular prisoner
shall not be hanged, those whose duty it is to see
that every accused person gets a fair trial will
have to ascertain what led to this indecent one
o’clock scramble, and especially if any official’s
personal convenience was being consulted. Mid-
night court proceedings in any case are most un-
desirable ; and where a life is at stake they are
hardly defensible under any conceivable circum-
stances. But it would be absolutely intolerable if
they were held simply to suit somebody’s—any-
body’s—private convenience ; and it is to be
hoped that the inquiry will, at any rate, set this
possibility aside.
Seddon’s Apologist.
Thk Canterbury Times (Maoriland), which con-
sistently supports Premier Skddon whether lie is
right or wrong, thus backs him in his eleventh-
hour interference over the Commonwealth Consti-
tution :
A Road Board, setting out to close a public road, is
compelled to invite residents and others to state any ob-
jections they may have to offer to the proposed action.
Similarly, any other public body that may contemplate
some action likely to cause inconvenience or injury to
private individuals affords those individuals an oppor-
tunity of objecting to the action, and in any case we
presume that the Supreme Court is open to any man who
may consider himself injured. But in the matter of
Federation, our Australian friends would deny us the
right to state our objections to the proposed constitution.
It is not as though Maoriland had attempted to inter-
fere with the domestic clauses of the Bill. This colony
must either remain out of the Commonwealth or
it must become a Federal province, and we are there-
fore bound to consider our interests, both as an
outside State and as a possible candidate for admission.
In either case our rights would he infringed under
the present constitution. We should have no power to
make a reciprocity treaty with any Federal State which
might be willing to enter into such an arrangement, and
there is grave reason to fear that such a treaty could not
be made by the Federal Parliament. Then in the matter
of judicial appeals, a case between a Melbourne firm and a
Maoriland firm would have to be settled, presumably,
in the Federal Courts, unless, indeed, the Federal Parlia-
ment granted the parties permission to appeal to the
Privy Council. Moreover, if Maoriland should seek to
enter the Federation she would have to submit to the
conditions laid down by the Federal Parliament, and
should any dispute arise as to the interpretation of the
clauses of the constitution which provide for the admis-
sion of new States, the matter would have to be settled
by the Federal Courts.
If that is the best explanation Digger Dick’s own
party-organ can give of his action, it is a remark-
ably poor one. The complaint that Maoriland
would have no power under the Bill to make com-
mercial treaties with any separate State of the
Commonwealth is absurd ; no Government allows
part of its country to make commercial treaties,
and thus establish a tariff of its own, apart from
that of the country at large. If Victoria tried to
make a separate commercial treaty with Dunedin,
and get Dunedin to make its own tariff arrange-
ments in the matter, fhis would probably become
apparent. The complaint that the Bill doesn’t
allow even the Federal Government to make com-
mercial treaties with Maoriland is equally absurd;
the Bill specifically empowers the Federal Parlia-
ment to deal with “the regulation of trade and
commerce with other countries,” and also with
“ external affairs and treaties.” If Maoriland
wants to enter the Federation, and feels it must
get there, it will, of course, have to do it “on the
conditions laid down by the Federal Parliament.'
It is equally true that, if the Federation feels it
must have Maoriland in the union, it must unite
with that province on the conditions laid
down by the Maoriland Parliament. The
present arrangement is that, at any future
time, Maoriland and Australia may unite if
terms can be arrived at agreeable to both
parties. If anything fairer than that can
be devised this paper doesn’t know what it is, and
Seddon doesn’t know either. The fact that the
Federal Government can’t be compelled to let
Maoriland join it unless it likes the terms is no
more horrible than the fact that the Maoriland
Government can’t be compelled to let Australia
join it unless it likes the terms. The spectacle of
a Dunedin firm suing a Melbourne firm, and being
compelled to accept the decision of the Australian
Court of Appeal at, say, Bombala, is not one whit
more tearful than that of a Melbourne firm suing
a Dunedin firm and being compelled to accept the
decision of Maoriland’s Court of Appeal in Lon-
don. As to the complaint that Australia wants
to deny Maoriland the right to object to Austra-
lia’s Constitution, Australia hasn’t the right to
object to Maoriland’s Constitution, and if it
had the unspeakable assurance to do so the
language of Digger Dick should be worth listen-
ing to. And even Seddon’s most abject apologist
never touches on the worst aspect of the case.
That is—that Seddon asked for none of these
alterations till the three Conventions were over,
and the Premiers’ conference was over, and until
the Bill was finished, and it had been accepted by
five Parliaments and passed by nine referendum
votes. And then he asked that all the work of
five years should be pulled to pieces to insert
something which he hadn’t the common courtesy
to propose before the Bill was finished, and instead
of first asking Australia to agree to certain things
he proposed that the British Government should
compel it to do so. It was for this reason
that The Bulletin said Richard Seddon had
exhibited the manners of a person who ought to
dine by himself in the scullery—and even his own
warmest supporter doesn’t attempt to show, on
this point, that he is a person who would look
well dining anywhere else. In fact, considering
the utterly selfish lack of consideration which he
showed for everybody save himself, this paper
isn’t sure that he wouldn’t look best of all dining
on the floor.


An Open Door.
“Rate-pater ” writes to The Bulletin :—
In the history of Sydney Municipal Council there are a
few pages which reflect in anything but a creditable
manner on the acumen or honesty of the various Aider-
men and Mayors. Among these pages are those upon
which are written the particulars of the Town Hall
Foundations, the Iron Railings, the Coonanbarra Affair,
and the big defalcations of several of the Council’s em-
ployes. The result of all these “affairs” may be con-
densed into that one very expressive American
idiom—“ Boodle.” Boodle, according to a standard
slang dictionary, means plunder, booty, perquisites,
profit ; and Sydney, a city where there exists
no recognised or discoverable Tammany Ring, has
indeed an exceedingly dishonorable record in the
wav' of Boodling. Experience teacheth. Does it ? W ith
the example of that sapient Council before us, one is justi-
fied in doubting the maxim, or else in rating Sydney’s aider-
men and Mayor as people on whom experience is wasted.
They have delegated the duties, to perform which they
are elected, to contractors in the past, and they have been
bled. Now they are going to do some more delegating.
The chief excuse for their existence is the sanitary regula-
tion of the city ; and this prime function they l are going
to hand over to—well, a syndicate. This contract is not
like a railway or Town Hall job which is practically done
once for all. It extends over a term of years. The
case stands thus: The Council proposes to
hand over the garbage and filth-collecting of the
city to a company for ten years, and to pay the said co.
somewhere about £30,000 a year to do what they, as
aldermen, are elected to do themselves. What a beautiful
open door ! Study the history of contracts in N.S.W.,
and recall all the extras claimed and paid, the allegations
of corruption, the several inquiries made, and the revela-
tions thereat. In the past, several Australian cities have
tried the experiment of letting out the city cleaning by
contract. It was a failure every time. Most times it was
a shameful and dirty and utterly disreputable failure.
Consider the danger a company with £30,000 a year, plus
extras (there are always extras), for ten years is to the
city of Sydney. What hope has a reform alderman
opposed to such a concern of fighting it in a city where a
man has no chance of election under £200? And how
many men of straw could such a company (I am not
alleging anything against this special company) find, and
back, and get elected, to go in and vote it more extras,
higher rates, or an extension of its lease or contract? The
whole business looks like a Tammany germ imported
direct from New York. And as prevention is better than
cure, this delegation by the council of its powers to a
non-elected authority should be squashed at once.
♦
Fixing 1 the Responsibility.
Victoiua’s lamentable authorities are patiently
inquiring into the reasons why the Glenefg carried
her rusted bones down into the great deep. The
results, so far as they have gone, would be more
interesting if they were less monotonous. In nine
such cases out of ten the explanation is that the
vessel was old. moth-eaten and rusted, and that
the mildew of years was in her very bones ; that
the marine authorities whose duty it is to find out
such vessels and compel their adequate repair
didn’t do their duty, that nobody expected them
to do it, and that nothing ever happened to them
for not doing it. Also, that the owners wouldn’t
adequately repair their vessels unless compelled to
do so, raid that no one ever expected the owners to
be otherwise, and that nothing ever yet happened
to any owner in this country for not being other-
wise. The general tone of the evidence against the
Glenelg is that she was old, and her plates were
worn thin, and that a man could knock a ham-
mer through quite a lot of them. It is not alleged
that she was distinctly cracked or split or leaky
anywhere, but that she had the flavor of old age
spread all over. This story has been told very
often in such cases. There are certain officials
who are supposed to make disparaging remarks
about such vessels, and even to prevent them
going to sea, but they are never prosecuted for
manslaughter, or made to pay damages, or fined,
or so much as sacked, for not doing it. No one
expects that they will be. No one seriously
demands that they shall be. No Government
passes any law to make them criminally or civilly
responsible for their neglect, or docks their
salary, or does anything to them. It would do so if
Parliament demanded it very badly, but Parlia-
ment doesn’t. Parliament would if the people
were in dead earnest about the matter, but the
people aren’t. No law is ever passed making
shipowners criminally responsible for the
lives lost through their neglect. They are
civilly responsible, of course, but the famous
Alert case shows how much that amounts
to. In that instance the widow of a drowned
man was awarded damages, but an infamous legal
system allowed the owners to appeal for one new
trial after another, and a judiciary which calls for
the highest disrespect gave them new trials till
the case spread out into a good number of years,
and the verdict was mostly, if not wholly, swal-
lowed up in costs. Most of the people concerned
are not legally responsible, and there is no net
cash to be got out of those who are responsible if
they choose to take advantage of the law’s delays.
The Alert case might have suggested a need for
limiting the number of new trials a rich litigant
could get, but it was nobody’s business in parti-
cular. As to the moral liability, it appears that
the owner isn’t seriously accountable because an
inspector passed his vessel as seaworthy. The
inspector isn’t fully accountable because he had so
much to do that he couldn’t make a proper exami-
nation if he wanted to—he says he wanted to.
And the people above him don’t remember ever
hearing that there weren’t enough inspectors for
efficient inspection. And nobody ever heard that
there was no money to pay any more inspectors.
Responsibility vanishes in a haze, and if it ever
brings up short against anybody that person is
either dead or he is the Government. And each
member of the Government asks, quite reason-
ably, if anybody expects a man to be a heaven-
bom statesman, financier, administrator, boss of
half-a-dozen departments, a person who can draft
Bills, run a Parliament, be a guide and friend and
father to over 1,000,000 people, know all about
almost every thing—for less than the salary of the
manager of a small insurance office. And no one
knows why he doesn’t get more salary. But de-
. cayed steamers go down all the same.

What Britain’s 250,000 are Fighting.
The subjoined interesting item appears in the
ultra-Jingo London Daily Mail, from the pen of
its war-correspondent, the eminent Julian Ralph,
who has been interviewing Cecil Rhodes. Surely
Australian Jingodom will accept Cecil Rhodes
as an authority !—lie of all men should know what
he is talking about in such a matter :
Mr. Rhodes surprised me very much by saying that
there have only been SO,OOO Boers against us in the field.
I replied that the idea at military headquarters is that
there are 65,000 of them against us. “ 1 know,” he said,
“ that is their method of accounting for their reverses and
avoiding blame at home for their mistakes, but, neverthe-
less, there are but SO,OOO Boers in this war. Look over
the census reports in your Whitaker, and you will see
that it cannot be otherwise. Men cannot be made in a
minute. It takes 20 years to make a man, and ive know how
many they had at the beginning of the roar. The Trans-
vaal only polled a little over 17,000 votes at. the last. Presi-
dential election, and even the boys voted. That State has
put in 15,000 men, and the Free State not quite so many.
As for the foreigners in their army, do you know the
facts? You have heard the foreigners accounted for by
thousands, yet the Irish ‘ brigade ’ is but one hundred
strong, there are only 150 Scandinavians, and for the rest
— they amount to between 1000 and 2000. They appear
more numerous because they keep shifting their men
around. The people who were besieging us (at Kimberley)
used to go away and fight Methuen at Magersfontein. and
at the other places before that. No, we are exa ugtrading
their numbers simply because by doing so we account for
bad generalship without confessing it.’’
Clean Dictators and Dirty Dictators.
It is announced that theN.S.W. Government has
been considering the necessity for sweeping away
the worst of Sydney’s slums, and it is further
added that “in the absence of a body like the
London County Council, with the powers that
Greater Sydney might be entrusted with, Mr.
Lyne thinks that the Government should come
forward and energetically tackle the problem of
improving the colony’s metropolis.” Which is
another way of keeping a dog and yet barking
personally. Also it is a shiftless, improvident
way of getting out, or partly out, of a difficult
situation. The Government provided Sydney
with a municipal body (or rather 41 of them) to
keep its finances straight, and then it straightened
them itself a good many years ago by taking over
the city debt. It provided a City Council to
clean Sydney, and it is now cleaning Sydney
itself. It provided a Corporation and a City
Improvement Board to. abolish slum properties,
and now it proposes to abolish them itself. Pre-
mier Lyne admits that the reason why things are
as they are (exactly how they are being really too
horrible to mention) is the want of a body like
the London County Council—a body representing
and governing the whole metropolis and elected
on a wide suffrage. In that case, either the
Government must create such a body, make it an
institution which will rest on the widest possible
suffrage, give it such authority that the biggest
and most ambitious men in the community will
struggle for seats on it, give it abundant powers
of taxation, lay on it the responsibility of re-con-
structing Sydney, and give it a fair start by
devising some means of cheaply consolidating and
straightening out the tangled finances of the 41
municipalities. Or else it must do some repairs
and alterations itself, and then leave the old
dreadful institutions to let things drift into the
old dirt and confusion again. It takes a strong
man and a man of genius to completely re-organise
the self-governing powers of Sydney—to see what
is wrong, and to know how to put it right, and to
put it right even though 41 mayors and 41 town-
clerks and 402 aldermen ask to be left in the same
old comfortable hole. And it only takes a thrift-
less ordinary man to get architects and surveyors
to plan a few new streets by way of a small
new patch on the big old garment, and then bor-
row the money to patch with. Lyne proposes, in
short, to fall back on a dictatorship because
Government by the people has been a failure-
through the unspeakable badness of the system
which prevented Government by the people
having a fair chance. A city dictatorship as a
temporary expedient, until the municipal institu-
tions are radically reformed, is good enough. But
unless some radical reform is e lected the choice
lies between permanent dirt or permanent dicta-
torship. And even that choice may not be always
available. Lyne is a clean dictator, but then he
won’t last for ever. And a dirty dictator might
come after him.
The Scuttle of May Day.
In the story of the Passion nothing, perhaps, dis-
gusts the modern reader more than that peculiar
fallibility which impelled Peter to deny his Great
Master. The advance guard of the Vic. Reform
and Democratic movement is the Peter of the
present occasion. The May Day movement is the
local variety of the International Socialist and
Labor m ivement. The raison d’etre of the latter
was a conviction of some middle-century thinkers
that war must stop when the raw material—the
workers—refuse to fight. A Peace plank com-
bined with a protest against militarism in
all its forms is the golden thread unifying
the otherwise glaringly - diverse May Day
Resolutions in all lands. But the Vic-
torian May Day committee knows too much
for that, and by a majority of 8 to 5 has decided
to eliminate the Peace declaration and the protest
against militarism in all its forms from the 11100
platform. There were three women on the com-
mittee, and they all voted for blood and organised
rapine. The May Day movement in Vic. may
now be considered as an ornamental kerosene tin
tied to the tail of the Dingo of Jingoism. What
was a platitude in years of Peace would have been
a wonder working Logos, an impassioned Reality,
in a time of war; but the Vic. May Day movement,
with acrobatic agility, performed the Great Re-
fusal, and the paving foremen of Hell proceed
almost sadly to paste its shuddering floor with the
good-inteutioned red blushing handbills of a
score of previous Vic. May Day celebrations.
One thing this great swallowing feat should
bring about, that is, the respectability of
the long-vetoed May Day procession. A not-im-
possible feature of its future will be Mayor
M'Eacharn in his robes of office in the van, and
picturesquely-arranged groups of Lascar seamen
in the rear. As a sort of corollary to the above,
and as evidence that the scuttle from Principle is
not an isolated one in Victoria, it may be men-
tioned that the Eight Hours’ committee has
devised a pageant for its procession representing
all the countries in the world in which eight hours’
graft obtains, and that —the Transvaal is omitted
from the group. For cowardly obeisance to cant,
for an uncbivalrous insult to a brave enemy, and
for “ un- British ” ungenerousness to a true friend
of its working population, this is hard to beat.
Some Thoughts bout the Common
Colonial.
Advertisement discovered in London Musical
Times of Ist March, 1000
Sydney Cathedral.
The Chapter of the above Cathedral are about to make
a new appointment consequent upon the death of the
late organist.
Salary, .£3OO per annum.
Excellent possibilities for a good teaching connection.
Passage paid, subject to conditions.
Application should be made to Precentor. Novello &
Co., Ld., 1 Berners-street, W., not later than March 8.
Now the Chapter in question are (to use its own
verb) supposed to be a Christian institution which
wouldn’t on any account say what isn’t true, or
beguile a fellow-creature into accepting a billet at
£2OO by promising him other advantages which
don’t exist, and which everybody here knows
don’t exist. There are no “ excellent possibilities
for a good teaching connection ” in Sydney.
There are already between 200 and 300 recognised
musical teachers in Sydney (they can be found in
the directory), and this does not include the mul-
titude of schoolmasters and mistresses who teach
music along with other arts, and the countless
obscure girl and women teachers who work up a
small, cheap connection in order to make a little
pocket-money, and spend most of the latter in
boot-leather expenses, incurred while plodding
wearily from one obtuse pupil to another. The
music-teachers of Sydney probably run to 1000 or
1500, and this intense competition has made their
lives an awful experience of poverty and drudgery.
It isn t so many months ago that a Sydney
musician about the most brilliant, and certainly
the best-advertised of the lot—had occasion to
mention publicly that his pupils numbered exactly
enjht. 1 here is no hope of any new man
working up a good teaching connection in Syd-
ney, and it is a shameful and un-christian
act for any chapter, or verse, or paragraph, or
other institution to beguile any stranger here by
baseless assurances of the kind. It is still more
unchristian -in fact it is an outrage on every
Christian principle— to invite out another com-
petitor, to make the hard life of the Sydney
music-teacher still harder. In any case the pro-
ceeding is a foolish affront to the country which
keeps the Cathedral Chapter of Sydney in bread
and cheese and finds it its daily beer. There are
musicians in Sydney already with very high quali-
fications—far higher than those of any new man
wno is likely to travel 10,000 miles for less than
Li a week —and the resolve to pass them over—it
would seem for no other reason than that they
b-Jong to the country which finds the Cathedral
Chapter its bread and cheese and other necessaries
and refreshments —is one of the insults which this
patient land puts up with. Most of these leading
musicians are English by birth but “blasted
colonials ” by adoption. If they lived in England
they would be perfectly eligible, but the fact that
they have been contaminated by recent residence
in Australia puts them out of the running. The
new English organist, when he has been m Aus-
tralia a few years* will also be a blasted colonial.
And if lie then applies for any other such billet,
people like the Cathedral Chapter of Sydney will
be amazed at the assurance of a mere, common,
low-down Australian (he will be a common, low-
down Australian by that time) in even dreaming
that he could be fit to hold a post of importance
in Australia. It is a curious frame of mind—if it
can be called mind.
Canterbury Times (Christchurch, M.L.) in its
issue of 4th April, came out with a fine blasting
indictment of the wickedness of everybody and
everything Boer. A sample blast :
The taxes on food are nothing short of extraordinary.
For instance, the consumer has to pay on the grain he
imports, including wheat, maize, flour, and all meal, an
ad valorem duty of 71 percent., and an additional 7s. (Id.
per l )01b. weight. On bacon and ham the same ad-
valorem duty is imposed, and in addition 90s. per 1001 b.
in weight is exacted. On eggs, too, there is the same ad-
valorem duty, but in addition a special duty of Od. per
dozen is demanded. Of course, there is a corresponding
tax on all fodder for animals.
And yet here are the cold, hard facts which any
one can find in that unpoetical, impartial English
publication, the Statesman’s Year Book (the
figures are for 18!)(i, being apparently the latest
available for the Transvaal):
Imports. Duties levied, per cent.
Transvaal .. £14,083,130 £1,355,486 91
Maoriland .. 7,137,320 1,818,972 25J
The short explanation is: In the Transvaal
grain, butter, cheese, cattle, sheep, bacon, ham
and coffee come in free. The Transvaal—as
shown by the above English authority—levies
lower customs duties than any Australasian
province barring N.S.W. Its rates all round are
not much more than one-third those of Maoriland.
And a slavish press curses the 9$ percent, country
for its “extraordinary” duties, because it is a
“loyal” thing to tell any lie, however mean,
about the enemy while the war lasts. Yet the
paper which tells the above yarn supports Digger
Diet’s Government in levying 25$ per cent. On
the Canterbury Times’s own showing, what sort
of a monster of iniquity and extortion is Richard
the Digger !

After some 300 unfortunate “contacts” have
been hurried off to quarantine at North Head, the
Sydney medical profession and other bodies are
petitioning the authorities to alter their definition,
if they have one, of a contact. This paper does
not set up as a medical authority, and does not
profess to decide whether the Board of Health is
right or wrong in saying that all persons brought
into close relation with a plague patient should be
quarantined. But it does boldly state that the
Board’s own methods of carrying out its own
orders appear to be quite inexplicable. Thus
when a man was stricken by plague in a Sussex-
street hotel, not only the other lodgers in the house
but the stray men drinking in the bar just off the
streets were dubbed as “ contacts ” and whisked
away as such. But many cases of plague are ad-
mitted into the metropolitan hospitals, where the
doctors on duty examine them, treat them for
some little time, perhaps, finally diagnose the
disease, telephone to the “authorities,” and go on
through the hospital wards to handle the hundreds
of sick under their immediate charge ; and if the
comparatively sturdy casual drinker at the hotel is
in danger of contagion, what about the enfeebled
Eatients in the hospital wards who are attended to
y doctors who have just left the bedside of a
plague case ?

Ex-Premier George Reid says that the present
N.S.W. Government was very wrong indeed in
not calling Parliament together to say whether it
would or would not agree to Chamberlain’s pro-
posed alteration in the Federal Constitution.
Reid is the mean, grudging kind of Opposition
leader who always says that the man who in-
herited his billet is wrong. If Lyne had called
Parliament together for so foolish a purpose, the
curse of G. Reid would probably have been the
biggest objurgation that has come home to roost
these last ten years. Fancy Lyne putting the
case to Parliament :
You have been called together to decide whether you
will accept the British Government’s proposed amend-
ments in the Federal Constitution. If you say you won’t
accept them, and the British Government insists on not
passing the Bill without them, you will have wrecked the
people’s Bill without the people’s permission. If
you say you will accept them, then you have thrown
away, without the people’s permission, any chance there
might still have been of getting the Bill in its entirety—it
is no possible use for the N.S. W. delegate to try further
to bluff the British Government into abandoning its
amendment when the N.S.W. Parliament has openly
accepted it. If you don’t say anything then what the
devil are you here for? Also what the —ahem —same
person, am I here for ? The people gave a mandate to get
the whole Bill. The obvious course is to bluff as long as
possible for the whole Bill, and if I absolutely can’t get it
to tell the British Government to make its amendments
on its own responsibility, and let the people decide
whether they will take the mutilated Bill or not. Only
an ass would strengthen Chamberlain’s hands by getting
him Parliamentary approval, or on the other hand,
deprive the people of their right to decide whether they
may not prefer the mutilated Bill rather than none at all.
I am that ass.
And George Reid, clumsily spreading his net in
sight of the fowl, actually wanted the wary Lyne to
do that.

Horrible are the things told lately to a Bendigo
jury about a back-country community in the mis-
sionary-exporting province of Victoria, Australia.
A young man of 21 was charged with polluting a
baby-girl of six. The child’s mother, an unmar-
ried woman, lived in Melbourne; she first heard
of the matter through the papers, and seemed to
wonder what all the fuss was about. The prisoner
is her cousin. The child lived with the grand-
mother up country; at the trial she shielded
the prisoner. The grandfather was too near an
idiot to understand questions in the box. The
accused’s mother is in a lunatic-asylum. i ...
child’s aunt, who was lately kicked to death, left
a couple of children; they pigged in the same
locality. One of them, 13 years old, is alleged to
have regularly cohabited with his six-year-old
cousin, and the older members of the mixed
families are accused of indulging in horrible prac-
tices with the younger ones. Not one of them
had even a rudimentary education, either secular
or religious. Three-fourths of them regarded the
police as their natural enemies, and the courts as
places only established as means of revenge.
From first to last there wasn’t the smallest trace of
horror of the offences alleged. The jury, in con-
victing on the capital charge, recommended the
prisoner to mercy on the uncommon ground that
he had had an awful bringing up ; and Judge
Hood was obviously so deeply impressed with the
same t.iought that it is extremely unlikely the
death sentence will be carried into effect.

Sydney Health-Board President, Dr. Ashburton
Thompson :
It is idle to hope that mere cleansing of the city will
be instrumental in stamping out the disease. We must
not expect to be entirely free from the plague until we
are entirely free of plague-infected rats.”
On the day of this? utterance 134 were caught and
cremated m the city. But as each pair of rats
can produce 2500 descendants in a year, 12
pairs would, at above rate of catching, keep
the trapper supplied, and add 1100 to the
rat population annually. if Dr. Thompson
is right, therefore, Sydney may accept the
plague as a permanent institution. But alleged
plague-infeoted rats ha ve been found in Adelaide,
Melbourne and Brisbane; yet humans have
so far almost escaped. Why should rats
infect people in Sydney and not in Brisbane?
Also, how can Dr. Thompson know that all the
150 cases, more or less, have been infected by
rats? He has, by this time, facts enough at his
disposal on which to base a working-hypothesis,
and if the infection is really spread by rats, be
should be able to give a pretty good guess how.
Ho ought also to have an idea why the disease
should be caught only from rats, and not, as far
as anyone knows, from men.

The subjoined extracts are from letters appearing
in Newcastle (N.S.W.) Morning Herald, of
April 23, and give ah idea how Boer-atrocitics are
manufactured. This is how Sergeant Wkarne, of
N.S.W. Mounted Rifles, describes the death of
Private Abrahams :
However, at Abraham’s Kraal, our wonderful luck,
which had been the cause of comment from the other
troops at every engagement, entirely forsook us. As the
fight was now simply a skirmish, we dismounted, leaving
the No. 3 in charge of our horses, and followed on, driving
the Boers back in great disorder, till they cleared off as
fast as they could gallop. We then returned to our own
horses, and found a knot of men gathered around a pros-
trate figure, that proved to he the body of Private Aura-
hams, of Bega. He had been shot whilst holding the
horses behind a kopje. The bullet—evidently being a
falling one—entered under his right shoulder, and must
have pierced his heart. He died almost instantly, and
without pain. He was a fine young fellow, and a good
soldier. That night we buried him near a kopje, a few
hundred yards from Abraham’s Kraal.
Captain Hilliard, late adjutant N.S.W. Fourth
Regiment, thus describes the same affair:
One of Captain Antill’s men was shot afterwards, while
passing a farm-house over which the white flag was flying.
His name was Abrahams (from Bega), and, strange to say,
the locality is known as Abraham’s Kraal.

Sydney’s Municipal Council met last week to con-
sider how best to re-organise the city’s sanitary
department. That is the department which was
responsible for there being 900 tons of filth in such
a very small area of the city that 270,000 tons of
filth seem a fair probable guess for the accumu-
lated foulness of the city as a whole. Sydney
wants its sanitary department completely re-orga-
nised, and evidently the Council knew that'its
method of re-organising anything couldn’t bear in-
spection, so it shut out the press and public, and
dealt with the question with closed doors. People
outside fancied at times that they heard objurga-
tion streaming through the keyhole, and shouts of
blast and blither pouring out through the crack
under the door, but perhaps it was mere imagina-
tion. When the door opened and the city
fathers came out like a torrent of wisdom
it was learned that they had decided to
ask the Government to appoint a city health
officer and pay him itself. That seems to be about
all. Recently Sydney’s too-eandid town clerk
remarked that the cleaned areas would be as dirty
as ever again in six months ; which was another
way of saying that the City Council, having dis-
gracefully failed to do its duty in the way of
cleansing in the past, was going to fail just as dis-
gracefully in the future. Probably that prophetic
town clerk told the whole truth —the man who
prophesies dirt is generally a true prophet. It is
an easy thing to prophesy about—almost as easy
as sunrise or dinner-time.

A. M. Simpson, of South Australia, ex-M.L.C.,
developed no very brilliant intelligence when he
wired to England that the people here couldn’t be
said to approve of the proposed Australian Court
of Appeal because they had had no opportunity to
vote on it separately—it was merely in the Com-
monwealth Bill, and they accepted the Bill as a
whole. Of course, they didn’t get a chance to
vote on the Appeal clause separately. But neither
did they get a chance to express separately their
approval or disapproval of the clauses about the
Federal Senate, or the House of Reps., or the
Governor-General, or the finances, railways, debts,
judiciary, the capital, the remedy for deadlocks,
the referendum, the franchise, or anything else.
Simpson’s logic, carried to its legitimate conclu-
sion,* proves that though the people approved of
the Bill, they may have disapproved most cor-
dially of every separate clause in it. Meanwhile
it is, presumably, because the people of S.A. cor-
dially disapprove of Simpson that he is out of
politics just now.
A Melbourne man writes to the papers in a
strain of bitter irony about tile-progressive charac-
ter of the local city fathers. He represents a new
light alleged to be much used in America, and he
offered, by way of experiment and advt., to put
up a lamp-post in any public place and under any
regulations the Council might impose, and in it to
burn a 1200-candle-light at his own expense. The
Council rejected the gratis illumination with
scorn. Still he can’t complain so very much. A
good while ago a Sydney man got permission to
put down, at his own cost, a small sample of a
new street paving, on condition he left a cash
deposit as a guarantee that the new idea would
not fail so badly as to put the Council to the cost
of renewal within a certain period. He put down
both the paving and the deposit, but though the
former has lasted far beyond the specified period
he hasn’t managed to recover the latter. So the
Melbourne man might have done worse. He
might have lost his lamp-post and been fined fo>*
obstructing the street with it.


SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
