MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s “ What Happened to Jones."
Theatre Royal “The Ladder of Life."
Bijou Rickards’ Variety Co.
Alexandra “ Hands Across the Sea.”
St. George’s Hall The Flying Jordans.
The story of “ Elizabeth, Queen of Eng-
land,” as embodied in the drama of that name at
Her Majesty’s, Sydney, can be put practically in
a sentence. She was in a dreadful rage for 44
years, and then died. All through five acts there
are hardly two consecutive minutes when the red-
headed Queen doesn’t blaze up in fury about some-
thing, or anything, or everything, or nothing, or
things in general. “Elizabeth,” taking it all
round, is stately and impressive and gorgeous, and
a trifle heavy. It is the story of a woman who
lived and raged alone all her life, with pride and
vanity and anger and all a red-headed woman’s
emotions gnawing at her heart; and who gradually
beheaded all her friends, and repented of it after-
wards, and swore she didn’t do it, and grew more
and more lonely save when her friends’ heads
came to her in her dreams, and who painted her-
self with a shaking hand in her tremulous old age,
and tried to mash her terrified courtiers with the
unsteady wink of paralysis in her last moments.
And then, after getting out of bed on the
wrong side all her life, she died in a bad
temper on the floor. She was not great in any
sense except in her courage, but she was large and
violent, and many great men lived in her reign,
and she encouraged them to go and wallop the
enemy at their own expense, and took a large per-
centage of the plunder. When there wasn’t any
plunder she said things. In particular she is sup-
posed to have been worse than unpublishable in
her language to poor, brave old Frobisher, when
he fancied he found a gold-field in the Polar
regions, and came back with a ship-load of broken
rocks which went nothing at all to the ton. Fro-
bisher was almost the first wild-cattist in English
history, and Elizabeth, as sole preference share-
holder, was grossly insulting at the meeting when
the noble old hero went into liquidation and
wound up. And, in addition to all this, Eliza-
beth drank much beer, and was only moder-
ately clean in her habits, and is reputed to
have personally spanked her maids with a firm
hand, and a long arm, and a hard heart, and the
terrible vim of a red-headed woman.
* * * *
The writer of “ Elizabeth,” though he has
glorified his heroine a good deal, represents her in
some respects very much as she was—a hard and
eminently uncertain fury, with a tendency towards
purposeless lying and shuffling and double-dealing.
Miss Nance O’Neil probably wears the English
crown a good deal more gracefully than Elizabeth
herself ever did. Her tall figure is the sort of
one that queens ought to have, and her splendid
voice is just the sort of one to behead people with.
It is a long, heavy, exhausting part, but Miss
O’Neil rages through it powerfully to the end ;
and in her occasional moments of tenderness her
Elizabeth is almost lovable. The weakest point
in a very strong version of the lady who was angry
for 44 years is in the last act—Miss O’Neil’s
perishing volcano isn’t old enough. The battered
wreck of 70 years, dying in a badly-painted con-
dition on the floor, wasn’t at all like Miss
O’Neil’s old woman—though the latter looks
much the more attractive of the two.
Kingston’s Essex is a very fine and pic-
turesque piece of work, and very true to the
dramatist’s idea. The weak, angry, unsuccessful
soldier who, at the age of 34, was still mashing a
queen of t>B, and who tried to raise an insurrection
because he was deprived of his monopoly of the
wine trade, and who called the dead-l>eats and
Domain-loafers and Yarra-bankers of London to
his standard, and then bolted home and barricaded
his door with the furniture, wasn’t at all like
Kingston’s earl, but that isn’t Kingston’s fault.
A.nd Plimmer’s James VI. looks every inch a
king, which the fat, peaceful man of nearly 40 who
succeeded Elizabeth never contrived to do.
Various other people do good service in parts of
more or less importance, and the drama is staged
with muc-h magnificence of building and apparel.
The Throne Room in Act 111. is itself wortn the
price of admission, and is cordially recommended
as one of the finest pieces of stage architecture on
record.
* * at *
“Robbery Under Arms,” which has been
revived so often that, in the ordinary course of
things, it should now be quite dead, is doing
so well under Dampier’s management at Sydney
Cri. that the date of its decease is still uncertain.
In view of future possibilities, however, that
hackneyed but reliable grief, “ East Lynne,” is in
preparation, and some Shakespearian productions
are also in view. The first Shakespearian drama
is being decided by vote of the audience, and by
last reports “Merchant of Venice” was ahead,
with “ Hamlet ” a close second. As a matter of
fact, these and “Henry V.” are the only Shake-
spearian pieces which many playgoers remember,
and they vote for them mechanically. If the
writer was a multi-millionaire he would pay for
admission for <>ooo citizens and eitizenesses who
would all vote for “ Coriolanus ” or “Titus
Andronicus,” and thereby infuse a little novelty
into the proceedings.
* #
Jones’s pretty comedy was still at the
wicket at Sydney Royal when this issue went to
press, and was batting vigorously. There is
nothing special to add to the friendly remarks
which were made last week concerning the
“Manoeuvres of Jane.” The writer merely ex-
presses his undying conviction that Jane is a very
nice girl, and the invisible loafer who hugged her
in the public-house, while she was waiting for the
missing lover who ought to have turned up and
eloped with her, was a person to be envied. It is
to be feared, however, that he was too far gone in
liquor to know what a blessed privilege had fallen
to his lot. The audience doesn’t actually see Miss
Noble being hugged by this dissipated intruder.
The curtain simply rises and reveals the newly-
hugged Jane in a terrible state of mind in a
public-house, while the wild laughter of the person
who hugged her is heard from the adjacent bar.

The “ Irishman ” will last out this week at
Sydney Lyceum, and next Saturday there is to be
a big revival of “ A Soldier and a Man.” There-
fore J. P. West, who is behind with his “ rint,”
will sit on the thatched roof with a gun for two
more nights only, and defy the agent and his
minions.
** # *
The good old Curse of Slavery drama came
off at Sydney Palace last Saturday, when the Afri-
can brethren exemplified the curse by dancing and
singing about “ Pickin’ de Cotton,” instead of
going and doing it. Meanwhile the masters and
mistresses go about saying what a bitter, bitter
curse slavery is. Their state of mind seems
reasonable, as they probably have to hire common
white trash to do the work the coons only sing
about. Billy M‘Lain’s Uncle Tom makes a mis-
take in being- too rheumaticky-looking and
wretched and broken-backed from the begin-
ning, so that the desire of everyone to
buy Uncle Tom at fabulous prices is a great
mystery. Meanwhile the Female Impersona-
tor as Eliza Harris successfully negotiates
what looks like three large lumps of camphor in
an infected area, while the orchestra plays short,
excited bursts of music to indicate that this is
dangerous. Afterwards he becomes himself and
does better. The Human Frog disguises himself
as a man during the evening, but brightens up the
performance by being as frog-like as possible. For
the rest, little Eva dies with a flop, as usual, and is
kept out of bed in order to join the Angelic Con-
tingent which arrives for Uncle Tom at 11 p.m.,
or possibly ten minutes to. Why the black race
in stageland, anyway, should be especially favored
in the way of celestial escorts is a great mystery.

At the pretty theatre with the hideous
curtain—to wit, Sydney Tivoli—Rickards’ latest
infant performer, Roy Cushin, seems likely to
equal the phenomenal Baby Powers. He is
beginning to warm up to his work, shows a beau-
tiful lack of stage fright, and while he pipes his
Jingo melody gesticulates fiercely and naturally
what time he declaims, “We’ll show them what
we mean.” If expression goes for anything
“ them ” are in for a bad time. Alf. Lawton has
resurrected that hoary chestnut “Turn over the

vocalist.

Leaf” for about the 25th time in the last six
months. If it wasn’t Alf. Lawton it was someone
else in the same company; besides which it has
been dragged several times recently from its grave
by other performers in other shows. Miss Stella
Ranger is a school-girly-looking Miss with a sweet

usually gets a well-earned encore, and whistler T.

** * *
The Ladder of Life,” which came along
to Melb. Royal on Saturday night, is a drama
wherein a sprightly housemaid grabs hold of a
red-headed Scotchman’s kilt and professes to wipe
her eye with the tartan. This is probably the
most artistic bit of bye-play in the show—there-
fore, the present chronicler gives it first mention.
The housemaid, and the auburn MacDougall
aforesaid, go far towards earning kindly con-
sideration for “The Ladder,” and they get some
assistance from a depraved baby-farmer and three
Cockney acrobats, who have enlisted in the
Gordon Heelanders with a view to storming the
heights of Dargai on gymnastic principles. Also,
the three bogus Caledonians hang head downwards
from the roof of a “smithery” and rescue Miss
Duggan from an anvil what time an eccentric
steam-hammer is making arrangements to descend
violently on her head as soon as she is shifted.
The humor of the thrilling sensation-scene counts
for righteousness in a drama that has no solid pre-
tensions to ‘be taken seriously. There are two
villains, one hero, and an apoplectic father, like-
wise a “ true sweetheart ” and a deserted child in
“The Ladder”; but none of these items in the
jerry-built narrative look like actual facts at any
point of the trouble. The hero labors under delu-
sions re his true sweetheart, and the derelict child
and the two villains are all the evening occupied
■with absurdly elaborate schemes for killing
people they could easily slaughter at any
moment. One of the villains, at the
outset, appears to be a Nihilist disguised
as an Afridi chieftain, and for a while he talks
imposingly about his desire to wipe out the
British race, whereas the other villain only wants
to destroy his cousin ; but later on the man with
the large gory ambition degenerates into a mere
civilised person, and his conversation refers
mostly to the missing will and the lost papers.
Finally both the plotters cut all connection with
the Afridis, and come to satisfactory grief in Eng-
land- Also, Miss Duggan, who is a converted
Nihilist, dies of remorse, leaving her unlawful
offspring to the care of the other girl. Robert
Inman, H. Willard, Augustus Glover, Miss
Ada Guildford and Daisy Howard figure
on the gloomy side of the drama, and are
fully equal to their awful responsibilities.
Miss Duggan, too, is equal to everything
but a foreign accent. Sundry warlike spectacles
are somehow less effective than they might be,
considering that the show is' liberally staged, and
if “ The Ladder ” is to thrive it will owe whatever
success it gets to the low comedy element, includ-
ing the three acrobatic Silvenis. Bert Bailey, as
the red-haired Heelander, is a broad, but un-
aggressive humorist, and Miss Dora Mostyn,
whom Melbourne hadn’t seen for some years,
showed herself on Saturday to be as bright and
brisk as ever in the character of MacDougall’s
young woman. And she needs all her briskness
to ward off a suspicion that some o f the little
jokes are a trifle rough.
* * * *
Business at the Alexandra suggests that
the local appetite for melodrama is declining.
“ Hands Across the Sea,” in its present unhappy
domicile, doesn’t seem quite the same thing that
Charles Warner of yore played at the Royal for
12 weeks, yet it is a more deserving spec, than the
average Aleckian horror, and the vehement pro-
testations of Alfred Woods’s innocence ought to
got more crowded encouragement. “Hands
Across the Sea ” has a tale that hangs together,
anyhow. The principal characters travel from
Devonshire to Baris on reasonable business, or
pleasure, and there the leading young man gets
lured into baccarat, and cast into prison for
murder, after which he gets away to Australia on
the same boat with most of his friends and
enemies, and all ends happily at Circular Quay,
Sydney. The escaped prisoner aboard the British
vessel is a fair excuse for hoisting the Flag when a
French captain comes to demand his body for the
guillotine. The fact that the British captain is at
liberty to stick to his passenger under the circum-
stances without any particular reference to the
Flag is not worth considering.
* * * *
Regarded as a jingo demonstration the
Flag in this instance is all right. Alfred Woods,
Miss Maud Williamson, Miss Mortyne, and pretty
Miss Lyndahl appear to more or less advantage in
“Hands Across the Sea.” Miss Lyndahl, who
was a promising member of George Rignold’s co.
the last time she came to Melbourne, will be a
better actress when she doesn’t hurry herself.
Alf Boothman, on the other hand, would be all the
better if he talked faster and didn’t wear a pink
and white complexion, and E. G. Coughlan, as
Tom Bassett, is a shocking reminder that Elton
“ created ” the part in Melbourne. The distance
dividing Elton from Coughlan is more than 12,000
miles.
* * * Hi
Other Melbourne shows stand where they
did, and St. George’s Hall has reopened with a
now show, led by the Flying Jordans. At the
Princess’s there are full houses for “ What Hap-
pened to Jones.” A surfeit of war dramas, war
pictures, and patriotic concerts had set up a cheer-
ful demand for farcical comedy, and Jones was the
man who came at the critical moment to supply
the needful. Jones is the harmless, necessary
liver pill worth two guineas a box. Also, “ An
Empty Stocking,” the little bit of sentiment which
precedes Charles Arnold’s joke, is a timely reflec-
tion on The Absent-Minded Beggar, and the
double bill looks likely to continue for two or
three weeks to come. At the Bijou, the multi-
farious visage of Amann, the keen little voice of
Irene Fletcher, and that everlasting joy, the
marionette, who gets bashed by bricks and jam-
tins and still swears that the missiles never
touched him, are principal attractions. Also,
Charles Seel has charms for a section of the mul-
titude ; but Seel, although an undoubted artist
within his limits, is not an all-round success.
* * # *
Williamson has revived his opera co. in
Adelaide, “ Pinafore ” and “ The Pirates ” being
staged. The holy city is immensely proud at
being chosen for the initial performances, although,
as a matter of fact, it is but a preliminary canter,
Sydney being selected for the first new work. All
the same, Adelaide has a badly-swollen head, and
the W.C.T. U. is almost unpopular just now.
* * * *
Rickards is not able to quite carry out his
original idea of having ’freshments served at the
back of the circles in tue new Melbourne Opera
House. The “authorities” have decided not to
permit any more theatre lessees to run pubs, under
their own roofs, so the little difficulty at the duly-
licensed Opera House will be got over by placing a
small winter-garden between the auditorium and
the bars. The way to the whisky will be under
cover, of course, and the “ garden ” should prove a
poetic attraction for all who feel disposed to go
and see a man in the thirsty summer-time.
** * *
“Constance Clyde”: Re “Lady Dolly,”
now defunct in spite of the occult professor made
up as an emerald green grasshopper and the wig
of the Bohemian Duke—it looked like a retriever
puppy, and was barely covered by an up-to-date
mining-expert hat, which, in Bohemia, the nobility
apparently never take off even in the ball-room —
one is in grave doubts as to whether the repre-
sentation was meant to be a high-class concert or
an opera. As a rule, it was opera ; but occasion-
ally it slid into concert, as when the Duke
sang his passionate love-ditty to the audience,
ignoring the object of his affections, and the
gallant, gay lieutenant handed the heroine up the
stage and solemnly introduced her to the audience
whenever she had aught to sing. More doubts:
What finally became of the hypnotic villain when
he had shed his grasshopper coat and attired his
recreant limbs in a tweed garb which gave him the
appearance of a respectable retired burglar ? His
fate is a matter of hazy conjecture. Presumably
he fled to the nearest lunatic asylum, or the pond,
or the infected area.
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
In Victoria the Deddick private-railway scheme
still keeps up a vigorous pretence of being real. The co.
makes proposals, and the Govt, seriously discusses them,
and neither party winks at the other to anv needless ex-
tent. Yet what is the Deddick Joke ? It Is understood
that the co. wants to make a line from a pleasant little
weather-beaten village on the shores of a shallow lake,
through a very moderately-fertile region where there is
about half an inhabitant to 2Jsq. miles, and ending at or
about a mountain, where a caretaker is supposed to be
looking after an alleged mine that has produced nothing
or thereabouts. Has Victoria run against a crowd of
unfathomable schemers, or has it merely struck some
amiable cranks with money ?

The Bank of Adelaide sends out another pros-
perous-looking report for the year which closed on 26th
March, 1900. The record of this institution for the last
nine years has been as follows :

The Bank of Adelaide has thus made a good deal of pro-
gress in the past nine years, though whether it was advis-
able to pay away in dividends all the profits barring
£35,017 is rather doubtful. It is a margin, certainly, but
it isn’t a very large one. The fact that the profits have
suddenly increased by £5387, making them equivalent to
£6 16s. per cent, on the shareholders’ funds (capital
£400,000 and reserve £165,000) is certainly gratifying, but
the promptitude with which £4OOO of this has been dis-
tributed in extra dividend (or rather, bonus) to share-
holders isn’t nearly so gratifying.
Still, the Bank of Adelaide looks a very sound
little concern. It has £400,000 paid-up capital, and its
reserve fund is now raised to £170,000. During the year
there has been an all-round increase in business, especially
in deposits, which have risen from £1,934,557 to £2,185,435.
And the reserve of cash and liquid securities is remark-
ably large. Even leaving out the item “ Money at short
cali, £516,000,” which may or may not be absolutely
liquid according to the shortness of the call or (at a crisis)
the promptitude of the person called upon, absolutely
liquid securities are £761,817, or over 33 per cent, of all
liabilities to the public. Including the “ short call ” item,
they are equal to 66 per cent. The holding of State
securities alone (£337,340) is a very massive second line of
defence, or Boer entrenchment, for a small institution.

On various occasions The Bulletin has sug-
gested that the National Bank of Australasia would have
to write off capital to provide for losses as yet unacknow-
ledged. It was the only paper which did so (it was also
the only one which foretold that the Commercial Bank of
Tasmania would require to make large provision for
losses). On 29th October, 1893, this paper said : “It is
difficult to form an opinion as to whether the small earn-
ings are only owing to the depression or whether the
National has still some depreciation to write off.” On
6th May, 1899, it wrote : “ The net earnings are only
equal to £1 4s. 4d. per cent, per annum on the share-
holders’ funds . . . which is so miserably small as to
strongly suggest . . . that there is need of a heavy writ-
ing down.” On 4th November, 1899, it said: “The
natural inference is that about £701,000 of the capital
wants to be written off as lost.” The National Bank now
proposes to write off £476,976 of capital, and £63,009 of
accumulated profits (£544,976 in all), which does some-
thing towards verifying these prophecies.

The present National Bank, Limited, when it took over
the business of the old National Bank which disappeared
in 1893, took over along with it £680,488 of thejfld Bank s
reserves. It has earned, as shown above, £252,746. It has
received £50,434 on account of forfeited shares, and it
now proposes to write off £476,976 of capital (being £u
per snare on the ordinary shares), which (along with
£68,000 of accumulated profits) is to go towards providing
for more losses. Therefore, what with earnings, old
reserves, and forfeited and written-off capital, the direc-
tors have had £1,460,644 to dispose of in seven years. It
has been disposed of thus :
Reconstruction expenses .. .. .. £14,360
Dividends .. 155,617
Reserve Fund 65,000
Used to provide for losses, 1893-’O5 .. 680,458
„ „ ~ 1900 .. 644,976
Balance on hand „ .. .. 10,203
£1,460,644
When the proposed arrangement is carried out
the paid-up capital will be £1,498,220, and the profits,
though the best since 1894, only represent £2 17s. per
cent per annum on the shareholders’ funds (the reduced
capital and reserve fund). Probably there will be a
further marked increase in profits next half-year, for all
the reconstructed deposits have now been cleared off, and
as the National Bank paid 4j per cent, on them to the last
they were a heavy load, but on present appearances it
certainly looks as if the amount written off was none too
large, if it is even quite large enough. At all events
there is nothing very brilliant actually in Bight for
the ordinary shareholders as yet. It would take
about £22,500 to pay them a dividend at even 2.j per
cent, per annum after paying the preference div. of
5 per cent., and the earnings haven’t quite reached that
yet. Still, there is a large improvement in deposits, note
circulation, cash reserves—in fact, in every department of
the business. And the prospects are vastly better than
they have been at any previous time since September,
1894—the date when the patient, ordinary shareholder
last heard of something to his advantage.
* * * *
The South Australian Brewing Co. (Adelaide)
issues its (12th) annual report for year ended 28th Feb.,
1900. The net profit, after writing £642 off leaseholds
and allowing £1441 for renewals of plant, &c., is £17,709.
This is equal to £7 Is. Bd. per cent, per annum on share-
holders’ funds (capital £250,000). A dividend of 5 per
cent, absorbs 12,500, and the other £5209, added to pre-
vious balance, leaves £16,115 at credit of profit and loss.
This is the only thing resembling a reserve that the S.A.'
Brewing Co. possesses. Until quite recently 7 percent,
dividends were paid, and this reckless policy of dividing
practically all the earnings prevented the concern making
much progress. The co. would be well-advised to
put £15,000 of its accumulations to a reserve forthwith.
It is burdened with a debt of £200,000 on debentures at 5
per cent., which is rather a lofty rate of interest, and with
a view to the extinction of this liability the dividends
should be rigidly kept down to the present level. When
bad times come, as they do occasionally, a big debenture
debt generally pulls the debtor company down, for the
interest goes on just the same in good times or bad. It
was on this point that the great Melbourne breweries
came to grief when bad days arrived and Victoria re-
trenched its beer.

* * * *
The Twentieth Century Mutual Tontine Invest-
ment Fund of Victoria is the latest thing in the way of
the cheerful and interesting tontine gamble. It is built
on the usual principles, which look about as safe as any-
one could ask for. Each member insures his life with
either the A.M.P. Society or Citizens’ Life Assurance Co.,
and transfers his policy to the trustees of the Fund.
When any member dies the Fund collects the amount of
the policy, repays to the heirs of the deceased all the
money he paid in premiums and 3 per cent, compound
interest thereon, and keeps the balance. Therefore the
defunct hasn’t lost anything. When the tontine period of 20
years expires, the trustees transfer back to each surviving
member his own policy, and divide among survivors, in
proportion to the amount of their premium payments, the
profits it made on the members who didn’t survive, and
the interest realised on these profits. The Equity Trustees,
Executors and Agency Co. holds the policies and the cash,
and is responsible. The chief danger seems to be that on a
long gamble (the division takes place in 20 years) so many
people may get tired and let their policies lapse that there
may not be enough left in at the finish to make it inter-
esting. Another minor danger is that the members may
not prove to be sufficiently diseased, and may not die fast
enough to provide very large profits for the survivors.
On the other hand, an epidemic might break out among
them, and the last man might become rich beyond the
dreams of avarice—to say nothing of the fact that he
might secretly murder a lot of his fellow-members, and
thus declare a big dividend for himself. A tontine is a
concern which makes one take an interest in another
man’s misfortunes, and leads him to call twice a day and
inquire if his sick fellow-member is feeling any worse.
• • * *
The anger of Darius Hunt. Some curious recol-
lections or uie past are awakened by the news that one
Darius Hunt, of Toowoomba, Queensland, is proceeding
for damages against P. H. Hart, M.L.C., poor, old,
broken-down ex-Premier Morehead, and A. B. Webster,
directors of the Queensland National Bank, on the
ground, as alleged, of “ fraudulently signing and issuing
false reports and balance-sheets of affairs of the Queens-
land National Bank ; and for misrepresentation of affairs
of the said bank ” —whereby Darius was induced to buy
shares. And when one considers the awful discrepancy
between some of the balance-sheets and some of the
things that came to light very soon afterwards, the plain-
tiff’s anger seems not wholly unreasonable.

The Riverina Frozen Meat Co., a Melbourne
enterprise which started sheep-freezing in the Riverina
(N.S.W.) just when the drought was at its worst, reports
five years’ progress as follows :

No dividends have been paid, so the company is £2522 to
the good. The paid-up capital is only £19,581, and as the
co. has borrowed £15,000 on debentures at 5 per cent,
and has a bank overdraft of £7515, it seems that two-

* * * *
At the recent examination into the bankruptcy
of a leading Sydney butchering concern, evidence was
given that a local usurer had for 3 years past been lending
money to the business at 6 per cent, per month —sixty
per cent per annum 1
* • • *
The Queensland sugar-yield for 1898 was 103,734
tons : the yield for 1899 was 123,289 tons—a decrease of
40,445 tons. Decrease of area crushed —2956 acres. Yield
per acre for last year, 1'65 tons the yield per acre for ’9B,
1‘99 tons. Average number of tons per acre for ’99, 14 'Bl
tons, against 13'72 tons for '98.. There was an increase in
cane area at Cairns, Port Douglas, Ingham, Mourilyan, Chil-
ders, and Bowen ; a decrease at Mackay, Bunduberg, Ayr,
and Ingham. In view of the evidently-decreasing fertility
of the fields, it is interesting to note that of 20,000 tons
of manure produced at Queensland bone-mills and meat-
works, nearly 10,000 tons were exported: 6177 tons of
guano also being sent abroad. 13 mills and one tramway
received advances under the Sugar Works Guarantee Act,
making a total of £541,204 expended by the State. A few
Q. planters have turned their attention to irrigation and
rotation, plus manuring, with notable results.

Yet again has the public been given proof of
how soft it is for one to entrust a single individual with
one’s money and affairs instead of putting them into the
keeping of a responsible trustee co. The present deplor-
able lesson—for several trustful ladies of slender means
are concerned—involves an amount variously stated at
from £lO,OOO upwards.

Bank stocks still ascend. Australasias are now
quoted at £7O, B.N.S.W. at £42 10s., Union at £37 10s.
Rumor has it that the B.N.S.W. dividend, to be
announced this month, will be increased by 1 per cent.
* * * *
A Wostralian co. has lately been the means of
giving bankers a steadier. A former sec. of the co. was
authorised to endorse cheques for the co. He did, and
one day after doing it also paid the cheque (it was for
nearly £600) into his own account. Judge Kennedy, in
the Queen’s Bench Division, said that it was in the
bankers’ favor that the secretary had been authorised to
endorse cheques; but seeing that the cheque had been
drawn in favor of the co., and that the bankers could
easily have ascertained whether anybody else had been
authorised to be credited with it, he thought that the
bank had not been reasonably cautious. So the mining
co. got a verdict against the bankers.
* * * •
Tv(X)-written copies of a most amazing confession
alleged to have been made and sworn to some years ago
by a well-known mining-man are again extensively in
circulation. The alleged confession in question was, it
would appear, actually printed long ago in an Australian
newspaper of some standing. The Bulletin has
recently received several copies of this alleged confession,
apparently sent from different quarters. The mining-man
in question can hardly be ignorant of the circulation of
this shocking indictment of his character, and he owes it,
not only to himself but to the lucrative and responsible
Sost he' now holds (and which he could never have obtained
ad there been a scintilla of truth in the allegations
against him) to make an example of those who are passing
round what are prima-facie a series of the most monstrous
and circumstantially-set-out libels ever aimed at any man
in a high position of trust.
* * * *
There is talk again at Broken Hill of re-organ-
ising the Miners’ Association —the formidable A.M.A. of
other days—oonfining membership to trained miners. At
present it consists largely, if not mainly, of those who are
not miners; many of them, with the most to say, are
not even mine workmen. The difficulty is to classify
mine workmen, because, though mining calls for skill,
there is unfortunately no apprentice system.
♦ * * *
The directors of the B.H. South have received
two offers for the Co. ’s blocks 5 and 6, supposed to be on
the main lode, and 87 and 88, which are on the eastern
flank. One syndicate offered 100,000 paid £1 shares in a
290,000 co., with £lO,OOO to go to the credit of the new
co. ; the other offered the same, but sprang the amount
of cash to £12,500. The directors will recommend the
shareholders to accept the latter. But even £12,500 is not
enough to start on such a big prospecting venture with.
Block 5 was formally held by a separate co., with a capital
of £50,000 ; it spent its money on thoroughly legitimate
prospecting, and then failed. It put down a shaft over
700 ft., and threw out long crosscuts at various levels,
especially at 500 ft. and 600 ft. There were “indica-
tions ” at a hundred points; and at that the develop-
ments stopped. From 240 ft. right down to almost the
bottom workings there were lode formations, one of
them nearly 15ft. wide ; but nowhere was a compact ore-
body met—just stringers of mundief and bands of calcite,
and nodules of galena, and sulphides much mixed with
refractory blende. Yet only one block—No. 6—lies be-
tween this and the South (block 7 and 8), where good
divs. are being made ; and next to that is the phenomenal
Central (Sulphide Corporation). What becomes of the
great lode north of the North and south of the South is as
yet unsettled, though over £200,000 has been frittered
away by nearly 80 different co’s. in trying to solve the
problem. The new co. will have to tackle the same old
riddle ; and though the position of its blocks is the very
best for the purpose, it ought to have more than £12,500
to face what may possibly De a very long and discouraging
search. A peculiarity is that the country and formation
about these blocks underlie east, while, generally, the
Broken Hill underlay is west; hence, no doubt, the de-
sire for blocks 87 and 88.

The King Conrad silver mine, adjoining
Howell’s Bora Creek (N.S.W.), has been sold for £lO,OOO
cash and 50,000 fully paid £1 shares. Great accounts are
given of both these properties.
" * * * *
Something quite unusual has happened at
Broken Hill—a coroner’s jury has returned a verdict that
an accident would probably not have happened if the co.
had carried out some trifling alterations to the winding-
engine as suggested by the Govt, inspector. The inquiry
was the most searching which has followed a Broken Hill
mining accident for many a day—partly on account of
recently-printed remarks on the rushing of such in-
quests ; partly on account of the victim being a member
of the Miners’ Association, thus leaving somebody able to
press for a full inquiry and interested in doing so. The
B.H. North is the company concerned.
• • * •
The good old Paddy Lackey mine, N.S.W.,
steadily worked for these 20 years, lately crushed 231 tons
dirt for 3330 z. gold—exclusive of cyanide yield. This is
the highest-grade bulk crushing the mine has yet turned
out. The Paddy L. is owned by a party of strong Salva-
tionists, who, a year or two back, had it under offer to
John Bull, cheap, but J.B. hadn’t the sense to close. He
could by now have got back all his money, and more, if
he had chosen to work.
*.» * *
Application has been granted for suspension of
all labor on the eight leases of the Brokers’ Joy, i.e. ; the
Lake George Mines. Apologies and excuses for failure
are now exhausted. Why don’t the directors say at once
that the ore is too poor to work and too hard to treat even
if it were not so poor? Parcels of scrip are still held in
strong hands, and though the show is comatose for the
present, pending results of the prospective cyanide ex-
periment, gambling will put shares up if history repeats
itself.
« • » *
The most productive N.S.W. gold-mine of to-
day is probably Neeld’s, at Wyalong ; but the gold is sold
at Melbourne, and little is heard about it. By the way,
it is stated that the Cobar Gold Mines Co. is now turning
out something like 2000 oz. monthly ; not counting the
20,000 tons of valuable slimes which it must have already
stacked.
• * * •
Peak Hill Proprietary (N.S.W.) 19th call of Is.
3d. has been announced. The six months have expired
within which time it was said, and supposed, the new
manager (late N.S.W. Govt. Metallurgist) would make co.
pay—said manager being paid himself only on condition
that he would make the mine payable within a reasonable
time ; reports from the mine state that the furnace is
working satisfactorily, and that the outlook is promising.
This co. is somewhat more popular than most of the
N.S.W. new ventures, and the tone of the scrip
market is good. By the way, should the Proprie-
tary be made into a big thing—it must be either a very
big thing or nothing at all—by the management, what a
lovely chestnut' they will pull out of the lire for the
Wythes and Mooney Co. next door, which seems to have
a continuation of the best of the Proprietary’s ore !
« • * *
Westralia begins to pile up its instances of boles
and shafts, abandoned in the champagne days, yielding a
lot more than tucker in the later days of long beers and
shandy. The Bobby Dazzler—good old name ! —at Laver-
ton was thrown up a couple of years ago ; but lately two
Eersons named McDougall and Buntine put in a shot,
roke into gold-stone, pegged the claim, put in another
shot, and then took out about 40oz. as the result of the
two charges. (
Happy Westralia if it can solve its sulphide
problem inside two years at most, while Broken Hill has
been struggling with its little load for four times that
long, and is still struggling ! Dr. Diehl’s bromo-cyanide
is loudly trumpeted ; but the tests so far have not been
independent enough to be quite convincing. As for the
Lake View’s adoption of the process, the new general
manager says that it would be injudicious to give cbsts at
present, because in the experiments they have varied so
much. Which also is not very convincing. And, any-
how, though that manager has adopted the process there
isn’t much in his management of either the B.H. South
or the Wentworth mines to inspire perfect confidence in
his judgment. The danger in connection with sulphide
processes is that many people are satisfied with a clean ex-
traction, overlooking the supreme importance of the cost
of extraction. On Broken Hill sulphides the imported
Dr. Schnabel experimented successfully, so far as extrac-
tion went, with over 30 different processes ; but he could
not find one that promised commercial success.
• * * *
During February, of 63,9190 z. gold won. at Kal-
goorlie only 54920 z. was from sulphides.
It is pretty certain that Chairman Landau, of
the Lake View Consols and others, was quite prepared to
cut wages as well as sack managers—one or the other,
sometimes both, is invariably done when the slump
comes. But the Mine Managers’ Association put its foot
down ; Landau had made the mistake of antagonising
that crowd. Still, reductions were tried where possible.
Callahan paid the drivers of the first motion winding-
engine 10s. a week above union rates. When he went the
10s! was knocked off, the men struck “ on their own,”
and others were put on. But when a new hand let a cage
drop to the 500 ft. level, pitching a miner into the cistern,
the management reckoned that it would be cheaper, after
all, to go back to the old scale.
• • • «
The Phillips River field, of which good reports
have been wired East, is marked on some maps as Ravens-
thorpe ; it is close alongside the Albany-Eucla telegraph-
line, and about 35 miles from Mary Ann Harbor. The
idea of Morgans, M.P., who has the thing in hand—it is,
from all accounts, wholly a reefing field—is to ship most
of the ore at this inlet to South Australian works.
* * * *
Southern Cross, where the Fraser’s group of
mines is, continues to look up. The custom cyanide
works make solid little profits ; Fraser’s is building a
cyanide plant of its own, which should be ready by the
beginning of June ; and the Fraser’s South is now adding
to its battery.
* « « *
The Ivanhoe’s trouble is that it doesn’t know
where to stack its tailings—and these days tailings don’t
spell mullock as they did. That was the beginning of
the rumor that the Ivanhoe and the Horseshoe (which
has plenty surface space) were going to amalgamate.
The Broken Hill mines were faced by just the same
trouble once ;but then, luckily for them, the co’s. working
the ground on either side got tired of prospecting for the
underlay or for casual spurs, and either abandoned the
leases or transferred them for a trifle.
* * * *

was 6 tons 19cwt. avoirdupois. But the mine with the
6 tons 19cwt. still inside it is the better-off.

* * * •
Proof of an old Bulletin contention, that the
Govt, mining inspector is often more interested in show-
ing that the victim of a mining accident was himself to
blame than in getting at the facts, was furnished by a
recent Westralian case. A miner, working for the Pad-
dington Consols Co., was injured ; it was shown that the
fall of ground was due to shortage of timber. As soon as
the miner reached the hospital the Govt, inspector saw
him and asked him how the accident happened ; and the
man replied that it was his own fault. The doctor who
was present said, “ I have spoken several times to the
inspector about my evidence. He has asked me several
times to recall the statement Craven (the injured miner)
made in the hospital about the accident being his own
fault.” Said Judge Hensman in charging the jury: “I
think the gentleman (the inspector) would have been much
better employed in making an examination of the mines,
which it was his duty to do, than in leading the plaintiff
to make a statement of that kind.” The battered miner
got £5O0 —or, at least, a verdict for that amount.
It transpired in evidence in a Westralian court
the other day, in a case arising out of a mining accident,
that the Govt, mining inspector for that district—a most
important one—had no credentials as a mining expert,
but had served his time as a joiner and wood-turner.
The manager of the Hampden Copper Mines,
Cloncurry, reports having broken and stacked on the
mine, for the four weeks ended 6th April, 53 tons fit sts
ore, averaging 40 per cent, copper per ton ; 147 tons
seconds, averaging 13 per cent. ; and 23 tons thirds,
averaging 61 per cent.; gross copper value, £3175.

I IThere has been much shuffling of cards and
folding of blankets on account of the fixing of the site of
Chillagoe township. The Govt, waited till the co. had
decided where to build its smelters ; then it bit out 1056
acres from Chillagoe run, and everybody who had backed
some other township proceeded to get a leg-in. But it’s
odds the first township won’t be the permanent centre.
Kalgoorlie, Broken Hill, the business part of Bendigo, and
heaps more were after-thoughts.

thereahouts, now stands at less than half that. Mine
closed down for the present, to the great disappointment
of Melb. scrippers who hold most of the stock in this
well-boomed venture. The report of the Chairman of
Directors, who is going to the field, will be anxiously
awaited. Great things were predicted of this venture.

Merchants, 506 little Collins-street, Melbourne, report
that the Luhrig system of concentration is gradually es-
tablishing itself in the principal mills of Australia. The
latest Luhrig vanner has a large capacity, and owing to
important improvements the life of the belt, &c., is greatly
increased. Vanners and spare parts are stocked in Melb.
and prompt delivery is thus assured. A small testing
plant is available near Melbourne. Upon receipt of
necessary data, estimates for complete plants, including
batteries, rolls, trommels, jigs, classifiers, vanners, &c.,
will be proceeded with by competent engineers.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, and all sortsof iron-work.*
* * * *
The Austral Otis Engineering Co., of South
Melbourne, have delivered the first set of electrically-
driven triple double-acting plunger pumps for the Junc-
tion Deep Leads. These pumps are a line sample of
colonial manufacturing skill. The total weight of the
pump is close on 100 tons. All the gear wheels, which are
Bft. diam. and 12in. face, have machine-cut teeth. The
Otis Co. had to design and build a special machine to cut
these wheels. Two other sets of these pumps have to be
built to go down the shafts on adjoining mines. They will
all be operated by a central power plant, now nearing
completion.*

The exemption swindle ! A small N.Q. gold
show, lately sold—machinery, battery and all—for £6OO,
applied for and secured an extension of freedom from
labor conditions, the reason assigned being vaguely and
laconically : the war. It is not stated whether President
Steyn is a shareholder and forgot, in the pressure of
other business, to pay up calls, or perhaps Gen. Cronje,
as chairman of directors, was unavoidably detained, and
a qiufrum thus unobtainable. Locally, the latter theory is
favored ; and the caretaker is collecting subscriptions to
cable to Her Majesty an appeal for the liberation of the
Lion of the Veldt.
• * * *
Judging from recent appointments of managers
for Chillagoe shows some people are developing the
notion that Tas. West Coast is a copper-field because Mt.
Lyell is located thereon. Writer has seen most of the
aileged copper shows in Tas. and thinks that many have
been so labelled out of pure sarcasm. Lyell district cer-
tainly contains copper shows, but the shows north of that
field are by no means Lyell.

N.S.W. Minister of Mines, W. H. Wood,
who has jumped in one day from lieutenant to
lieutenant-colonel, easily beats Jawbone Neild in
the matter of a rapid ascent of the military ladder
of fame. The calm disregard of the intervening
ranics of captain, major, &c\, exhibited in the case
of Air. Wood, is absolutely sublime. But if an
Australian Premier is not above the red-tape of a
military office, who is? Lyne, jun., the Premier’s
heir-apparent, has just been appointed to a lieu-
tenancy in the newly-formed Lancer squadron
(out of, it is said, several scores of applicants), and
the Lancer’s commandant, Colonel Burns, will no
doubt wake up some morning to find several field-
marshals in his regiment. Still, if the ungodly
sneer at the Lyne crowd for annexing these gaudy
military billets, it should be noted that there is no
money-grubbing in it. By the time Colonel Wood
and Lieutenant Lyne are fixed up to kill in their
new uniforms, &c., it will probably cost them £lOO
apiece.
FI NANCE.
FATHER O'LLABA : “ What’s the trouble between
you an’ Murphy?”
PATRICK BRADLEY : “It was about a loan, yer
riverence. I lent Murphy 55., an’ I was that
mad with myself f’r doin’ it, knowin’ I'd niver
see the money back, that I spent 6s. 6d. in
dhrink, an’ teas fined ss. f’r Lein’ disorderly.
An’ when Murphy paid me back the ss. I icas
that glad to see it ag’in that I spent 7s. 6d. in
dhrink, an’ was fined is. for bein’ disorderly.
An’ whin Murphy asked me to lend him another
5s. I called him, a thafe, an’ said I'd lost more
than a pound by him already. Th in ice had a
foight an’ ss. dhropped out of me pocket whin
I was on the ground an’ Murphy jumpin’ on
me. An’ whin I got up I was fined 58. for bein’
disorderly. I’ve lost too much by Murphy.”
The Vie. military authorities have cer-
tainly done their best to disgust any aspirants for
war-glory. The Bushmen’s corps was sent under
canvas in the midst of the most drenching rains,
where it lived for days in its wet clothes, and
slept in wet blankets on the sodden ground in
dripping tents. The cooking, such as it was, had
to be done in the open air, and the scanty rations,
less than a mutton chop per man for a meal,
would have provoked mutiny amongst a far more
disciplined body of men. Even when leave of
absence was grudgingly allowed, the unhappy
bushmen had to pay the full fare, 4s. Gd. return to
Melbourne and the camp. And to add insult to
injury, the Melb. citizens are now benevolently
raising a fund to supply the men with tobacco on
their sea voyage—though tobacco is only a shilling
a pound on board ship. If S. Africa is a worse
country for soldiering than Victoria, may Heaven
have mercy on the Bushmen’s Corps !
* • * •
So far, South Australia has been the most
fortunate of all the provinces that have sent sol-
diers to the Transvaal. Three men of the first
Contingent are dead, two by bullets and one by
typhoid, but no others have been injured, and one
man of the second Contingent has died of pneu-
monia. The Bushmen’s Corps is intact, but one
of the Imperial Corps, which has on by just left the
province, died while in camp at Adelaide. That
makes three fatalities by illness and only two by
war.
« » » *
“Jack” : While the departing Bushies
were proceshing through Sydney, the other day,
many of the horses fell owing to the slippery state
of the blocks; and near the Sydney Arcade one
man came down pretty hard and rolled under the
horses of his “ next-four.” As he jumped up and
made for his moke, a fair thing in spectacles sud-
denly grabbed him round the neck, pinned a
badge to his coat, and kissed him a hard one on
the cheek I was two floors up, yet the “smack”
sounded like a box in the ear.
* * * *
More war items ! An old lady of a N.S. W.
country town was peacefully dozing in her chair
t’other day when an army of schoolboys rushed
in, seized her, chair and all, and finally deposited
her on the other side of the road. " On inter-
rogation she discovered that they were the
British army, while she represented the inhabi-
tants of Ladysmith who had been relieved. They
also declared their intention of thus relieving her
every day till similar news came of some other
place in Africa, when they would forthwith pro-
ceed to Mrs. Jones’s over the way and relieve her.
The old lady ungratefully complained to the
schoolmaster, who forthwith caned the British
army; but as these latter continued their daily
onset she wisely drew up a treaty, wherein she
consented to be relieved every day at half-past
three if the army, in pursuing the enemy, would
cease to smasli windows, but confine their shelling
operations to the hen-house, having first escorted
the inhabitants thereof to some less vulnerable
kopje. So far the nations have not had to inter-
fere.
• * * •
Transvaal and Orange Free State used
postage-stamps are, in anticipation of the early
demise of the Republics, being rapidly snapped up
by those cranks who regard stamps with more
affection than they do their wives.
* * * *
The report that Justice Dash wood in the
middle of a murder-trial at Port Darwin, hearing
of Lord Roberts’ first victory over the Boers, pro-
posed that the court should sing “Ruleßritannia ”
is not, although bizarre to the extreme, wholly
without a precedent. In Ireland, in 1843, O’Con-
nell, passing along a Dublin road, encountered a
funeral procession. The corpse was being carried
on the shoulders of four men in the front, and
weeping and lamentation, as was the custom,
engaged the crowd that followed. But the sight
of O’Connell changed the scene. The coffin was
deposited on the side of the road and all took off
hats, waved handkerchiefs, and gave three cheers
for the .great Dan. Then they picked up the
coffin, started to weep anew, and marched on.
* * *
Talk about the heroism of the battlefield—
—there is a greater that never gets a line in the
papers : the heroism of men and women who are
not living and moving in a state of exaltation !
Here is a sample. A man, a member of a very
respectable family standing high in a certaiii
straiglit-laced religious sect, had domestic troubles
and got right down in the gutter, winding up by
taking a colored woman as a paramour and finally,
in the madness of drink and jealousy, shooting
and killing this woman’s sister. The man had, in
fact, got down just about as low as he could ; and
yet, when he was put on his trial for murder, his
sister, instead of disowning him (indeed there was
nothing to connect them), put all the pride of her
excellent social position in her pocket, travelled
several thousand miles to be by his side, supported
him while he was in the dock, and, when sentence
of death was pronounced, promoted a petition to
save him from the gallows.
Publication of letters received from Briti di
prisoners at Pretoria is farcical, anyhow. One of
them has written to his brother (Constable Barnes
of Wentworth) stating that the men are “fairly
well treated ” ; but he couldn’t have said less than
that with any chance of getting the letter passed
by the Boer “censor.” The Jameson Raid
prisoners were not allowed to send unpleasant
news of their treatment, and for that matter they
had little to complain of “ except that their food
was on one occasion served to them in troughs.” It
was hash-day with the Boer commissariat dept.,
probably. The raiders passed most of their time
playing marbles for money, and their gambling
shocked the thrifty burghers who had them in
charge.

* * * *
Boiling with indignation, a good Brisbane
housewife recently hunted up the municipal
authorities, who have lately been conducting some
sort of a hygienic campaign, and complained that
a well-known city merchant, whose property
adjoined her own, kept his drains in so scandalous
a condition that a most evil odor continually arose
therefrom. A nuisance inspector was forthwith
dispatched to investigate. The odor was immedi-
ately (under the lady’s direction) detected and
located, and found to arise from a plentiful supply
of carbolic acid with which aforesaid drains had
been duly drenched. The gudewife had never
“smelt such a stink in her life,” she guilelessly
confessed.
* * * *
Brisbaneites are making heroic efforts to
dodge the plague. Over the river the local Council
hawks filthy garbage through the main streets and
dumps it down in the only public park, covered by
a film of earth that the first tropical shower will
wash off. The state of the others may be im-
agined when this place is seriously alluded to as
“the model Borough.”
FASHION NOTE.
TOOTSIE : “Hi! Billy, d’yer ’ear Jessie Wilkin’s
got ther boobonic?”
BILLY: “Gam, ’as she! This ’ere try in’ to be
fashionerble ’ill be the death of ’er!”
Some time ago Dr. Howard, of Melb.
Health Department, offered to go to India at his
own expense to study plague, but permission was
refused. All through the piece, such of the Aus-
tralian authorities as were approached in the
matter have behaved in a most uncivilised and
ignorant fashion with respect to plague contin-
gencies. It will not be soon forgotten by the
medical profession how the Victorian Government
sent its pet detective in hot haste to run down a
doctor who, in view of the world-spread of the
disease, had brought with him from abroad
bubonic bacilli to make serum ; and the authori-
ties, when they seized and burnt the doctor’s
tubes, were so proud of the feat they had per-
formed “ in the interests of public safety,” that
they actually cackled about it for weeks.
* * • *
Williamstown (Melb.) council pays for
dead rats lid. per head, and Footscray, on the
same line of railway, 2d. Result: twice a-week
a crowd of small boys take half-tickets from Wil-
liamstown to ’Scray and travel the five miles by
rail laden with bagfuls of defunct and possibly
bubonic-smitten rats, which they dump gaily into
the hat-rack or flop carelessly down on the newly-
upholstered seats. Yet, when the plague does
reach Melb., and possibly sweeps off half the
population, the other half will wonder how on
earth the thing got a start ! Where a city is
divided into a dozen municipalities, when it should
be only one, the 12 mayors and the twelve councils
can never agree about anything— not even the
price of rats. Union would save money in many
ways —even in rat-bonus.

Recently offered for sale in public market,
of Melbourne, a cow which, when condemned and
seized by an inspector, was found to have her
udder a, mere mass of tubercle. Yet the milk
from this horror had probably been in use right
up to date. And the milk from one such cow
would contaminate with the seeds of tubercule
the milk from a hundred ora thousand ot'c.r cows
with which it might be mixed at a dairy farm or
milk-depot.
* * • *
A one-time Vic. M.P. was waylaid after a
sitting when a woman’s-rights’ discussion was on
by a gaunt and bespectacled enthusiast from the
gallery, who wished to argue him
out of several fallacious beliefs to
which he had given utterance in
his speech against the emancipation
of the sex. The bony politician in
skirts fastened on to the unhappy
man, and, walking beside him,
pointed out with great lingual
velocity that he was quite wrong
in assuming that woman would
have her delicate bloom wiped off
or lose her charm and modesty in
the rough-and-tumble of politics.
The member wriggled and squirmed
uneasily, and paced the corridor
with a worried look, too terrified
to venture out into the night with
his fierce companion. But the lady
was not shy. “If you are going
down the street,” she said, “ I will
walk with you and we can argue
this matter out.” That drove the
champion of a delicate and angelic
brand of womanhood from his last
trench. “Excuse me,” he said,
“but I really cannot accompany
you. Fact is, my wife may be wait-
ing on the steps, and I am afraid she might—er—
make a —er—disturbance if we walked out to-
gether.” As a matter of fact, the lady was waiting
on the steps. She had a habit of waiting on the
steps and in the shadows.

The banana business in Melb. has been
ruined by recent press remarks concerning the
possibility of this fruit spreading the plague.
Good, sound, well-grown bananas are now being
vainly hawked around at “ Id. per dozen,”
whereas at 6d. per dozen in ordinary times the
niarket is rushed. The scare is in this way driv-
ing scores of one-time flourishing banana-hawkers
into the uncertain paths of bottle-gathering and
burglary. Apropos an American M.D. declares
that the banana of commerce is no longer fit for
human consumption ; it is a degenerate plant, to
be avoided by all but aboriginal races who have

not yet quite emerged from the arboreal and cave-
dwelling period of the. past, The • banana is an
endogen : it flowers and fruits under transplanta-
tion, but never seeds—except at the Andaman
Islands, in the Bay of Bengal, where it grows
wild. Elsewhere it has lost the power of seeding,
and has to be propagated by cuttings. Charles
Kingsley, in “At Last,” goes into the matter at
length, and, in his opinion, the banana of the
Andamans stands alone in the vegetable kingdom;
it is the sole vegetable survival of Tertiary times
when there was a continent all round the Equator,
and Lemuria —the home of the anthropoid apes—
covering what is now the greater part of the In-
dian, South Pacific, and Antarctic oceans stood
high above the sea-level. Three millions of years
ago, bananas, no doubt, were excellent food for the
immediate ancestor of primitive man—the “ Cata-
whine,” or missing-link, recently discovered in
Java. Nowadays the banana is played out; better
give it to the canary.

* * • •
The other day a mild-eyed man who had
been waiting for the door of the Board of Health
offices to open, rushed in and grabbed the inocu-
lating medico. “Am I infectious ? ” he demanded,
shaking his fist savagely; “tell me—am I infec-
tious?” “Infectious from what?” asked the
doctor, with a surreptitious step towards the
poker. “From that blanky inoculation of yours,
of course,” shouted the man, dancing excitedly.
“ Inoculation —of course you’re not. It isn’t in-
fecting at all.” “ Thank God ! ” said the visitor
with a sigh of relief. “ But look here, I want you
to give me a letter to that effect to my wife. You
see,” he went on hurriedly, “ we’re not long mar-
ried, and she’s a bit nervous about the plague, you
know.”
• • * *
Ed. Bulletin.— Re this Sydney rat-scare.
Why does not Sydney’s Mayor import a shipment
of ferrets and turn them loose in the city ? The
rats would soon be wiped right out. I remember
aflour-millon N.S.W. South Coast being so infested
with rats that wheat couldn’t be stacked in it, and
all the cats could do was iike taking a man or two
out of China. At last the owner got a pair of
ferrets — and in three days there was not a live rat
in the building. The ferrets then tackled other
parts of the town, with the result that not a rat
has been seen in it since. A ferret can go any-
where that a rat can, so that there is no get-away.
The slayers themselves might become a nuisance,
but it would be after our time, as they breed but
once a year, and then only one at a time.—A.G.
The whole duty of woman, according to
recent Vic. critics of the woman-suffrage move-
ment, is to bear children and avoid politics. In
short, to bear and forbear.
In Victoria Barracks (Sydney) is a dissi-
pated ram much given to the consumption of
cigarette and cigar “bumpers.” To satisfy
his tobacco-craving, the audacious mutton
recently upended a gorgeous volunteer
officer, and annexed the cigar lie was
Mnoking. The outraged warrior has since
demanded the ram’s execution, but, as
the warrior could be much better spared
than the ram, the latter will probably
continue to flourish.
Adelaide Hospital trouble nearly settled
itself the other day. All the striking medicos
were about to resume their places; when Premier
Holder, against the wishes of the Hospital Board,
insisted upon appointing Poo-bail Ramsay Smith
(one of the original doctors imported) to the
lionorary staff. As the original strikers don’t
speak to the perky little doctor, matters remain as
before.
A certain town in Northern M.L. possesses
a unique combination of hearse and pleasure-
waggon. You can hire this in the morning for a
picnic (when it’s a dashing four-horse drag), and
in the afternoon you may see a funeral headed by
this identical vehicle disguised under a huge black
lid and with two of the same horses sedately crawl-
ing along. The owners of the combination have
been known, in a single day, to drive a circus-band
round the town, retire and wash the
elephant-and-tiger bills off, come
out again as an electioneering
car, gather up all the
voters, retire again and
lick the candidate’s
bills off, and emerge
again late in the
afternoon adorned
with white ribbon
and dash away a
wedding-party.
It also meets
the late train
every even-
ing, and is
advertised
as a ’bus.

So far, there is but one good story told of
Sydney University undergraduate wit: “On one
occasion, two candidates, and two only, a lady and
a gentleman, were presented for the science degree.
With the robed Chancellor reciting his formula
before them, the suggestions were irresistibly
nuptial, but they were not generally perceived till,
when the official had concluded his scarcely-
audible remarks, a deep response, ‘I do,’ came
from the undergraduates’ benches.”
* * * *
F. S. Boyce, B.A , L.L.8., who stated the
case for the Sydney University undergrads at their
make-shift commemoration, was one of the lesser
lights who did yeoman service for Australia in the
struggle for the Commonwealth Bill (and for
which, by the way, lie, with others, got mighty
little thanks). He made a peculiar start in his
speech at Sydney Town Hall last Saturday. “ I
am not here, ladies and gentlemen ” (impressive
pause) ; “ Ladies and gentlemen, none of you are
here” —another pause, filled in by wild cheering
from the students, quick to take advantage even
of their champion. Then Mr. Boyce went on un-
disturbed to explain that no one was there to in-
sult the Senate. Which, under all the circum-
stances, was a very necessary explanation indeed.

Lord Brassey, with a vain eye on the
Governor-Generalship, is determined to keep him-
self in evidence in Melbourne. He has written,
from the Seychelles, a gushing commendation of
the Vic. Railway Commissioner who, it appears,
is quite the primest thing in Railway Commis-
sioners that Uncle Tom has ever struck. Victoria
is rejoiced to hear, furthermore, that local porters
were never openly rude to Uncle ; and he was
rarely or never told to “go and hump his
blanky bundle himself.” It is a positive fact,
though, that the Brassey never fell off the Vic.
railway system, and that is something to be grate-
ful for.
» * • »
Judge Stephen, the other day, sentenced
the swagman, Busch, who brutally assaulted the
school-teacher at Wellesley (N.S.W.), to 14 years.
If the prisoner really did everything imputed to
him, and under the circumstances set forth, he
richly deserved his fate. But Busch was a friend-
less stranger, and undefended. His story was that
he had no “intent,” and only wanted at first to
look at the school maps. ‘ ‘ People always regarded
swagmen with suspicion; the lady may have
thought he intended to insult her, and when
she grabbed his gun he had to pull it away
from her.” It is just possible that the
truth lav somewhere between the two ver-
sions. But one thing is sure and certain :
It is a monstrous thing that where a penniless
man is put up to be tried for everything short of
his life ne should not have placed at his disposal
even the slenderest means of defence, despite the
money and trouble expended over his prosecution.
The Judge was quite right in declaring that no
woman should be sent by the school authorities to
such a lonely place as that at which Miss Huie
was stationed, and it is to be hoped the Education
authorities will profit by his Honor’s remarks-

• • • ••
The Niagara outrage has been a somewhat
amusing and various sensation as sot fortli by the
Australian press, In the heat of the moment it
was represented by the cable as another bit of
Boer treachery ; next morning it was the diaboli-
cal work of tne grain shovellers’ union; but on
the third day it had developed into a Fenian con-
spiracy, with power to add to its numbers, so to
speak. The Bulletin concludes that the outrage
was really the work of the Australian Federal
delegates, whose object was to avert general atten-
tion whilst they smuggled the Commonwealth
Bill past the British Government.
* * * *
Also that terrible calamity, the Ottawa
fire, was described by the cable as of “pro-Boer”
emanation. Next came the yarn that it was
started by “the Fenians ” as a mark of sympathy
with the Boers. Now it is admitted that the con-
flagration was accidental. Wanted, very badly, a
Pacific cable, run by people that take no stock in
the old-world hatreds that have been such a curse
to Australasia.
* * * *
The very latest idea in the way of Victorian
telephone reform has been struck by P.-M.-G.-
Watt. Hereafter, when the ’phone attendants
come on duty at 8.30 a.m., they are to ring up
every subscriber and ask him if his instrument is
working all right, and if he has any objections to
make. At that hour of the morning it would be a
great deal more to the point to ask him if he was up
and has got all his clothes on yet, and whether he
found the bath cold, and did he use Pear’s soap or
Sunlight ?
* * • *
One Beck was sentenced last week at
Armidale (N.S.W.) Circuit Court to six months
hard labor either for stealing or receiving, not for
both, and neither Judge nor jury could say for
which. Two persons were charged with stealing
or receiving £25; the jury acquitted one and
found the other (Beck) guilty in the words of the
indictment: Guilty of “stealing or receiving.”
“But which?” asked his Honor. Enquiry was
bootless. The jury had got there and wouldn’t go
another inch. “But I don’t know for which
crime to sentence.” However, his Honor slept on
it, and next day recorded six months’ hard,
leaving, apparently, the prisoner to take it out in
whichever capacity suited him.

Mackennal’s fine statue of V.R. has duly

The Sydney Royal Show was a passing
good show in its way. though it might have been
better. Had the thermometer registered 180°
under a hat, or the mercury cracked the tube with
the quintescence of cold, then things would have
hummed. As it was, it was an ordinary kind of
show without special feature to impress it on the
tablets of the brain in contradistinction to the
hundreds of other similar exhibitions that have
been held ever since Fred Webster took the helm,
and before. Perhaps the public would rather not
have been frizzled up like hash-house bacon or frozen
down like refrigerated mutton, but had either of
these contingencies occurred they would have
found that shelter awaited them in the P. and B.
Ruberoid house. There they could have preserved
their equanimity while others outside suffered all
the pangs of extremes of heat or cold according as
the fates or inclement Wragge had decreed. It is
a strong point about this Ruberoid that it acts as a
kind of insulating material which, through its
being a bad conductor of heat, is equally impervious
to the most penetrating rays of an out-back sun
or the hoariest of hoar-frosts. A timber and gal-
vanised-iron crib is therefore not alongside it for
comfort. Nor does it nearly approach it for
artistic appearance. This, however, is a mere
detail that doesn’t count much where draughts
prevail. The whole Alpha and the Omega, and
the Beta and the Theta, of the matter lies in the
query—ls it cheaper ? When the local bank mana-
ger holds the whip that is what he will ask. When
the mother of six wants a new bonnet; that is what
she will ask. And the man that provides for
them all naturally asks the same question. The
reply is that by erecting a P. and B. Ruberoid
crib instead of one of the galvanised iron variety
you save stuff all the time; you save in the initial
cost; yon save in construction, eventually saving
your breath on oaths; so what more do you want ?
A member of the Bulletin staff, who hasn’t got a
profound craving for dossing in the open, is
thinking of petitioning the member for the
Domain to log-roll a few Ruberoid cribs into his
camping-ground. A water-frontage abreast of
Lady Macquarie’s Chair would be N.T.9.
A profane sawbones sends to The Bulle-
tin his “ Hymn of the Plague Bacillus ” :
Eternal Father, by Whose grace
O’er this wide world we hold full sway,
Incline Thine awful ear a space
As—ere we humbly kneel to pray —
Our Hymn of adoration soars
To where Thou reignest King Supreme ;
The childish treble of our spores
Commingling in the holy theme.
From buboes tense and tender, Lord !
From ulcers wide and widening fast
Our praise bursts forth with one accord—
O may Thy mercies ever last!
How great Thy fostering care sublime !
Thou madest Man for our delight—
The dark-skinned hordes of Eastern clime,
The succulent and juicy white —
O flesh so fair 1 O blood so sweet!
Their swollen throbbing glands attest
With each despairing labored beat
Thy love Divine made manifest 1
With Thee beneath our banners wide,
What shall our hellish foes attain ?
With impious test-tube—stain—glass-slide—
And all their prophylactics vain—
And “ culture-plates”—How they blaspheme,
Profane bacteriologists !
Thy righteous wrath shall surely gleam
On these accursbd scientists !
And when this world dissolves in flame
To light our passage to the skies,
Celestial journals shall proclaim—
The Plague arrived in Paradise :
There with a chastened, calm delight
We shall disport in glands Divine;
Nor antitoxins may affright,
Nor Haffkines murd’rous in design.
