A WOMAN’S LETTER.
~ ~ _ _ Sydney, May 1, 1900.
My Dear Mtee.—
The Sydney University undergrads have had
their commemoration after all. A wet-blanketed
side-show commemoration, very different from
their original idea, which was to hold a satirical
festival, borne of the songs intended to be sung,
and published ere students realised the strength
or the powers that be, show how satirical the
commem. might have been but for the Senatorial
Thou shalt not play the goat, the ass, the ape,
the ox or tin-whistle before Me ! ” All of a sudden
such verses as this (re “the Teaching Staff”) were
withdrawn from the programme—
There’s Micky who apparently has shrunken in the wash,
There s Jummy with a liver, and a tongue that’s often
rash,
And Pitt’s the proud possessor of an adamantine chlr,
And Trecky is a beggar who is hardly fit to live.
Chorus—Dons, profs, &c.
So it became a commemoration of Second Thoughts
and not of Original Impulses, and its flatness was
mfmitely more in keeping with the real temper of
the Australian undergraduate than the grotesquely-
artincial, pumped-up hilarity of previous celebra-
tions.
Our undergrad, is not naturally so recklessly
high-spirited and wantonly hilarious as his old-
world, and more especially his Continental, con-
temporaries. He is on the average an eminently
sane and sober-minded person, and some faint
suspicion of “the wit to madness near allied ”
the possession of a spark of genius—is surely the
only decent warrant for a well-grown youth
assuming in a street-procession a pair of perfectly-
superfluous donkey’s ears, or hauling a semi-
decomposed leg of mutton up at the end of a
string m the presence of an allegedly learned
assemblage.
There is little of the truly Bohemian in the
Australian ’Varsity student. Unlike so many of
his old-world prototypes, whose imagination he so
sadly lacks, he is eminently practical and law-
abiding, as witness his prompt right-about-face on
the present occasion when the authorities showed
their teeth. So far as up-to-date experience goes,
our students may be relied on never to write and
sing a song which will cause a revolution, or
murder anybody in the interests of Liberty, or
even of tyranny.
Three street urchins, shoeless, hatless, partly
shirtless, wholly insanitary, a German band and a
couple of hundred undergraduates. That was the
order of the procession that passed me in Pitt-
street en route to the down Hall. There was
nothing grotesque in it. No academic cap
was this time replaced by a mask, no asses’
ears were worn, no gown was doffed in favor of
calf-skin. Some of the students carried banners,
others—with pugilistic faces—smoked short pipes.
All looked self-conscious and rather ashamed of
themselves—perhaps because they Lad promised to
be good boys and Respectable.
At the Town Hall the campaign opened with the
singing of “ The Absent-minded ” and—but
here let me digress. Years ago, when mother was
young, there was a pianoforte-piece called “ The
Maiden’s Prayer” in the land, and almost as
“popular” as the “A.M.8.” is to-day. The
visitation lasted some decades, then the Fltegende
Blatter, a German paper that absolutely pers-
pires in its efforts to be comic, brought out two
cartoonlets on the subject. In the first, a maiden
is shown at the piano playing her “Prayer.” At
her shoulder stands Old Scratch smiling, and
beating time with his tail. In the second, bat-
talions of souls are being pitchforked into Tophet
by 0. Scratch’s understudies. The crime of these
lost souls was profanity induced by too much
“ Maiden’s Prayer.” I hope my moral is obvious.
Hall and galleries were packed with visitors.
At the back of the massed undergraduates was a
farden of color. The brightest headgear and
louses seemed to have eddied there, but, as a
matter of fact, the patch was merely thrown into
relief by the black-capped and gowned students’
square. It reminded me, as seen from the gallery,
of the poppy-show we made in the days when we
were shedding our milk-teeth. A petal from all
the flowers we could find sandwiched between two
pieces of broken window-glass. The -.. . f et gera-
nium petal killed the other colors, yso how we
loved it ! In this millinery patch the intrusive,
quarrelsome “ automobile ” shade killed even the
primary colors. From a distance the women-
students in their caps and cloaks and white
collars and cuffs looked like nurses ; only, unlike
nurses, they tucked away some obviously fine hair
under their trenchers as if ashamed of it. I advise
them to ask a theological student for the loan of a
Bible and read 1 Cor. 11. 15.
Mr. Whitfeld, president of the Undergraduates’
Association, and a crowd of graduates occupied
the platform, and looked as if they had come to
listen to a sermon on eternal punishment. The
undergrads behaved like seminarists. Never was
such an orderly commemoration. Only once was
the academic seriousness marred—when Lords
Roberts and Kitchener and Baden-Powell and
Kruger came up the hall to the foot of the plat-
form and asked for degrees. Lord Roberts ap-
peared to be suffering from blind staggers, and his
legs were stiff er than those of the real “Bobs.”
He saluted frequently and sat down on his helmet
often. Lord Kitchener’s facial make-up was so
good that he looked as wicked as the Khartoum
person he represented. At least I hope, for the
mummer’s sake, that it was make-up. Otherwise,
unless he can continue to wear some proof of per-
fect respectability, such as collar fastened behind,
a policeman’s badge, or a military uniform, he’ll
find himself so heavily handicapped in his career
by that face that he’ll never dare to move abroad
without credentials from two clergymen. Kruger
was ten inches under the real Krugerish standard,
and looked as if he would be grateful for a job at
umbrella-mending.
J can’t understand why the women students
didn’t make a demonstration in favor of Baden-
Powell; the gentleman who acted him looked nice
and had the Hat. Baden-Powell without that
Hat would be nothing but—Powell.
Mr. Whitfeld, a lock of fair hair verandahing
his forehead, made a good speech in a beautiful
voice explanatory and apologetic of the relations
between the Senate and the Undergrads’ Associa-
tion. Some one told me the speaker was studying
engineering. What a waste ! With that gift of
voice and Heaven-given tongue ! That such a man
should be a student of stump-jumping ploughs and
iron rams instead of a barrister championing
beauty in distress, or a High Church curate or
priest hearing confessions !
Mr. Reid, Q.C., in the scarlet robes of a doctor
of law, did not remind me of Essie Jenyns’ Portia
as he rolled up to the platform. He got the
biggest reception of the day and made one of those
speeches that leave the hearers midway between
happiness and misery. A kind of let-me-go-
home-and-beeome-good-and-great feeling. Shy-
ness versus jostling, self - reliance versus Self-
sufficiency, gave him some good openings to talk
at the “young mind.” It was all very fine yet
simple, and might be boiled down to Thoreau’s
“ Rescue the drowning and tie your shoe-strings.”
The ever-blushful George Reid hates the young
man who knows not the charm of shyness, and at
the statement he was wildly cheered by the male
uadergrads.—each was so fully assured of his own
kinship to the modest violet.
Student songs followed. “The Microbe that
Lurks in the Rat ” was quite a success, and the
squeak of the trapped rat on the Town Hall organ
was realistically pathetic—you could almost smell
the toasted cheese that lured him to his doom.
Presently all the young men of the University
Sports Club, responding to their roll-call, came up
to receive their badges and shake hands with the
President of the Sports Union.
The dressing was like the day—a St. Martin’s
summer day—-holding truce with winter. There
were tailor-mades and muslins and —and a sea-gull
sitting on a hat —a fashion which, lam told, hails
from the Pacific Slope. The wearer was a really
pretty girl, but you didn’t know that for a long
time because the personality of the gull held you.
It was a full-grown, able-bodied fowl with a power
of a beak, and it was leaning forward trying to
get a peep under the brim at the face of the
wearer. The life and humor in its eyes and pose
were astonishing. You could scarcely realise that
its legs were off and its vitals replaced by cloves,
pepper .and sawdust. Lady Harris, not looking
up to her usual form, was present with Miss
Harris.
Mayoress Harris’s reception last Tuesday after-
noon was a brilliant opening for the season. The
Mayoress wore grey brocaded in white. Miss
Harris was in heliotrope. A musical programme
of twelve numbers was capital. So were the tea
and decorations.
The last stage of the Victoria Ladies’ Club
ended in a Pitt-street auction room last week.
Not here any more than in London are women’s
residential clubs a success, yet there is a good
opening for them. An article in a recent number
of the Nineteenth Century attributes their
failure to vexatious rules and restrictions and high
rents. The Victoria Salon died some months
ago, and now the residential club’s furniture, its
grand piano, and mosquito-netted cots have passed
under the hammer. All went cheap except the
grand piano; that was withdrawn at £49. Mr.
Lawson nearly wept at having to let a case of
dessert knives and forks “which, on Miss
Manning’s honor, cost 10 guineas” go at £3 7s Gd.
A little Lee-Enfield rifle brought less than a
pound, two “ British bull-dogs ” (their ominous
click would alone keep males off the premises)
went at half-a-crown each. Some members of the
next Bushmen’s Contingent will go out murder-
ously armed, that’s certain.
Miss Alice Hollander’s debftt at the Y.M.C.A.
Hall drew such an audience that a third of it
couldn’t get past the mat. Some.of it stayed on
the mat and kerb, and was rewarded for its
patience and love of music with golden notes—all
the high notes. Miss Hollander has found favor
with the critics; recognised judges reckon her
voice a very good one, with a certainty of being
better. Scores of self-constituted judges they
who said Madame Albani couldn’t sing—say that
Miss Hollander hasn’t a voice and will have
a worse one. If the p ... debutante had put
herself in the hands of a fashion-expert, she would
have looked well—much better, in fact. That
short, scanty Empire robe strained across the bust
made the artistic eye sad and mad.
Yet another just-fledged contralto will sing at
the Y.M.C.A. hall on Wednesday, May 9. And
her name, Camille Sers, is a most attractive one,
and suggests a French novel. Favorite artists will
assist Miss Sers, and the concert will be under the
management of Mr. J. Ed. Sykes.
Everyone of any social consequence will be at
the Centennial Tennis Club’s sixth annual dance
at Leigh House on Friday, May 18. Tickets are
selling fast.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The old sweetheart who has taken Thekla
Dubberke for better or for worse is not likely
to repent his temerity, I reckon, inasmuch
as the evidence re Thekla’s share in the Boot
Trunk Tragedy business went to show that she
had the makings of a valuable wife. She was
not without feminine sympathy for unfortunate
Mabel Ambrose in her sufferings, but when the
girl had been done to death by a pair of heartless
fools, and sympathy couldn’t bring her back to
life, Thekla’s courageous callousness came to the
aid of her trembling companions. The idea of at
once ‘ giving them away ’ to save herself had no
charms for the fine, healthy Thekla. She strung
up her nerves and did her best for ‘ the firm,’
and now she has got a husband the chances are
that she 11 battle like a Boer woman in his
interests. * Thekla was a bit blue as a spinster,
but it was true blue all the time. May she live
happily and become the mother of a numerous,
strong-minded family !
“Judge Casey’s retirement from Vic. County
Court causas _ no weeping among lawyers in
general. ‘lt is a pleasure to listen to you, Mr.
fenookins,’ he would say to one in his oily way ;
and to another, snappishly, ‘ Nothing you can say
will affect my mind, Mr. brief.’ The Judge is an
inveterate _ diner-out, is supposed to be a fine
judge of wine, and can say pretty things to pretty
girls. The alert blue-stocking girl thinks him ‘ a
foolish old man.’ Not even C. J. Madden has a
politer smirk than Casey, but Madden also feels
politely, while people thought Casey was least to
be trusted when he was smiling most. He has
several times been to Egypt, and will go there
again in his retirement. He likes looking at the
Sphinx, and fooling round among mummies.
“ ?f°f- Marshall-Hall is bursting with energy,
and nis concert season promises to give musical
Melbourne plenty to listen to. Concerts under
his baton are on during the present week. It is
time for him to do something startling. He has
contributed nothing sensational for the diversion
of the city for over a year ; and a year without
Marshall-Hall getting into trouble of some sort is
a year thrown away.
In connection with Victoria’s portion of the
Imperial Contingent, Victoria has surpassed her-
self in her capacity for muddling. If she did not
want to send the men away, she should have said
so ; if she agreed to do it, she should have done it
properly. The scandals in regard to the whole
affair are almost uncountable. Too many men
were accepted. All were ill-fed, badly treated in
every conceivable manner. For a week they ate
sodden food, slept in pools of water, and were
perpetually wet tnrough. Their saddles are bad.
I heir guns were taken away at the last minute—
because—well, no one seems to know why. The
surplus men were kept in camp until the last day,
uncertain as to their fate —not even knowing they
were surplus. _ Then they were turned out, money-
u j man y instances in rags, for their clothes
had been worn out by the hard kfe of the first
weeks in camp.
“In travelling backwards and forwards from
Langwarrin, though in uniform, no reduction was
made in fares, although all members of other con-
tingents had travelled free without question. An
old woman who wished to bid her son farewell
walked 40 miles to do so, for neither he nor she
had the money for the fare. If Victoria does not
approve of free passes, she should have allowed
none to other contingents. She did allow them,
however, and created a precedent which has been
ungracefully and meanly broken. However, the
worst of the whole scandal is, I am told, the defec-
tive saddlery, and someone should suffer. But no
doubt it won’t be the guilty even then.
“After all, there may be much method in the
seeming madness of the damsels who go to China
to convert the heathen Chinky. News came to Mel-
bourne the other day that two of the Hot Gospel-
lers from that city—Miss Lizzie Hunt and Mr. J.
C- Platt—were lately married at Pao-ning, West
China. Woman’s first mission is to convert a
bachelor, or widower, into a husband.
“Weddings grow more and more theatrical,
boon superannuated scenic artists and ballet mis-
tresses may be able to turn a penny by group-
ing and marshalling Naicest marriage-pageants.
At a recent smart country ‘ tying-up ’ the
church smacked of a pantomime fairy-dell, what
with floral pillars, arches, and wedding-bells. The
floor was strewn with rose-petals, and the pulpit
and chancel-rails sulked behind a tropical planta-
tion. The leading actors faced the limelight, so
to speak, in front of an ivy trellis, from whose
open doors white doves flew, while a big blossom
bell dangled overhead.
“Me this same marriage: the railway arrange-
ments didn’t provide for a timely honeymoon
departure ; but, ble»s you, a wedding without a
farewell tableau would be like a pretty widow
minus a becoming ‘weeper’s cap.’ Anyway, at
the psychological moment the young things, in
chic travelling finery, stepped into a carriage
drawn by ‘ spanking greys.’ The bridegroom took
the reins, and off they dashed, amidst tears,
cheers, rose-leaves, and confetti. When the guests
were gone, the wanderers returned from their
drive, ate a good dinner, put on their second-bests
most likely, and left in real earnest by an evening
train.
“Another recent bride, a parson’s pretty
daughter, made a charming picture at the altar, as
she clasped devoutly a marriage-service, bound in
white moire, with long-fingered satin markers.
“Smiler Peacock is going to be married to a
Warmambool girl. His laugh is doomed. He is
said to have solemnly promised the lady to give it
up. In any case, he could not keep it up.
“Miss Holden, upon whom Mr. Peacock, that
incorrigible bachelor, is to bestow his hand, heart,
laugh, and all his other worldly goods, is a sister-
in-law of Mr. Horsfall, a local solicitor. The
bride-elect, an every-day, pleasant, chatty type of
girl, first struck it rich in the affections of the then
Minister of Education more than a year ago, when
he went to an A.N.A. conference at Port Fairy,
Miss Holden’s parental home.
“ People await on tenterhooks the announcement
of another political engagement, implicating a
bachelor, whose single bliss was a tried and trusted
thing in the long ago, when Mr. Peacock was but
a chick under the maternal wing.
“ The epidemic wedding-gift of the moment is
a silver photo-frame, while for trays, &c., mounted
in silver, ebony promises to oust oak.
“ Womanity has something in common with cat
and tiger nature. For instance, a belle cold-
shoulders an admirer and marries t’other fellow,
but she doesn’t wipe the lover all forlorn off her
slate ! If sugared smiles and honeyed invitations
can cajole an unsuccessful suitor to his charmer’s
wedding, lie’ll be there. Failing the chief mourner
himself, his sisters, cousins, or aunts are found
acting as bridesmaids, or in some other capacity,
where they serve as channels to convey accounts
of Herself’s bliss and bridal beauty to their restive
kinsman.
Chronicles of wedding-gifts provide interesting
reading for those in the know. Lorn adorers
often give spoons, goodness knows why.
“ The second son of Mr. Joshua, of ‘ Boomerang
Brandy ’ fame, has gone north to be married. His
bride, Miss Ballantyne, is a Queensland girl,
beautiful and charming, and their home will be in
Melbourne. The bridegroom, who belongs to the
firm, is an artist of some ability.
“Newest evening gowns do not soften the
meeting-place of bust and bodice. A firm line of
satin, or just the top of the bodice itself, is now
more modish, if less becoming.
“ Enormous silk flowers, poppies, orchids, &c.,
in green, pink and mauve, will crowd the hats
next Cup-day. Grapes and blackberries already
make beholders eye autumn toques with a hungry
glare.
“ The coming skirt is to have draperies caught
up at the back or on the hips. Even tailor-mades
are trimmed with straps and bands. Rear skirts
for the present have either single or double box-
pleats, flattened by ironing.
“The bubonic plague germ ought to have a
monument erected in its honor, to be placed (say)
in the Town Hall room where the councillors
1 wolf ’ their provender. The little beastie has
done what the doctors, the sanitarians, and the
decent members of the public have hitherto failed
to accomplish. The present writer’s back-yard,
for instance, has never been officially inspected
before. Now all backyards are being inspected by
an officer with gold braid on his flat and brass
buttons on his coat. The mere approacli of the
gorgeous being is enough to make backyards clean
themselves automatically. Writer’s dust-box,
which was poked by the ferrule of the inspector’s
umbrella, was heard saying to the back-drain that
it had been visited by the Lieutenant-Governor,
and was going to have the Royal Arms put on the
lid. Mere brag, of course, but the pride of the
dust-box was pardonable.
_ “ When a Melb. plague-inspector recently
visited a certain house and came in due course to
the kitchen the door was shut in his face, and a
sound was heard as of something weighty being
dragged across the floor. This was suspicious, and
the official insisted upon admission ; but as soon
as the door was opened the feminines who had
been escorting him rushed to the table and made a
great effort to conceal with their outspread skirts
something which was underneath the table. After
several futile efforts to see the investigator kicked
under the table, and his foot struck something
that returned a sharp metallic sound. ‘ What’s
in that tub ? ’ he authoritatively demanded. ‘My
sister ! ’ shrieked one of the girls. The poor man
had intruded upon an impromptu bathroom. He
gasped and bolted.
“I wonder how Samoa is getting on under
German rule. I was there some years ago, and the
big fat soldiers and sailors were cordially detested
by the natives. I don’t imagine Germans are
naturally more cruel than Britons, but they are
more over-bearing and tyrannous, and insist on
ruling where rule is not needed. However some
of the women put up with them contentedly. I
stopped to speak to a handsome girl of twenty
who stood on the beach with two children at her
skirt and a third in her arms. ‘Yours?’ ‘Yes,
mine ! This one,’ signifying the eldest, ‘ Consul’s’
little boy. This one Fritz Hoffmeyer’s. This
one my husband’s ’—and she bent over the infant
fondly. The incident stuck in my mind, because
I had two Melbourne girls with me—and they
flushed a lovely crimson.
“ Poor Mrs. Dr. Blair is quite heart-broken over
the death of her aboriginal boy, Lamey, whom she
adopted when he was a little Queensland foundling.
The piccaninny had grown into an intelligent,
polite little gentleman, and his ‘mother’ could
never see anything funny in the contrast between
their complexions. Nor was it until Larhey’s
death that she ever heard the story—which is a
wicked fib. by the way—of her introducing ‘my
boy ’ to Lady Hopetoun, and being snubbed with
the sneerful remark, ‘ By a former husband, I pre-
sume.’ Lady H. didn’t say anything of the sort.
But the fibster wasn’t very wrong in supposing
that the chillv, silent Governoress might have said
it if the idea nad occurred to her. Women would
say some awfully sweet things to one another if
they could only think of them at the time.
Y ours affectionately,

Noticeable that Banjo Paterson’s and
Baden Powell’s initials are the same—they are
both B.Ps. When the gallant pressman saw so
many well-dressed and shapely damsels thronging
the streets of Bloemfontein he knew the P.D.
corset was in evidence and accepted this as a proof
of a high degree of civilisation and good taste—on
the part of the lady inhabitants, anyhow. Thus
both B.Ps. concur in their admiration of the
female form in its P.D. state.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
“ Cecil Rhodes, ” writes somebody, “is a
glorified Randolph Bedford.”
Sergeant-Major W. Napier, of Carlton, is
believed to be the only man in Victoria in posses-
sion of a V.C. The distinction was won in India
in 1858.
Brassey’s son Tom has abandoned the idea
of going to the war. That’s fortunate. There
was always the danger of a Brassey being regarded
as a bore.
An interesting item is that R. D. Meagher
(M.L.A.) is applying to be restored to the N.S.W.
roll of solicitors, off which he was struck after his
Dean affair.
While Henry Lawson was passing through
Melbourne, last week, a quick sketch, in oils, said
to be a very powerful picture, was made of him by
John Longstaff.
During a post-mortem on the body of a
man recently drowned in the Campaspe River
(Victoria), Dr. Kelly, of Echuca, found a wax
match imbedded in the deceased’s heart.
Sydney suburban Police-court amenities :
Fuming little solicitor—“My friend opens his
mouth as if he were going to eat me.” His friend—
“ Pardon me, your Worship, I’m a Jew and don’t
eat pork.”
American-Consul-gasometer Bell the other
day prefaced a full column of mellifluous skite, in
Sydney Daily Telegraph, with the remark that
he doesn’t like appearing in print. The Bulletin
should smile!
Mrs. Fraser, the fair shootist, proposes to
follow up her luck by taking a pub in Melbourne.
Her application for a transfer of the license of the
London Hotel, Market St. (off Collins St.) will be
made this week.
Capt. W. R. Randell, who opened up the
navigation of the river Murray to steamers before
Capt. Cadell took a boat up the stream, completed
his 70th year May 1. He still lives at Cumeracha,
S.A., and has an interest in Murray boats.
Dr. Torreggiani, the Italian R.C. Bishop
of Armidale, has been lying seriously ill at
Waverley, Sydney. The bishop, scaling close on
21st., is reputed the heaviest ecclesiastic in Aus-
tralia, and the heaviest bishop in British posses-
sions.
Died, at Powell's Creek, Centralia, Alfred
Pybus, one of the best of bushmen—the man who
led Gov. Kintore’s party across the continent in
91, and by his good work turned into a picnic
what might easily have been something far less
pleasant.
The modified Linotype outlined by J. A.
Andrews in a recent Bulletin is being taken up
m earnest in Melbourne. Some energetic spirits,
among whom are a couple of practical printers,
think they scent a good tiling, and are eager to
commence operations.
Adelaide’s first bishop, the muscular Dr.
Short, in his day a crack boxer and runner, has a
frandson in the S.A. section of the Imperial
uishmen. On the other side, the youngster is a
grandson of Egerton Warburton, one of the
pluckiest of Australian explorers.
1 he Earl of Yarmouth, whose girly imper-
sonations shook up Australian society a few years
ago, is appearing m New York as a real live pro.
apparently in opposition to the Duke of Manches-
ter, who is in a rival co. The bloatered earl got
rats from the N. Y. press for forgetting his lines.
According to an Argus contributor, that
doughty pugilist, E. P. Slavin counted the loss
of three toes among his Klondyke experiences.
He joined in a rush from Dawson City, stayed out
all night to protect his claim (one man who knew
not Slavin tried to jump it), wore leaky boots, and
was frost-bitten.
Sergeant-Major Griffin, recently memorial-
tableted at Parramatta, N.S.W., was not —as has
been asserted—“ the first Australian to fall in the
South African war.” Griffin was killed at Rens-
burg on January G, but on New Year’s Day had
fallen, at Sunnyside, two Queenslanders —Jones
and M‘Leod.
Of Sydney aldermen, five have held their
seats for 20 years or over. Dean and Lees score
20, R. lowler and John Taylor 21 and 22 respec-
tively ; John Harris has mopped his brow there
for 23 years, and the patriarchal M. Chapman
counts 35 years in office. No wonder Sydney has
wooden aldermen.
J. Langdon Parsons —ex - parson, ex-
Minister of the Crown, ex-N.T. Govt. Resident,
and now S.A. Japanese Consul—is trying to
squeeze into S.A. Leg. Council. He is one of the
brightest^orators S.A. has seen, and—although a
red-hot lory now—was one of the men who
secured free education for S.A.
In Vic. Supreme Court, t’other day, Mel-
bourne’s Mayor M‘Eacharn sought to make a
public statement re a certain suit, and, in
doing so, began by addressing Judge a’Beckett

Judge Casey retires from Vic. County
Court in time to take a turn or two through the
current Paris Exhibition. He was a commissioner
at the Paris Exhibition of over 20 years ago, from
which he carried away a C.M.G. Probably his
most useful performance was the compilation of
“ Casey’s Justices’ Manual.” He also, by motion,
established the Victorian Parliamentary Hansaud.
He is a native of Ireland, and 70 years of ago.
E.\-Attorney-General Want enters lnsssih
year, May 4. He has had a fair share of the good
things in N.S.W. legal and political life. Briefs
have abounded. On two occasions a seat on the
Supreme Court Bench was in his offer, and the
Premiership of his party was once formally ten-
dered to him. Withal, law has for him no fascina-
tions, and the atmosphere of the courts disagrees
with him. Politically, he holds a portion of the
creed of every party.

Capt. Cook first
descried Australia
19th April, 1770. The
anniversary of the
occasion is the time
to say again that
Captain Cook never
visited Port Jackson.
Despite common
opinions, his eye
never lit on the Beau-
tiful Harbor. Cook
as a boy was the
stamp of imp now
collared and run on
the reformatory-ship
Yemon. He was a
level-headed and courageous man when at the age
of 42 he landed at Kumell, Botany Bay. (He
did not fly the Union Jack at the masthead,)
Cook did not appropriate the whole of Australia
by right of discovery; he only claimed the
Eastern Coast, and it was this coastline alone
which justified his giving the name of New South
Wales. Miles of the coast are undo ibtedly like
parts of the Welsh coast. The first reputed
“ devil ” found by his sailors in Australia Cook
hunted down and found to be a bat, thus strang-
ling a superstition in its infancy.
Dr. Leith Napier, the Adelaide front-rank
surgeon, who had his skull fractured at the mili-
tary camp while the second contingent was being
prepared, has made such a marvellous recovery
that recently he was able to visit the Imperial
“ Bushmen’s ” camp. Like Dr. Ramsay Smith,
the other imported senior, Napier is a military
enthusiast—his only son is in one of the S.A.
corps in Safrica.

Court of Appeal materialise, who will be the
“Lord” to represent Australia? Of course he
will have to be made here on the spot. Will it
be the most capable man, Sam. Griffith —or the
obsequious little Way? It certainly won’t be
N.S.W. Chief Justice Darlcy, who, it is cabled,
“ implored ” Agent-General Salomons to protest
publicly against the abolition of the Australian
appeal to the Privy Coun'-il.

li Banjo ” Patterson’s book of verses from
The Bulletin, “The Man from Snowy River,”
has now, within four years from first publication,
had an issue of 20,000 copies at five shillings—the
biggest success ever made by any book in Austral-
asia. And where is the modern English versifier,
bar three or four, who has sold even 5,000 copies
of his verses ?

Chief-Justice Way, S.A.’s dapper, self-
important Judge, was asked recently for an
opinion on some disputed point. He cleared his
throat and gave what he called “ the legal view.”
“ Now,” he said, “I will give you the common -
sense view.” The antithesis made people laugh,
and the perky little Judge colored up. “Law and
common-sense,” he said with much dignity,
“always run on parallel lines.” “True, your
Honor,” chipped in a local scribe, “ but not only
may parallel lines be any distance apart, but they
can never meet.”
To be found out by lightning on one.of
the interminable plains of the N.S.W. Far West
is hard luck, indeed. Yet it was the fate recently
of A. E. Roberts, one of the most pushful of the
Lands Department’s staff surveyors. He deserved
longer life for his industry ; for, instead of idling
away the long evenings in camp he had filled
them by reading for the Bar, and was shortly to
go for his qualifying exam. Mr. Roberts was re-
sponsible for the greater part of the survey of the
White Cliffs.
One morning John Norton arrived at Cir-
cular Quay from His waterside residence, and
hailed a cab; but his two dogs wouldn’t get on
board, preferring to frolic about the Quay to the
accompaniment of much benison from John.
Presently, catching one of the canines, John
hurled it into the cab with a saintly exclamation ;
whereupon a bystander exclaimed—“ One !” Then
the other tyke was caught and cabbed ; and the
bystander counted—“ Two !” And, when John
himself followed, the wag announced —“ Three !”
Jas. Bon wick, F.R.G.S., author of numer-
ous Australian historical books and discoverer of
the tomb of Gov. Phillip, celebrated the 60th
anniversary of his wedding at his residence,
Upper Norwood, London, on 17th April, 1900.
Of late years he has done congenial work
in London as assistant compiler of the his-
torical records of N.S.W. In 1860, when In-
spector of Schools in Victoria, he had a para-
lytic seizure, and was a semi-invalid for years.
He is now in good health at the age of 84. A
very unusual history.
Edward Murphy, M.L.A. (Vic.), recently
deceased, was a genial soul without an enemy.
His great legislative feat was getting the Bungaree
Racecourse Railway Bill through in the teeth of
Premier Turner’s declaration that the Govt, would
not accept it under any conditions. Murphy
made a speech so full of engaging Irish humor
that the House, in a fit of hilarity, passed the
measure by a large majority. Turner fumed, and
said he would arrange with the Leg. Council to
throw out this objectionable line of railway ; but
the M.’sL.A. only roared all the more.
A queer story is told of Benjamin Bridge,
of Narrabri and thereabouts in N.S. Wales, the
noted horse-manipulator and daring bushman
He broke gaol in N.S.W. long ago while serving a
sentence, and having lately been caught in
Westralia was convicted of escaping and sent up
again for two years. It is stated that while at
large after his disappearance he turned up in
Western N.S.W. effectually disguised as a blank
gm and while masquerading as such made the
necessary arangements for his devoted wife to join
him in the West. It is claimed for Bridge that he
lived an honest life while at large, and the Judge
before whom he was convicted promised that if he
could bring evidence to that effect a commutation
would be reoommended.
A. M. Donohue, the London Daily
Chronicle war correspondent who was present at
the capture of Cronje, was less than two years ago
a reporter on Sydney Evening News. He was
noted in Sydney for always having the luok
to be present at accidents. He managed to be
at most of the fatal tram accidents which occurred
while he was in Sydney. He was there when
the lion “Burns’’chewed Fitzgerald’s trainer and
also when diver Peart jumped to death. His luck
has not deserted him sinoe he went to England
with the Lancers, for he managed to get appointed
to a roving war correspondentship, and several of
the seniors being disabled or killed, he slid into
the job of representing a big London daily—right
in the very spot where all the accidents were
happening.
H. M. Hyndman, who was in Ladysmith
luring the siege, experimenting with a new gun
ui the interests of an English firm of manufac-
turers, had a narrow escape of being an able man-
but he is a faddist, and doesn’t stick to things!
After a creditable journalistic connection with
Pall Mall Gazette, under Greenwood he
visited Australia and Fiji in 1869-70. When in
Melbourne, he contributed to the Argus a remark-
ably smart and slating criticism of Marcus
Clarke’s earliest novel, “Long Odds” (first pub-
lished as a serial). The only chapter he spoke
well of was the one written by G. A. Walstab
during Clarke’s illness. Hyndman is remem-
bered in Fiji as the man who offended missionary
Lonmer Fison by objecting, at that good and
genial parson’s table, to grace before meat, with
tiie remark, “ I never pray on an empty stomach ”
On his return to the old country, he tried to create
a scare about the Indian finances, which did not
come off. His next idea was that of becoming a
leader of the Socialists in conjunction with Cham-
pion and others, and he was successful to the
extent of getting himself imprisoned. He seems
now to be a sort of glorified drummer.
Solomon Joseph, proprietor of Tamworth
News, and one time editor of a Hebrew journal in
Melbourne, was last week found dead at his office
at 66 years of age, Mr. Joseph had many of the
qualities which should make a country newspaper
pay handsomely if handsome returns were possible
m such an occupation. He was one of the N.S.W.
country editors who took a weekly letter at a few
shillings from pressman Reseigh Martin (lately
deceased), who had a distorted Railway-Commis-
sioner Eddy on the brain. Whatever the regular
news might be, Martin (he had a connexion in the
N.S.W. railway department) ran in a couple of
pars, rankly libelling the Chief Commissioner.
His special delusion was that Eddy was filling up
N.S.W. railway service with male and female
relatives from England. Eddy at last picked out
a few pars , and scared the proprietors well-nigh
out of their wits. “Be prosecuted for criminal
libel or surrender the writer’s name.” Martin
too was frightened, and begged the newspapers
to keep his name dark, a request some of them
complied with, but which Mr. Joseph, for
pizness’ sake, ” declined to entertain. As the
matter in all the letters was the same, Eddy was
content with Joseph’s divulgement, and ran Mar-
tin into hard labor for a couple of years. Now
Joseph, Martin, and Eddy are at rest.

The season for visiting the old country having
come round again, the upper four-hundred of Sydney are
booking passages for London, and as a safeguard are as
usual depositing their plate and other valuables in the
Sydney Safe Deposit. In many cases they are also rent-
ing safes for the storage of their deeds, etc. In either
case the rentals are very moderate, and the business of
the Deposit is, we learn, rapidly increasing. A visit is
freely invited. The address is Ash-street (back of
Paling’s), off George-street.*

Aboriginalities.
A peculiar case of “ insulting behaviour ”
comes from the vicinity of Bright, Gippsland. A
miner living in a slcillion within a couple of miles
of the township was disturbed in the night by
what he first imagined was the restless and un-
reasonable conduct of his wife, but having fully
awakened he was amazed to find that there were
three people in the bed. His wife was over against
the wall, but next him on the right was a foreign
body of considerable bulk. The husband and
father promptly hoisted the latter on to the floor
with a solid bump, sprang out after, and struck a
light. What he discovered was Jim the hatter
from the hut under the hill, sitting on the
floor, blinking stupidly at his host, and looking
deeply grieved. Jim was in a condition of melan-
choly inebriety, and commenced to shed tears
when expostulated with, and wailed aloud about
the inhumanity of man when the furious house-
holder threw him out into the wild night and
tossed his clothes after him. Jim had simply
wandered into the house and turned in
with his neighbour. The only explanation he
would offer was : “I wash sho lonely, ole f’lr— sho
lonely! ”

“ P.T.F. : The Australian does love a
horse . Cocky friend of mine spent all last week
searching for stray stock. Not out of saddle whole
r • r r, c i ODn OYir i
time except for absolutely necessary sleep and
tucker. Saturday night lie rode 10 miles to town
for a little relaxation. Took it astride a wooden
gee-gee on a merry-go-round. Told me afterwards
that he had done 57 trips, and only knocked off
then because he was down to his last “bob,” and
(apologetically) a man must have one beer when he
comes to town.

' “ W.M.B. Forty miles from Narrabri 1
struck a hut occupied by a kind of hermit. He
- the nephew of a well-known General and hajl
been 2u years in the busli. The God foisaken patch
he was on wouldn t grow anything in a good
season let alone a bad one. Ho had about 30 goats
which he used as meat. No one ever passed by
except the mail-boy and an occasional swagman.
The recluse had a yearly income from “’Ome”
yet he chose that sand-heap to live on, solus.
* * * *
“Trotter”: Heard at Gulgong six that
swagmen were camped in the park pavilion. I
joined them, and gathered that No. 1 was a travel-
ling shoemaker. He carried an enormous swag, in-
cluding tools and a pair of iron lasts weighing
251bs. No. 2 was an Englishman and an ex-bank-
clerk. His swag consisted of six chaff-bags which
he placed inside one another and then got in him-
self—he called it “rap.” He had been 10 years
on the track, and only worked when he couldn’t
help it. No. 3 was a seasoned veteran who had
his round from Mudgee to Wellington, then across
to Gunnedah and back by Muswellbrook. He
knew_ all the places to call at and those to miss.
This is the kind of thing : “Brown’s at \V., he
gives tea and sugar—the old man left it in his will
that no swagman who called was to go without
tea and sugar. At Blunt’s station you get a
deener (shilling) but no tucker. At Smith’s you
get a pannikin of dust (flour).” And so on.
Someone asks: “How about tobacco?” “Oh,
I’m never without weed. You see I smokes bad
tea; then I gets into shows, races, anywhere
where there’s a crowd. I picks out two or three
toffs and blows the smoke all over them till some-
one says, 4 My dear man ! what kind of tobacco
is that you’re smokin’ ? ’ 4 ’Tain’t terbaccer,’ says
I, ‘it’s tea. Can’t get terbaccer these times.’
Then they hands me a pooch or half-a-plug to go
away.” No. 4 was a dapper little piano-tuner
who confided to me that he had been drinking.
He had no swag. No. 5 was a cook, chiefly given
to hard swearing, and No. 6 an Indian hawker
who sat in a corner by himself and ruminated.
Truly a mixed lot!

“ R.H.L.” : Fossicking on the Wentworth
(Gippsland), I started sinking one morning in a
new gully. Saw a snake—stuck the pick m the
ground, and smote him dead with the shovel;
then, turning round for tho pick, very nearly put
my hand on another snake, corkscrewed round tho
handle. Drawing off wearily to get a good biff, I
spotted another, coiled-up in the dish. I left.
* * * *
One of the characteristics of the Aus-
tralian bushman, in the stock-fattening parts
especially, is his ability to tell the weight of a
beast almost to a hair. The weight-guessing com-
Eetition is the most popular part of many way-
ack shows ; and very often one or two strike the
exact figure. At Goulburn, lately, no less than
four “guessed” that a bullock on exhibition
weighed 9401 b., which was right.

“ San Reno, ” on young blacltfellows :
Have you ever observed the marked difference in the
spirits and energies of the adult blackfellow a'nd the
young ditto ? In my experience of the Australian blacks
this contrast has always attracted my attention. The
average Australian white adult (though not of the most
energetic race on earth) surpasses the average black in
energy, life, and go, but the black youngsters are far
more Sjvely and full of animal spirits than the white
children. About the age of 12, or soon after, the small
black’s brain and energy seem to give out, and he becomes
a murky pessimist. In a small butcher’s shop in a Cen-
tralian township some years ago there were with us two
comical and frolicsome little blackfellows about 12 years
old, the liveliest, most cheery little fellows I ever met.
Their curiosity was marvellous : anything they could not
understand they would worry over for days. They
were willing to work, too. Anything that Dealboy
or Tarpot was put to do he simply jumped at.
The new sausage-machine interested them very much.
Tarpot solemnly dropped lumps of beef into the machine,
while Dealboy turned the handle, both with wide-staring
eyes watching the meat as it disappeared and emerged.
Tarpot asked ‘ ‘ Where that pheller meat get to ? ” I pro-
mised to open up the machine and show how it worked
when they had put through all the beef. That beef went
through in record time. When the machine was opened
Dealboy raised the question, “ S’posem we put wallaby
longa that pheller, you think it come out bossum ? ” Tar-
pot looked at him in scorn. Next to the shop lived the
local J.P., who was the possessor of a glass eye, with the
cold, pitiless gaze of which artificial optic he was wont to
transfix the casual drunk. Tarpot volunteered to turn
the grindstone while the J.P. sharpened an axe. After
working manfully for a few minutes Tarpot happened to
look up, and the rigid optic almost mesmerised him. Long
and earnestly he gazed, a look of amazed wonder on his
ebony countenance. The grindstone stopped, and the
handle fell from his nerveless grasp. In an awed
voice he solemnly asked, “My word, Mither Bosite,
what for that pheller eye been look that way ? ”
Tarpot spent the rest of the day trying to acquire the
fixed expression of the J.P.'s glass eye, and Dealboy
helped him. A few mornings later, when the incident
had slipped from my mind, Tarpot and Dealboy were sent
to drive a cartload of beef Co the station. Standing on
the footpath, I saw their driving became more and more
erratic, till at last the horse got on the footpath, and the
shafts and harness became entangled round a verandah
post. I disengaged the horse and the ruins, and then
demanded an explanation. It came promptly. Tarpot
and Dealboy had been trying to drive and to wear the
expression of the J.P.’s glass eye at the same time—hence
the disaster. I have seen Tarpot since then. He is a list-
less grown-up nigger, and a shipload of glass eyes would
not turn him from his path, provided he knew they were
not eatable.
• * • *
“ Tokai ” on a food-horror :
The Q. Minister of Agriculture lately said, in answer to
a question, that he was not aware that any Queensland
meat-works kept pigs which were fed </n offal. Yet pre-
sent writer knows of one big concern (often visited by a
Govt, inspector) where, when things are in full swing,
scores of pigs of all sorts and sizes—some with sore heads,
others with sore eyes, sore backs and sore legs, tumorous,
hideous brutes —feed upon and wallow in offal. Some
time ago a commercial traveller was spending the night
at an accommodation house on one of the Queensland
roads. A big cattle station was within a quarter of a
mile (chiefly owned by a large meat firm), and a number

Dear Bulletin.— Be animal chivalry (B,
10/3/’00), I once had tw6 fox-terriers—one of each
sex—and during the five years I had them
together T nover once saw the dog bite or maltreat
the slut in any way. For amusement I have often
started them “scrimmaging” in fun, and when,
as nearly always happened, she commenced to
“go” for him in earnest, he would stand per-
fectly still, his head turned aside, and let her
worry away with such a will that I frequently had
to go to the rescue. Often, too, I would give
them a large bone—making each take an end ;
but the moment my authority was withdrawn the
dog relinquished his end. He was a dog of mettle,
too, and could keep his end up with the best of
his own sex. As to horses, however, I have fre-
quently seen stallions kick mares, apparently
without provocation.—B.W.

“ Ken ” : Re animal chivalry or otherwise.
My fox-terrier incessantly chivvies the cat, except
when puss has maternal duties to perform. Then
ho declares a truce; but, alas ! on the other hand,
when his own spquse is rearing a family, he snaps
a*id snarls at his progeny whenever they come
near him. Doubts as to paternity a possible
cause. But, for downright unchivalry, our family
rooster takes the cake. Besides neglecting to pay
the usual attentions to a poor, old, half-blind hen,
he has been known to sneak up and rob her of a
hard-earned worm.

“ Werritne© ” : “ Wonga ” (B. 24/3/’OO / ) is
a bit mixed about snakes. Most venomous snakes
can expand the head, but they cannot expand or
contract the body; and the reason “W.” couldn’t
pull his snake out of the hole was because he was
pulling against the edges of the scales, which rise
slightly from the body. To demonstrate this, put
a snake on level ground and draw it backwards.
I once caught a whip-snake by the tail when it had
got half into a hole, and hung on till it broke in
two, the forepart disappearing below.

BEHIND THE BAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By the camp-fire I am sitting,
And I watch the shadows flitting
■Through the tall and dusky gum-trees that like
ghosts spread out afar;
There’s a sadJiess stealing o’er me
As a vision comes before me,
And I dream again I see you as you stand behind
the bar.
And I often think and wonder,
By the starlight as I ponder,
Am I any of the many (could a small boy reach
a star ?). t
While the firelight' 8 rising, siidcing,
Again I dream that lam drinking—
Drinking drinks by white hands given as you
stand behind the bar.
In these dusty wildernesses
Voice whose tones were soft caresses,
Like music when some careless seraph leaves
God’s gates ajar,
Comes back unto me calling,
And the reddened ashes falling
Are like jewels in your scented hair behind the
city bar.
A.A.

“Puyyaka” on Snakes and Snakebite.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When a man realises that it is a genuine tiger-
snake that he has withdrawn on his finger from a
hollow log, he is apt to do some hard thinking
about snakebite remedies. He has probably read
various wall-placards on the subject, but in the
emergency his ideas of treatment are apt to be
confused with the restoration of the apparently-
drowned and the precautions against typhoid.
* * * *
First, about the snake itself. Australia has
more of the venomous colubrines (with grooved
poison-fangs) than any other country, but only the
larger varieties—black, brown, tiger, &c.— are
imminently dangerous to man. Our one viperine
snake, the death adder (Acanthophis antarctica)
enjoys a great local repute, but there are doubters
who place him below the tiger-snake in lethal
powers. He is a true viper, and the perforated
fang which distinguishes him certainly should
facilitate placing the poison deep in the tissues.
REAL AUSTRALIAN BLOOD.
South Australia likewise contemplates organising
a Gentlemen's corps. Herewith are submitted port-
raits of personages of undisputed rank, to wit —
King Billy, of Mudla Wirra, Port Lincoln
Tommy, Coorong Charlie, Tarcowie Pete, Oodla
Wirra Jacky, and Tommy Walker, who, no doubt,
would, if approached properly, be willing to act as
officers, and so obviate the difficulty which will
undoubtedly crop up of finding persons of suitably
gentle birth willing to fill the mere ranks under the
command of the local Johnny Aitkenses.
The death-adder is also the only viviparous Aus-
tralian snake, all others laying eggs, although in
some cases the young, after hatching, seek safety
in the parental throat, after the fashion of some
species of shark.
* * * *
Snake-poison is probably of almost identical
composition in all species, and is closely similar to
that of various stinging insects, such as the scor-
pion. Taken from a healthy, hungry snake, it is
a clear, yellow fluid, somewhat viscid, and giving
an acid reaction. The taste is mawkish and
faintly acid. Squeezing out the glands of an aver-
age-sized snake may yield up to three-quarters of
a drachm, representing, perhaps, eight or ten effec-
tive bites. The poison is harmless when swal-
lowed (as the gastric juices and the bile speedily
neutralise it) unless there is some breach of surface
in the parts it touches ; but if it remains long in
contact with an ordinary mucous surface (as when
injected into the eye while the snake is being
handled) it may be absorbed and set up severe
effects. The blood of a snake-poisoned animal
produces symptoms when injected into a healthy
one, but the flesh may be eaten with impunity.
* * * *
Snake-poison is, toxicologieally, not a “simple”
poison. It contains at least two active elements,
and closely resembles the poisons produced by
germs in such diseases as diphtheria and septic
trouble. One of these elements causes clotting of
the blood in the vessels, and may be neutralised
by moderate heat. The other (or others) which
affect the heart do not lose their effectiveness
when boiled. Certain substances, however, neu-
tralise one or both of the active principles—and
here we come to some of the so-called “ antidotes.”
A l-in-60 solution of fresh hypochlorite of lime
(“ bleaching-powdor ”) injected into the tissues
around a bite will destroy any venom it meets,
provided it is fresh and contains 80 per cent, of
available chlorine. One per cent, gold chloride
will do the same—permanganate of potash mixed
with snake-venom, outside the body, neutralises
it, and the mixture is harmless on injection ; but
in contact with the body-tissues the permanganate
rapidly loses this power. Hypochlorite of lime is
said to prevent the blood clotting in the vessels,
but experiment has hardly supported the assertion.
* * * *
The risk from snakebite depends upon —(1) the
amount of poison injected (depending usually
on the size of the snake and the fullness of the
glands) the time of j ear ; snakes being most
venomous just after casting their skins ; (3) the
interposition of any material, such as cloth,
which may wipe the poison out of the tooth-
groove ; and (4) the position and depth of the
bite. The depth of bite makes an especial differ-
ence. When a strong snake hangs on to the part,
the poison will probably be injected not merely
into the skin-tissues, but into the loose parts
beneath, or even directly into a.vein, whereby it
will be sent through the body very rapidly. The
mental effect (terror) also plays a great part, as it
seriously lowers the resisting-power.
* * •* *
When the bite is unquestionably that of a
venomous snake (and the orthodox pair of punc-
tures don’t place this at all beyond doubt) certain
tilings should be done, whether an “antidote” is
used or not. Prompt action is essential, in order,
by destroying the poison locally,_ to prevent it
from entering the general circulation. The first
necessity is a ligature on the side next the heart
(the bite being on one of the limbs) twisted hard
with a stick, twitch-fashion. Then cut freely into
the marks, even scooping out a piece a quarter of
an inch or so deep. Suck the wound hard (there
won’t be much blood after the first gush), and if
there is any liquor at hand give it moderately.
Probably half the reputed “snakebite” deaths in
Australia are due to alcoholic poisoning by ener-
getic friends. Washing the wound with hypo-
chlorite of lime or gold-chloride solution, if at
hand, or even with Condy’s fluid, is serviceable.
If the bite is on the face, neck, or body, the liga-
ture is generally difficult to use, although, in a
stout subject, a piece of flesh may be pinched up
and tied. In such a case, it is well to scoop out
the marks and cauterise with a hot
iron, In any case, the victim
should be kept moving and awake.
The writer has a vivid recollection
of walking behind with a bunch of
“prickly Moses” while two men
toiled up-and-down a verandah for
a whole night with a 15st. patient
between them.
* »
As to antidotes—all have their
advocates. Prof. Halford, of Mel-
bourne, injects into a vein 10 or 20
drops of strong ammonia, with
rather more water. It is well to see
that it does go into a vein, other-
wise an abscess will supervene, and
may cause death in any case. The
large vein crossing the bend of the
elbow is the safest, the needle being
run into and along, not through it.
The intention is to stimulate the
heart against tho paralysing part
of the venom. Dr. Mueller, of
Yackandandah (Vic.), working on
the same linos, used strychnine, but,
at the Aus. Med. Congress, in 1892, the
Mueller treatment was shown to give a death-rate
of 13'2 per cent, in 113 cases; while “other
methods” (very vague) yielded a death-percentage
of only I'4 in 313 cases.
* * * *
Calmette's anti-venom serum, however, is the
most practical antidote to date—in the scientific
sense of the word. The manufacture is interest-
ing : Three grammes of dried snake-venom (worth
£6, by the way) are dissolved in 300 cubic centi-
metres of distilled water. Half a cubic centimetre
of the mixture (about eight drops) is injected into
the neck of a healthy young horse, and 15 days
afterwards the dose is doubled; the treatment
proceeding thus, till at the end of a year the horse
can stand 50 cubic centimetres —enough to kill 25
unimmunised horses. After 10 months of this
process the serum is ready, and in two or three
years the horse can stand 100 c.c.’s at a dose.
The immunity is then kept up by quarterly
injections of 100 c.c.’s, and the serum, after
being sterilized, is always obtainable.
A 200,000 unit strength is employed—
i.e., a rabbit into which has been
injected 1-200,000th of its body-
weight of serum will resist a dose of
venom that would otherwise kill it
in 12 hours, and one cubic centi-
metre injected into a vein protects
against a dose that would be fatal
in 40 minutes. In the human sub:
ject, 10 or 20 c.c.’s (three to six
drachms) of the serum is injected
into a vein; other precautions
(ligature, &c.) not being neglected.
Some advise a dose of as much as
seven drachms. There are, as a rule,
no unpleasant effects, although ab-
scess has occasionally resulted. Of
the effects of this serum surgeons in
all parts of the world speak highly,
the bites of the Indian cobra and of
Russell’s viper (previously thought to be
inevitably fatal) having been success-
fully treated with it. lis, therefore, pro-
bable that the Calmette serum will speedil_
supplant all other snakebite-cures ; but too much
stress cannot be laid on the need for the imme-
diate use of ligature and knife, whatever other
remedies may be used afterwards.
One Fenn, lately arrived from Cape Town,
was interviewed by Melb. Argus the other day.
Fenn was in Kimberley during the siege. He
thinks the Boers a bad lot, and has the usual
admiration for the angelic Atkins. A cheerful
item from the interview :—
Another day one of Col. Kekewich’s “ Tommies ” had a
thrilling adventure. In one of the sorties he was about
to take a stray Boer prisoner, when the latter shouted
“Mooney, Mooney!” Tommy Atkins thought the Boer
was asking for his money, and waxed very indignant. The
Boer still went on with his “ Mooney, mooney,” which so
exasperated the “ Tommy ” that, shouting “ I’ll mooney
you,” he thrust his bayonet through the Boer’s body. It
was afterwards explained to the Tommy that the word
“ mooney ” meant mercy.
The Boer prisoners brought in (after days of fight-
ing in mud and dust) were, Mr. Fenn says, “ a
dirty, unkempt lot.” The Boer’s neglect of his
tooth-brush and other toilet requisites when under
fire in the trenches and on the kopjes is gradually
weaning from him the last vestiges of all true
Jingoes’ sympathy.

Recent geological researches prove that
hippopotami, rhinoceroses, elephants, and such
big game, existed before historic time all over tho
site of Rome, the Oampagna being a favorite
jungle. How science turns the flank of all our
ideas ! Romulus and Remus become painfully
modern.
In Queensland, recently, a country domes-
tic, having seen her mistress write out a cheque,
asked if that was all that was necessary to obtain
money from the bank, and was told it was. A
few days later the ingenuous damsel abstracted a
form from the cheque-book, filled it up for £5 55.,
attached a signature, and presented the document,
undated, at the bank. She was, of course, de-
tained ; but, at the police-court, discharged, as
having been unaware she was committing any
offence.
Auckland (M.L.) has the same old garbage
tip—‘the tip is a chronic trouble in these regions—
and it has just gained the additional knowledge
that large numbers of pigs are habitually fattened
on the gruesome mound. Which recalls that one
of the great wants of every Australian city is a
place where veritable dairy-fed pork—pork that
has never tasted offal—can be bought. For such
an establishment, run with absolute honesty by
men in whom the public believe, there is a fortune
in every capital.

Kruger and His Thumb.
CHARACTERISTIC STORY OF OOM PAUL
The Boer President is universally regarded as a
“tough nut” to crack, and even Britain may
well exercise caution in dealing with a man of the
iron will exhibited in a story told by a contem-
porary. One day whilst shooting, the Boer Presi-
dent had part of his thumb blown off. The sur-
geon advised amputation of the arm, but Kruger
said no. Then he suggested the hand should be
removed, but as Kruger still said no, he threw up
the case. The patient thereupon sharpened his
knife, and with spartan courage cut off the top of
his thumb, and the wound healed. Thus his hand
was spared. Although operations often prolong
life they are sometimes ill advised. Another in-
stance is that of Mrs. Lois Stratford. Mrs.
Stratford resides at Millicent, and is only 20.
When asked for information she said : “For three
years 1 suffered srreatly from internal troubles.
The doctor said I would have to go under an
operation. I would not consent to this although
I suffered greatly from headaches, weakness, loss
of appetite, dizziness and sickness. I noticed Dr.
Williams’pink pills advertised and I decided to
try them. After taking four boxes a great im-
provement was noticeable, and six boxes entirely
cured me. I am now bright and active, able to
undertake my household duties without distress,
and my improved appearance is further, pleasing
evidence of my recovery. J can walk ten miles a
day now without any trouble.”
Mrs. Stratford did well in not consenting to an
operation, for Dr. Williams’ pink pills cured her
pleasantly and completely because they act upon
the cause of disease, removing its foundations, en-
riching the blood, and retoning the nerves. Thus
they cure anaemia, debility, liver troubles, sleep-
lessness, heart palpitation, consumption, bronchitis,
coughs and colds, erysipelas, rickets, St. Vitus'
dance, paralysis, locomotor ataxia, etc. Sold by
the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place,

IT WAS NOT THE CLIMATE.
“I have been well as to my general health so
wrote the late Dr. 0. W. Holmes a short time
before his death —but have had a good deal ot
asthma The climate is cold and rough, but I have
found much that is delightful about my residence
here in Boston (America). Perhaps, after alb tho
fault is not so much in latitude 42 as in age 82.
That was it. The wise thinker did not deceive
himself. He knew that it is the wasting muscles
that complain of the steepness of the hills and the
thinning blood that fancies the winters are colder
t han they used to be. r , ,
And it was not because Mrs. Amy Harvey had
lost interest in her domestic affairs that she was
led to say everything was a trouble and a burden.
Xo; it was because she had lost her strength.
Whether a duty is a burden or not depends on the
back which has to carry it. Ah, dear me, how
constantly experience enforces that lesson upon us.
Then what keen concern we feel in the answer
to the question : What will strengthen quivering
limbs ? What will restore the vigour of the mind ?
“All my life,” says Mrs. Harvey, “I have
suffered off and on from indigestion and weakness.
I felt low and languid, with a disagreeable,
sinking sensation after taking my meals. I had no
energy ; everything was a trouble and a burden.
As soon as I had partaken of food, no matter how
light, I felt a fullness and a gnawing pain at my
chest, as if something were tearing it.
“ I also suffered much from nervous headache
and was often dizzy. My breathing was short, and
and if I exerted myself I was out of breath. As
time went on I got extremely weak and could not
attend to my work.
“ I saw doctor after doctor and was told by them
that my ailment was owing to my food not
digesting.”
[The doctors were right. That was the trouble,
and the only one. Yet, instead of being a com-
parative trifle, as many shallow persons say, it is
the most serious of all diseases. Of course some of
her food digested more or less fully, otherwise she
could not have lived a fortnight. But the nourish-
ment was never enough for the wants of the body.
Hence the nervous headaches, the asthma, the
sinking sensation, the dizziness, the gnawing pain,
and the disabling weakness. The body under
these conditions is like a house abandoned to
decay. All gone to raqk and ruin.]
“ I took the doctors’ medicines,” continues this
lady’s plain account, “but my case was an obstinate
one, and none of them seemed of any avail. •
“In May, 1891, a neighbour told me of the
benefit she had derived from Mother Seigel’s
Syrup. I got a bottle from Messrs. W. Fox and
Sons, Chemists, Bethnal Green, and when I had
taken it two days I felt much relief. I continued
taking it, and could eat and enjoy my food without
having pain. I now gained strength and felt
brighter than I had ever done in my life.
“ Since then I have kept well, taking a dose or
two of Mother Seigel’s Syrup when I need it; and
I find it always puts me right. You can publish
this statement if you think fit. (Signed) (Mrs.)
Amy Harvey, 19, ‘J’ Block, Dufferin Street, Bun-
hill Row, London, June 26th, 1896.”
“In March, 1894,” writes another, “I had a
severe attack of influenza which left me extremely
weak. I was troubled witli indigestion and want
of appetite. Having read of the wonderful cures
made by Mother Seigel’s Syrup I decided to try it.
I got a bottle from Mr. Win. Hy. Jones, the
chemist in Caledonian Road. In two days I felt
great benefit. I could eat, and food agreed with
me, causing no pain. In a few days I teas com-
pletely cured and well as ever.
“ Since that time I have kept Mother Seigel’s
Syrup in the house as a family medicine, and it
always relieves when any of us suffer from indiges-
tion, &c. I have pleasure in making this statement
and you can publish it if you wish. (Signed)Geo.
H. Barker, 44 Montpelier Road, Kentish Town,
London, N.W., June 9th, 1896.”
These excellent letters call for no explanation.
Their sincerity and truth is plain on the surface.
And when we remember that what the writers
affirm of Mother Seigel’s Syrup is also declared by
thousands of others, the value of this remedy is
established beyond question.
Concerning “ Bull-Punchers.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Eleven industrious bullocks and a heart-broken,
fence-breaking yellow bull, who has seen better
days, constitute the team. Bully is always placed
in an excellent position in the team, and being an
important personage he gets blamed for every-
thing ; when the waggon bogs up to the axle, the
loving master always makes for him with the up-
raised whip. “ It’s you, yer yellow ! Why
the don’t yer keep over ? ” And so on,
ad lib. And the gentleman who was brought up
in the respectable surroundings of a dairy-herd
has to answer for the lot. The waggon is
always a second-hand one; perhaps the wheels
are new and the body old, or vice verm.
The brake is generally defective (“ever since she
run over th’ noo qpttin’ ”)—on hard, going up-hill
and down-hill —it won’t act at all, and lets the
waggon run away with the bullocks. The tyre on
one of the wheels is always loose, and the team is
stopped every mile or so to tap it on, or peg it up
with an axe. A dog of no particular breed is tied
with a rope under the waggon, and for the first
Eart of the journey always hangs back and yelps ;
y then his feet are half worn away, and he begins
to pull all he knows in the right direction, limping
painfully. A small bay horse, showing plenty of
rib and shoulder-blade, is moored with a huge
chain around his neck to the pole at the rear.
Every time the waggon stops he dozes, and isn’t
awakened until the waggon, starting again, gives
him a tremendous tug. The result of this con-
stant jerking of the primitive towing arrangement
is that the horse’s neck is stretched right out of
Sortion, so that on first sight he looks like a
1 giraffe, and when he grazes he cannot get his
head up level again unless some one lifts it. When
at perfect rest the head hangs across the top-rail
of an obliging fence.
* * * •
“ Your bullocks don’t look too well,” I once re-
marked to a lanky bullock-driver; “how’s the
grass on the road ? ” “Ain’t none !” he drawled.
“ Was goin’ to bring some corn-’usks for feed, but
left ’em b’ind. I’ll bo ’ome, any’ow, arfter ’mor-
row ; they’ll get w’ips then.” A big roan bullock
in the load hero turned round, and as much as
said (by his looks), “Oh, what a thundering he !”
“ Hurry up, now, and get the corn horf 1 ” said
another to me, one very hot day. “I got to got
back to the Black Water’oles (12 miles out) to give
’em a drink. Aint ’ad any since dinner-time yes-
terday ! ” “Why,” I replied, “take your team
up the road here 200 yards, and they can drink
the Hunter dry, if they like.” “Too far;
I’m in a ’urry ! ” And, when unloaded, he went
pubwards, leaving his parching bullocks standing
in the yard till near dusk, when ho started for the
Holes. This sort of teamster (and there are scores
of him) ought to be toiling on a treadmill.
• • • •
The bed and tucker-box always accompany the
bulloeky, and when the waggon is being unloaded
they are roughly thrown overboard. The above-
mentioned dog takes charge of the former, and the
latter generally manages to get in front of one of
the wheels, so that, when the bullocks make a
move on their own responsibility, the box and
contents are badly crushed and battered. And
as the blue-faced owner scrapes the damper, beef,
and home-made jam off the wheel and tries to
straighten his billy and frying-pan, his tongue
remains paralysed for the time, on account of the
strength of language that he is unable to give off.
Remonstrate with a bullock-driver for cruelty
to his wretched animals, and you will always get) ,
the same drawling answer —“ That’s all right,
mister 1 These blauky bullocks is mine! ”

The Case of
MR. JAMES STEPHEN POTTER
(by our special reporter )
In consequence of information received
the other morning, our reporter drove to
Mount Joy-street, South Brisbane. It did
not take long to find Mr. James Stephen
Potter, who is we 1 and favourably known
in that portion of Queensland’s capital city.
Sitting under the front \erandah of the
house, the following conversation ensued,
and was faithfully taken down in the
newspaper man’s note look.
“You have been ill, I am informed?”
was the first question.
“Yes,” replied Mr. Potter; “I suffered
from indigestion and a wasting away of my
flesh. I couldn’t manage my meals, and it
has always been a bad sign with me when
my appetite goes off. A most unpleasant
and bitter taste was always on my tongue
and the roof of my mouth. I had a dull
feeling in.thehead, and,generally speaking,
t was all cut of order, and when I went
up to be examined by the doctor for the
friendly society, of which I wished to
become a member, that medical man refused
to me. The nature of my employment
causes me considsrable confinement, And I
thought, perhaps, want of exercise was the
cause of my illness. I now started with
dumb-bell exercise, and persisted in that for
some time, but all this was a 3 useless as the
physio I took. I began to fall away in
flesh. I couldn’t sleep at night, but lay
in bed awake three partffof my time longing
for the morning light, and when tho day
returned I felt dull, depressed, and weary,
and quite unfit to tackle the duties of the
day.”
“ How long did all this last ? ”
“ Oh, for some weeks. I was getting
about tired of it. I grew weaker aud
thinner all the time.”
“ What were the doctor’s orders ? ”
“ The doctor’s orders were to take Cle-
ments Tonic, and 1 only wish I had known
about the recuperative powers of that
remedy when I first fell ill Not to take op

purchased some Clemeuts Tonic, and at
about the same time I read an account of

in the newspaper paragraph were very
similar to those that affected me. I

This I now found was giving way

Then as to appetite. Whereas once I could

quite hungry to my meals. My food now
had quite a different taste', and was no
longer a weight upon my chest making me
heavy, tired, and drowsy in the day time,
but nourished me and gave me strength and
vitality.”

Once more I slept a wholesome, dreamless

strikes Melbourne, it is long odds on his Ministry

Agent-General Reeves writes to his Maori-
land friends that the English newspapers are full
of the grossest exaggerations concerning the war.
Has he seen any of the Australian papers, whose
earners send the cream of the exaggerations and
none of the occasional unvarnished facts appearing
in the London press ?
S.A. Leg. Council elections promise to be
very dull in Kingston’s absence. The ex-Pre-
mier meant to make those contests a battle-ground
for Household {suffrage, but the campaign is, so
far, utterly spiritless. Kingston’s manifesto is
now on the way out, and it is to be hoped there is
gall m it, for the sake of old times.
S.A. goes on extending its system of
homestead blocks—the plan under which working
men may make homes and part incomes for them-
selves near the larger centres of population. A
portion of the Angas Estate, just outside Adelaide,
has lately been bought at public auction, and now
the Department is bargaining for some of the pick
of the wonderfully rich south-eastern potato land.
As regards the weird decision of Mel-
bourne City Council to reject a German tender at
£17,000 for electric-lighting machinery, and to
accept a British tender of £22,000 (by way of
showing that the Council approves of the war in
South Africa)_ —how will it be should it turn out
that the English firm which gets the contract is
strongly pro-Boer ? The Melb. Council, in its
haste to throw away £5OOO of other people’s money
to show its personal Jingoism, never stopped to in-
quire whether the tenderers were Jingo or not.
Meanwhile if the Queen, who is to all intents and
purposes wholly German, hears of this indirect
aspersion on her countrymen, she will doubtless
be pleased.

WIFEUS: “ Oh! you monster. Bo you put my correct age on the tombstone !”
A very dull member of N.S.W. Parliament,
Mr. David Davis, who has been drawing his Par-
hamentary salary during the recess of the past
five months, will not alone continue to draw it in
this holiday fashion, but has just started on a six.
months leave of absence from the Water and
Sewerage Board, the salary as a member of which
he will, in similar holiday fashion, pleasantly
pocket.
Martin Basedow, editor of the locally-
influential S. A. German newspaper, drops out of
Parliament after 24 years. His Zeitun.g occasion-
ally printed the opinions of well-informed Ger-
mans about the Jingo business in South Africa,
and this the local Jingoes resented with all their
teeth and claws. So Basedow, instead of allowing
the Leg. Council voters to defeat him, withdrew
his candidature.

that great and truthful military expert, the cable,
it is now the Boer women who are responsible for
certain unspecified atrocities perpetrated upon
unknown wounded soldiers in unmentioned places
° n jj f battle.” This is probably in-
tended to bring peace of mind to those weaklings
who recoil at the idea of the enemies’ women being
disembowelled per bayonet in the trenches.
