Although South Australia has been a con-
firmed Protectionist province ever since 1887
when the present tariff was imposed, it is likely
that four of its six Federal House of Representa-
tives members will be Freetraders. These are
Premier Holder, President Chaffey Baker, Paddy
Glynn, and Symon, Q.C. The wobblesome Play-
foid once coquetted with Freetrade, but, as he is
the father of the 1887 tariff, it is presumed that he
is still on that side of the fence.
A question for the politicians who con-
template anti-indecency legislation: Is it or is it
not as demoralising to the community for a news-
paper to print extended detailed reports of the
proceedings in incest, abortion and rape cases as
for the same newspaper to print advertisements of
remedies for the cure of certain diseases ? And,
if it isn t, why isn’t it ? In a word, is it possible
for a newspaper to print acres of beastliness
(always a safe draw) for the public good ?
In 1899, according to Coghlan, the best
increase of population was in Tasmania (2‘BB per
cent.) ; Queensland (2.31 per cent.) was second;
Maoriland (1*75 per cent.) third ; Westralia (172
per cent.) fourth; and after these four Protection-
ist provinces came N.S.W. (171 per cent.).
South Australia and Victoria brought up the rear,
so the solitary Foreign-trade province only
managed to get fifth place among seven. Taking
the increase of population for the last eight years,
Westralia has the largest percentage, Tasmania is
second, Queensland, third, Maoriland fourth,
N.S.W. fifth, S.A. sixth, and Victoria seventh.
The Sydney Labor light—one of the
M'Laurin, Dibbs, Want and Co. anti-Federal
spouters—who argues in Sydney D.T. that “ bar-
rister ” (which he wasn’t) A. G. Taylor’s success-
ful appeal to the Privy Council proves the neces-
sity of continuing that tribunal’s present relation
to Australia, forgets that N.S.W. Supreme Court
had previously found in favor of Taylor. It was
against the N.S.W. Supreme Court decree that
that appeal to the P. C. was made. The existence
of the Privy Council as an appeal court simply
retarded for a very long time the justice that
Taylor would otherwise have got almost imme-
diately.
Since Kingston’s Household Suffrage
scheme was beaten last Dec., S.A. has had three
new proposals. Solomon’s £2O franchise scheme
was promptly thrown out in the Assembly, and the
Holder Government then proposed a £l5 scheme
which the Tory Council rejected. Now Holder
goes for enfranchising man and wife occupying a
two-roomed house, an obvious sop for the country
constituencies.. Meantime, Kingston is certain to
stick to his original scheme (his manifesto is on
the vvay out) and the Liberal party is in danger
therefore, of being split into two camps at the
approaching Council elections.
If N.S.W. Govt, does not promptly table
resolutions reducing the number and emoluments
of members of the Leg. Assembly, several private
members are prepared to spring definite motions
on the matter. One motion likely to receive con-
siderable support proposes to reduce the members
to 60 at £2OO a year, and the Ministers to five,
one (the Premier) to receive £lOOO, and four £BOO.
The salary reductions will be claimed on the
ground that members and Ministers are almost all
holders of other profitable occupations. And yet
while an assurance office gives its manager
£2OOO or £3OOO a year for life to manage the con-
cerns of a few thousand policy-holders, what folly
it is to expect a really good man to give his life to
politics on the chance of being allowed for a few
years to manage even the local affairs of 1| million
people for £lOOO a year, with the prospect of sack
any moment !
Launceston Examiner (Tasmania) thinks
it sees a beautiful and simple way out. When the
British Government has amended clause 74
(the Privy Council appeal clause) of the Com-
monwealth Constitution, instead of sub-
mitting the whole amended bill to the
people, it says, let that one amended
clause be submitted to the people.
Which is much the same as suggesting
that Fysh’s head and feet should stand
for Parliament separately so that the
people could elect one end of him if
thej’ wanted to, and reject the other
end. If the people reject the Bill
because the new clause is in it the
Bill is Lost. And if they reject
the new clause separately the
Bill is Lost also, for the trouble
can only arise if the British
Government refuses to have
the Bill without the new clause.
If Fysh’sbody and legs are made
life-members of Parliament and
only his. head has to go for re-
election it lqay make things look
easier, but if Fysh’s head is
rejected he will be just as much
out as if he was all rejected.
Sydney is full of back
lanes that belong to nobody,
are cleaned by nobody, and
for which nobody is res-
ponsible. A builder made a
lane or narrow street to give
access to some property. He
madeitanyhowhepleased. Then
the City Council refused to take
it over or recognise it, because it
wasn’t made the right
way. The result is that
it is no man’s land, and
its cleansing is as ut-
terly neglected and ig-
nored as if it was in
Timbuctoo. One much-
needed reform is a law
compelling the councils
—city and suburban—
to take over these neg-
lected byways, and
pving them power to charge the adjacent land-
lords a lump sum (to be fixed in the Act and
proportioned to the amount of lane taken over)
for extra responsibility.
S.M. Herald is—for it —severe on Chief
Justice Darley
We are reluctantly compelled to place on record our
profession of regret that servants of the State in this
country should have used servants of the State in London
as a medium through which to oppose the Commonwealth
Bill, embodying as it does the verdict of a double referen-
dum in four of the federating colonies, the declared
judgment of 'the Parliaments of those colonies, and the
direct instructions of the Premiers to the delegates sent
to Westminister to watch the interests of the Common-
wealth Bill.
“ Servants of this country ! ” Bah ! They
are, nearly all of them, servants of that
country. Australia pays them, but Australia
be hanged !
London Times is of opinion, it is cabled,
that if the right of appeal to the British Privy
Council is abolished it will retard the “invest-
ment of British capital in Australia.” And The
Bulletin says, “Thank God!” Also still louder,
“ Thanh God !” Likewise it gets on the housetop
and cries at the top of its voice, “ Thank God ! ”
’ ’he investment of British capital in this country
, "is made it a wretchedly embarrassed place where
almost every paving-stone, wharf, public building,
every inch of railway, every public tank, dam
and waterhole, every pier, harbor, breakwater,
dredge, locomotive, railway-car, tram-car, road,
telegraph-post, almost every inch of telegraph and
telephone wire, every gaol, lockup, big gun, small
gun, cartridge, fortification, torpedo, benevolent
asylum, pauper asylum, lunatic asylum, and every-
thing else the State has built or bought, is mort-
gaged for every penny of its original value to the
British money-lender. Even ordinary wear and
tear haven’t been written off out of revenue. A
country can no more grow rich by importing •
money from England than a private citizen can by
importiug money from Aaronstein, the money-
lender. If it is true that the abolition of appeals
to the Privy Council will discourage the import-
ation of debt and embarrassment into Australia
then they should be abolished at any cost short of
gory war.
Many “a so-called “ abuse of the white
flag,” on the Boer side as on the British,
is doubtless accidental. A firing-line may be
extended for a mile or more ; and soldiers
at one end may easily be unaware that a white
flag is hoisted at the other. In any event, the
white flag is used by all armies as a means to
trick the enemy ; and the rules regarding it are
by no means settled. Here is what Lord Wol-
seley, Commander-in-chief of the British army,
says on page 300 of “'The'Soldier’s Pocket-Book ”
for the instruction of the English officer who has
to go under the white flag to the enemy—
After he has fulfilled his mission he must return with-
out delay to the general by whom he has been sent,
unless one of the objects for which he was sent is the
gaining of time—for instance, to receive reinforcements
during a fight, in which case, of course, the longer he can
loiter, by, for example, dismounting to search for an
imaginary stone in his horse’s shoe, the better it is.
In the same book the man carrying the white flag
is advised that he may reckon on the chance of
being fired on, because it is very difficult for
the enemy to cease firing at once. And' pre-
sumably Wolseley knows something about the
matter.

pose of making them “come out in the form of
snakes in 48 hours.” Then came the alleged
attempt to rob Annie of her innocence. Annie
sobbed hard while telling the end of her story, in
spite of which lawyer Gaunson subjected her sim-
plicity to a severe cross-examination, and brought
witnesses to swear that the Wilkins’ domicile was
visited by many male persons who used much bad
language. Finally the birth-mark maker from
Westralia was committed for trial on light bail.
Nervousness.
Every nervous person drags down the health of
the other members of the family—nervousness is
contagious.
This season of the year finds women tired beyond
belief.
■ Their nerves have suffered. They are neither
good company for themselves nor for their friends.
Those who are wise are taking the most respon-
sible of all remedies, are getting back their
nervous vigor, their proper weight, and healthy
color. Following the advice of eminent physicians
everywhere. Dr. Robert Shaw, L.S.D., M. 8.,
Rockhampton, gives a strong verdict in favor of
Warner’s Safe Cure, which he has used in pre-
scribing for kidney and liver disease. He says:
“ I have much pleasure in stating that I pre-
scribed Warner’s Safe Cure very frequently whilst
acting as medical officer on the Caims-Hebarton
Railway Contract, and obtained more satisfactory
results from it than any formulae of my own pre-
scribing.”
The best physicians openly endorse and recom-
mend Warner’s Safe Cure, authorising a public
use of their statements that Warner’s Safe Cure,
in case after case, cures biliousness, nervous dys-
pepsia, purifies the blood, and regulates the
stomach, liver and kidneys, and rejuvenates the
fagged-out or diseased system when everything
else fails.*

Free Speech and a Free Fight.
“C.A.8.,” from London to The Bulletin, re-
curding Frae Speech, Free England, and a Free
Fight:—
The first of these quantities is “ non est.” Of the exist-
ence of the second much reasonable doubt might now be
entertained. But the third, on the night of March 2nd,
in _ that .part of the Strand opposite the sacred pre-
cincts of Exeter Hall, was a fact patent to all beholders.
In the afternoon a meeting nad been held of those
opposed to the present war, very unpopular views at the
present moment, but views incontestably held by a con-
siderable proportion of thinking men in England, Herbert
Spencer amongst them. However, the mob, which never
thinks, holds the view that the Boer must be exter-
minated and the Republics annexed to the British
Empire. Now, the mob on this occasion had not
recovered frem the effects of the night before, when it
had hqwled and boohooed till 4 a.m., not only (as was
natural) over the relief of Ladysmith, but also over the
stupendous fact that England with 40,000 men could
actually beat the Boers with 4000. The said mob was
going its way along the Strand on the said evening, at
about 6 o’clock, when behold 1 a flaming placard of the
Sun newspaper, “ Boers at Exeter Hall. Disgraceful
Speeches.”
Away ran the mob in the direction of Exeter Hall,
swelling with patriotic fury. Up the stairs they went,
getting thicker and thicker every moment—a true Lon-
don mob, brutalised, maddened, a deplorable sight in-
deed,-the cumulative result of centuries of Conservatism.
Now the mob was drunk, doubly drunk, although alco-
holic liquor had not had much to do with it this time. It
was-drunk with the news of victory—small blame to it —
and drunk with that elevating sentiment of malignant
hate of the Boers so persistently instilled into it not only
by the Press but also by the Pulpit. And so the drunken
mob stormed gradually up the stairs of Exeter Hall, until
Borne dozen of them forced their way into the meeting.
The Union Jack waved aloft, and we heard the news that
Britons never would be slaves, and how Britannia rules
the waves. But the organisers of the meeting had been
before them, and provided a body of volunteer chuekers-
out, distinguished by a small badge from the rest of the
audience. And the chuckers-out did chuck out with a
vengeance. Down the stairs the intruders were pitched
head-foremost, and cracked over the head with anything
that came handy. Their Union Jack, by-the-bye, at
• present adorns the walls of one of the London peace
committees.
The rowdies being ejected, the chuckers-out left the
hall,' and with the stewards of Exeter Hall locked the
doors, and fought the ever-increasing mob outside. The
patriots stormed first one door and then the other, but at
neither could they gain admission, the doors of Exeter
Hall being very stout and almost impossible to break in.
However, they banged and thumped away at the main
doors and on the walls —which pleased them and did no-
body any harm. The meeting numbered some two or
three thousand, half of whom were women, while several
thousands more were in the surging crowd below, making
fruitless efforts to force a passage.
Through the thick bolted doors, as an accompanime 2.
the speeches, came the dull sound of the mob booing
and cheering in the Strand below. Every now and then
the noise grew louder, “ 800-00-00-00-00. Rule, rule
Britannia, &c. 800-00-00-00-oo.” The speakers at the
meeting, however, paid little attention to these convincing
arguments in favor of war, and continued their speeches
unruffled. Someone presently slipped in and begged for a
glassofwater. Some was given him from the table on
the platform. Blood was flowing freely outside, and the
water was wanted for one of the wounded Heads were
cut open, faces scratched, clothes torn, buttons sent the
way of those on the bodice which encased Peoottv’s
plump person ; hats were bashed in, and the scene was
an instructive reminder of the civil and refining influences
of English education and the deep love of the English
people for the ancient constitutional liberties of their
native land. .
At last 40 police had arrived, and the proceedings were
carried out to the very end without any more interrup-
tion than came from the harmless bellowing of the crowd
in the Strand. On the stairs, however, there was a sight
to behold. The fight was one of the most serious ever
known at a London meeting, and the blood trickled from
many smashed faces. After the meeting some of the
speakers (Sir Wilfrid Lawson among them) escaped by a
side-door. By that time the 40 stout policemen had
forcibly kicked out the crowd, and there was at all
events internal order, although the Strand was almost
blocked till some time afterwards.
The scene suggested many reflections. What an unre-
deemed brute is the Londoner when once his brute side is
played upon. Should misfortune ever overtake England,
and revolution come, are there not in all this seething
mass of degraded humanity elements as evil as those which
caused the Reign of Terror in France ?

CHEAP AND FASHIONABLE TAILORING.
CG. HATTE, of King-street, Newtown (near Sydney,
• N.S.W.), has recently made great additions and im-
provements to his Tailoring Department, which is now one
of the features of his extensive business. He receives regu-
lar shipments, from his Home buyers, of all the newest and
most fashionable goods, and has one of the smartest cutters
in the colony, Everything is made to measure on the pre-
mises; a perfect fit is guaranteed, and patrons may rely on
every satisfaction being given. Self-measurement forms
and patterns sent free by post. Hatte’ssuits of Indigo serge,
all-wool tweeds, and sergeretteslwith extra pair of trousers
given in) for 42/- are the marvel of tailoring and the talk of
Australasia. For 50/- Hatte makes a suit of tweed or serge
fit for a prince. Inspect or send for patterns of his new
woollens, comprising vicunas, West of England, Scotch and
Blarney tweeds, at 57/6, and 65/- ; the great value offered.
Hatte makes a smart Chesterfield overcoat from meltons,
vicunas, and Venetians from 45/-, and the hew paddock
coat, with full back, side vents, crescent pockets, silk-faced
and velvet collar for 55/-. Hatte’s speciality is in dress-
suits, starting at £3 3s, made of purple dye elastic coating
with silk-facing. For excellence of material, perfect fit,
latest fashion, and superior workmanship, Hatte is un-
equalled, whilst his charges are at least 30 per cent, under
those of ordinary city tailors.

Attorney-General Wise in Reply.
The Editor of The Bulletin. Sir,—l have so
much respect for the political criticism of The
Bulletin, because, however one may disagree with
it, it is generally founded on some clear principle,
that 1 do not like to pass without notice your
trenchant remarks about myself in last week’s
leader. You accuse me in this of a complete
change of opinion with regard to the relations
between Australia and Great Britain, and justify
the charge by very short extracts from an article I
wrote for MacMillan’s Magazine in Jan., 1888.
Now, so far am I from admitting this charge to be
correct, that I accept that article as my justification
for my conduct as a Minister in relation to the
dispatch of troops to S. Africa. The writer of the
leader has, by selecting two extracts apart from
the context, misrepresented what I wrote, as
I will proceed to prove.
. The tenor of the article is indicated by its
title, “A menace to National Unity and its
aim is to show the danger of causing dissension
between Great Britain and Australia by premature
schemes of Union.
All that is desired l say, “is to criticise from
the standpoint of a Unionist the ill-advised action
of some who believe themselves the friends of Union.”
I claim to regard affairs to-day from the same
standpoint; and to be now as I was ten years
ago, a supporter of union between Great Britain
and Australia. .1 then proceeded in the article to
give what I still believe to be solid and material
reasons for desiring union, the chief of which is
our geographical position, which connects us com-
mercially with China and the East, where, unless
England is predominant, our interests must suffer.
I next referred to the Soudan expedition, which
I said in words which I accept to-day—
Placed us at the point where those roads part which
lead, the one to isolation, the other to union ; and it will
need all the self-restraint and resolution of the political
leaders both of England and the colonies to prevent our
being forced upon the barren and ungrateful path. ...
The Soudan expedition forced us prematurely to the
front, and caused an uneasy questioning of our relations
with the rest of the empire.
Thinking as I then did that the time was not ripe
for settling the questions which this expedition
raised, and believing also that the occasion had
not required any offer of troops from Australia, I
urged that
organic questions (of politics) ought not to be lightly
raised. They ought not to be discussed in the market-
place until they are ripe for a solution. Imperial Federn-
tion is emphatically one of those questions which ought
not to be dragged prematurely on the platform. The tie
between England and her colonies is of such a light and
almost imperceptible character that it ought not to be
exposed to constant strain. The tie is strengthening 1 year
by year and if a sudden strain should come upon it there
is little doubt but that it would hold.
Then followed a warning against making Union
a cry of an English party, and these words of advice.
What then is the course which a critic who is not
unfriendly would recommend to the Imperial Federation-
lsts? It is not an attractive course, nor will it readily
recommend itself to editors who love sensation. [Mr.
Stead was then showing signs of starting on the war-path
on this question] or to public speakers who enjoy large
phrases. It is this: —“ Watch and wait; inform the people
about these colonies ; teach them our resources ; let the
best of them come to us ; educate them ; but do not, as
you value Union, talk about our political connection until
you are ready with a practical suggestion for making it
stronger.”
Then I added some remarks about the evils of
dependence—remarks which I still believe to be
true, and in this connection used the phrase which
you quote in your leader—
Union with England we gladly believe will co ne, but it
must be the union of an equal with an equal, and not the
union of a superior with a subordinate ; so that it is no
paradox to say that the first step towards union must be
separation.
The quotation continues with words your writer
omitted from his article, as follows:
We are manifestly not yet ready to take that step-, and
a public man would be guilty of little short of treason if
he were to advocate it. . . . Every year sees us approach
more nearly to a Federal union ; and every year also will
see, unless some inconsiderate pedantry should bring our
destinies within the range of English party politics, a
growing attachment towards England, founded* upon a.
higher sense of public duty and a more judicial perception
of national interest.
I concluded by quoting from Sir Henry Parkes’
speech in the Legislative Assembly;, of Nov. 25th,
1887, a well-known passage, in which he looked
forward to a great union of the British Empire,
constituted by federated groups.
Now, sir, my contention is that neither in senti-
ment nor word does this article conflict with my
present political action. I believe that the pre-
sent war, whatever judgment we may pass upon
the diplomacy which caused its outbreak, is
emphatically a war for British supremacy ; and as*
such, I urge that Australia does well to bear a
hand in it. Ido not regard this as “doing
Britain’s bidding,” but as a voluntary act of our
own, dictated by the highest form of self-interest,
viz., loyal duty to a high ideal. Those who are
enemies of England and wish her ill will naturally
not take this view ; but I claim that all who, like
myself, have always believed that Australia finds
her strength in Union with England, will rejoice
in a movement which has made that Union closer'
by proving to ourselves and to the world that we
are ready to enter upon that “ Union of an equal
ivithanequal” whichl hoped forinlBBB. I certainly
did not anticipate at that time that in so short a
period as 12 years the then prevalent indifference
on the part of Great Britain and suspicion on ours
would have given place to that mutual confidence
and understanding which is now so significant of
the relations between the two countries. A Feder-
rated Australia able to protect herself, and ready
to play her proper part in the protection of the'
British Empire, by guarding British interests in.
the East, may be in a position, far sooner than
anyone had dared to hope, to make that alliance
with the other free communities of the British
Empire, which was the political ideal of Sir Henry
Parkes, from which you accuse me of having de-
parted.
B. R. Wise.
[Mr. Wise asserts that his critic has misrepresented!
him ; but why does Mr. Wise misrepresent himself f
He received full credit for those Imperialistic aspira-
tions, of which he speaks with not unbecoming pride.
Mr. Wise, it was pointed out, “ believed in alliance-
with England in 1888, aa he does now.” But he be-
lieved then that the first step towards a perfect alliance-
was a complete separation —as he does now ? It is, then,
with the object of separating Australia from Britain
with the object of attaining that independence which, a*
he points out, is on Australia’s part essential and must be-
antecedent to Imperial Federation—that Mr. Wise, as a.
member of the Lyne cabinet, places upon Australia’s-
shoulders the responsibility of Imperial war. That is, in
order to secure the independence of Australia, Mr. Wise
advocates that course of action which, above all others, is
calculated to prove Australia’s dependence —for how can
dependence be better shown than by the payment of a
war-tribute of money and men towards a war in which
Australia, like an ancient satrapy, has no voice?
And in order to secure the separation of Australia
from England, Mr. Wise approves that course of
action which, as Imperial Federationists declare, has done
more than anything else to tighten the existing bonds. Mr.
Wise s continued belief in the need for the separation and
independence of Australia is, in fact, proved by the zeal
with which he promotes a policy calculated to bring
atont the exact opposite. And the person who proves
that Mr. Wise goes down in order to go up, goes back-
ward with the sole aim of going forward, is—Mr. Wise.

Kensington (Sydney) got a day in with the
ponies lost Saturday, there being no metropolitan horse-
meeting on that date. A hig sprinkling of the usual
‘ horse ” people were in evidence, but thev found it just
as hard to pick the little ’uns as the big ’uns. Two even-
money favorites in Flower Girl and Selina, and one odds-
on, Minimus, got home ; but the other three events must
more than have swallowed up the short-odds winnings,
so poor Ikey got out of the crush all right.
* * *• *
A common dodge on the part of Sydney pony
jockeys is to squeeze in next the rails near the finish of a
race, and if defeated, enter a protest for interference.
* * *
The eminent and clever Mr. “Jack” Brewer
has, after waiting patiently in England, got his knife in
under their fifth rib—per medium of The Grafter’s victory
in the City and Suburban. Sievier-Sutton (“Bob ”Sutton,
the cash bookmaker, now connected by marriage with the
pure-merino aristocracy) is said to have won £20,000 on
the race, and the nosey gelding has been backed exten-
sively for the Jubilee Handicap, to be run May 12. “ Most
•Of the Australians in England backed Syerla, the property
•of Humphrey Oxenham, as they believed the reports
about The Grafter being ill. Mr. Oxenham’s horses,
Syerla and Sailor Boy, have since been removed from Mr.
J. E. Brewer’s to Mr. Halsey’s stables.” The English
sporting papers were unanimously of opinion that Brewer
•could neither ride nor manage horses ; and it is cabled
that the Times remarks that The Grafter’s recent running
■was somewhat peculiar.
• * • •
/' Gun ” : “ Bob Sutton ” (Robert Sievier)
•originated cash-betting in Australia many years ago,
and had several ups and downs. Two or three times he
•started operations at Rand wick and Flemington with an
•empty bag, and pulled through ail right. Returning to
England, his good looks and fetching style landed him in
■clover, for he married the Marquis of Aylesbury's sister,
and jumped into lasting affluence apparently. * His offer
recently to raise a company of yeomanry for service in
South Africa, was “ declined with thanks” by the British
War authorities. “ Bob ” must have gathered in that
twenty thou, as a backer, for he is hardly likely to have
slung the “ Tommy ” round his neck again.
* * » *
The Grafter went away well-endorsed with Mel-
bourne Cup (9.2 up), 0. B. Fisher Plate, and Flying Stakes
(wherein he accounted for Bobadil at weight-for-age) as
recent achievements. Good judges expected him to leave
bis mark in one or two big races, but reports that trainer
Brewer was full-up of the old country, and the fact that
The Grafter’s owner had returned to Australia made hope
very sick. The nag’s best previous performance in Eng-
land was fifth in Cambridgeshire Stakes, where he carried
8.3. His first impost was 9.7 in Epsom Plate, then came
'9.12, 8.10, 8.11, 8.3, and 8.8 in successive events. Second
nag (Innocence) got home twice in eight starts last season,
and third horse (Goblet.) once in four.
* • * , •
Ten-year-old gelding Swagman—winner of Dis-
posal Stakes at Wagga (N.S.W.) Saturday— has had a
chequered career. Six years ago he won in and around
Sydney, and has since given Victoria, Westralia, India,
and South Africa a turn, gathering in money all the time.
He was hamstrung at Wodonga (Vic.) two years back,
but some clever vet. fixed him up.
• * * •
Career of Bananaland champion nag— the Malua-
Malpra gelding, Malula—shows the luck of the game, or
what a change of trainers may do. Recently, MaJula
•easily beat a good field in fast time, and won the April
Handicap—a mile and a distance—with 10.2 up. Though
racing about Brisbane for a long while, Malula had little
•or no success. Always regarded as a good hut unlucky
nag, he evidenced a remarkable weakness for running
■second or third, hut was rarely first past the post. Per-
haps only one win was notched by hhn until he became
the property of Mr. Tatty, at an outlay of £55. The nag
was placed under Tom O’Brien’s care, and a week later he
appropriated the Corinthian, at Taft.'s January meeting.
•Since then it has been pretty well all win, as in nine starts
be scored eight firsts and a second.

the A.J.C. Plate, has only been done Under 6.40 on 10
•different occasions. Carbine’s third victory was achieved
in 5.27 mainly because Mr. M’Laughlin’s nag, Oorreze, set
him a go.
• * * *
The Sydney dailies often make stereotype-men-
tion of the satisfactory arrangements at Randwick. But
without referring to the meagre provision made there, in
comparison to Flemington (Vic.), for patrons of the flat,
it may be pointed out that a practical deception is played
upon everyone who pays Is. for a racebook, expecting same
to be thoroughly reliable. To show how far it isn’t so,
take the following : “ Easter Stakes, Maltster Bst. 71b.,
Hautesse 8.5, Kinglike 8.4.” Misled by these incomplete
extensions, many visitors invested on Maltster and King-
like, and were bewildered when the race was won, hands-
down, by Hautesse. Inquiry showed the actual weights
to be Maltster 9.7, Kinglike 8.9, and Hautesse 7.12.
* * * •
’Ome mail news is that the famous nag Flying
Fox has taken up his quarters at M. Blanc's stud in France!
The owner is game ; he wanted to race his new purchase
in Ascot Gold Oup, and thus risk defeat and consequent
deterioration in stud value. Only ten mares are to be
put to Flying Fox this year. Flying Fox ha* been a
huge gold-mine, and looks like keeping on that way, bar
accidents. He raked in, while racing, over £40,000, and
was sold for £37,000.
* * * *
Leviathan metallieian Humphrey Oxenham,
following in the wake of that other whilom leader of the
Australian betting ring, Joe Thompson, goes ’Ome to
stay if he finds “ fielding ” there to his liking. He was
at once an institution to his brother Mo and the latter’s
natural enemy, the punter ; for, with the bag on, he’d lay
anything “ till the cows came home,” and in big piles,
too, and when he cast the badge of office aside to hack
"something good,” no price, however short, deterred
him. Socially, the nerveful, off-hand “Oxy” was the
mouthpiece of the leather-lunged ; politically, he was
their lobbyist. Did a designing M.P. attempt to lop off
anything from the privileges of Mo, " Oxy ” was moved *
did somebody breathe “ tote ” in the law-making shop of
the country, “ Oxy’s ” “ pull ” silenced him. Ikey is now
in sore straits how to fill the breach ; there’s nobody in
sight within cooey of requirements.

At the Australian record auction-sale of horses
at Alb try last week, 1270 horses were sold for £13,300.
The lemand was entirely on account of the South African
war.
* • * *
Maud S., whose record of 2.8 f stood as the trot-
ting record of the world formany years, is dead. Maud S.
made her record on an oval track, in an old-fashioned
hickory sulky, and many judges of trotting in America
reckon that the old-time mare’s record is equal to the
modern records on kite-shaped tracks in pneumatic
sulkies. Trials have been made by some of the modern
cra..-ks under the old conditions, but they have never been
aulc io beat the Maud S. record.
* * * •
At Onkaparinga (M.L.) races, Easter Monday,
£22,705 10s. went through the tote. Said that the sum
represented over a sov. for each man, woman, and child
on the course. Last year the total was £24,578 ; the year
before, £21,434 ; in 1897, £21,834 1 os. : and, in 1890,
£18,136. There’s no mistake about Onkaparinga meeting
as a one-day affair and the Onkaparinga people as patrons
of the tote.
• • * #
M.L. turfites discuss the advisableness of mak-
ing the re-handicapping system universal. At present, if
a horse with a big impost wins an event after declaration
of weights and incurs a 71b., 101 b., or 141 b. penalty he is
out of court right away ; whereas if no such penalising
condition were attached, the adjuster, in re-handicapping
the horse, would prohahlv only give him a pound or two,
or allow him to stop on his original mark. In the case of
lightly-weighted animals it would be different. Re-han-
dicapping seems to be better than the penalty plan. The
public would see first-class horses, unhampered by an
excessive grand-stand burden, under colors ; and owners,
instead of “ waiting” and running byes in view of a
plum, would likely race their nags out. The two clubs in
the province that have adopted the re-handicapping sys-
tem have found it answer perfectly.

One of the two following camp-fire yams may
or may not. have some truth in it.—S. Q’land, 1887 : “ The
(stolen stallion) Dook of Athol ? Ah ! ’twas me an’ Bilk-
pinched im off the blokes as shook ’im. We ’ad our mark
as ud buy an’ ast no questions, so we ’ooked ’im an’ slipt
’im over the border, an’ got a ’undred : an’ that’s all I’m
goin’ to tell yer.”—Coolgardie, 1895 : “Duke of Athol?
Well, it’s a pretty old affair now, so 1 don’t mind telling
Five of us were in it. Two stole the horse and handed
him over to my brother-in-law, Teddy and myself. Then
they got, and we took the horse to the Liverpool ranges and
planted him on top of a place that was a precipice on
three sides and the fourth closed with a slip-rail. We
camped about 3 miles away, and carried water and grass
up to him. But one day Teddy came back in a sweat.
‘ The troopers are on to the Duke ! I tracked shod horses
up to the rail along the spur. 1 s’pose they are hiding up
there waiting for us.’ ‘ Whew !’ gasped my brother-in-law,
‘ that’s 10 years apiece if we are caught 1’ So we cleared
for Q. and never went back.”
* • • *
“ X.”: The little game some Sydney bruisers
and those pulling the strings are playing threatens to
knock the bottom out of the present big* pug. boom
’Twas ever thus with them. Same old biz has been going
on since the days of Figg. Wrestlers settled their sport
years ago, and rarely get a look-in now. Boxing is
moving that way. Atop of the recent affair between
Tim Hegarty and Billy Williams, and that soft snap
(Broughton) dug out for Oribb, comes the refusal
of Mick Dunn (Sydney) and Fred Preston (Melb.)
to fight at Metropolitan A.O. (Sydney) “ because the
house wasn’t big enough.” The club offered £75 when
the match was made ; “ big head ” prevented acceptance.
Gate totalled £4O, hut that was “too small.” Five
hundred people went home cursing pugs and pugilism
generally. Writer wouldn’t give a shilling to see the
match as such. Preston’s reputation isn’t a good average
one, and Dunn, though 11.4 champion of the mother
province now, when the quality is shoddy, never was a
top-notcher, and has deteriorated a heap. The public
knew all this, so, in the circumstances, £4O was a bit
more than the fight was worth, and more than either will
ever get again in Sydney unless Oribb takes Dunn on,
which is not a remote contingency provided Dunn is
willing.
• • * *
“ Old Timer” : Last week the bruising-boom in
Sydney got another facer, which, following Hegarty-
Williams, Cribb-Broughton, and Dunn-Preston fiascoes,
should go very near compelling prof, pugs to go to work!
as there is little demand for them in other provinces.
Tweedie-Hegarty match proved a farce, and the much-
boomed Hegarty’s stock collapsed like a pricked bladder.
Tweedie’s showing was worse, but nobody expected much
from him ; he’s clever enough for anything—lack of grit
and capacity for fight is the trouble. 1200 people looked
at Hegarty trying to get at Tweedie, and the latter
jigging, ducking, or side-stepping away without an
attempt at responding for nearly an hour and a-half
(20 rounds), and then the thing was declared a draw. The
crowd boohooed and bantered. Tweedie made a speech :
“ Gen’lmen, Mistegarty is de cleverest man in Australia,
and I wasn’t goin’ in ter foight him. 1 made up me mind
ter make a dror of it, and I did.” Hegarty followed suit:
“ • did me best, gen’lmen—Tweedie is a cleverer man
than me, and I was giving away a stone. I’ll fight any
man in Australia at 9.6.” This is all the satisfaction the
crowd got for wasting two hours, and parting with good
coin. It wasn’t an arranged affair—if it had been they’d
have made things livelier. Truth is, Tweedie was scared
by Hegarty*s reputation, and laid himself out to take no
risks, and keep as much out of harm’s way as possible.
Pugs hailing from Melbourne are at a discount in Sydney
now ; they have had a hand in every one of the farces
mentioned at the opening of this par.

Details of fight between N.S.W. pug .Tim Ryan
and Dido Plumb, in London, last month, are to hand.
tV ant of condition beat Ryan ; he had the better of
matters for six rounds but then fell away and was
knocked out in the eighth. Odds of 2tol on him at one
stage. According to reports he has adopted that ugly
Yankee crouch which Australian Tut Ryan and Tim
Hegarty assume and suffer through. The good old Larry
Foley , Peter Jackson, Griffo and Bob Fitzsimmons style of
standing up square and making the most of reach and
height, and putting the weight of the body' into every
punch, is tne best method after all. Because World's
Champion Jeffries and “ Kid” McCoy crouch and win, it
doesn’t follow that everyone else can do likewise; besides,
odds are that each would do even better if he shaped
properly.

Rastlers are chin-wagging again. Harry Dunn,
over in M.L., has responded to llartz Mountain giant
Rennert’s challenge, and lodged £25 for a match from £5O
to £2OO a-side. Rennert is hunting for his backer now.
Mount Morgan (Q.) Wilson also wants a cut at the Teutonic
Hercules, who’ll have his hands full if he takes both on.
Writer hasn’t seen a finer-built athlete in Australia than
Rennert. If he wrestles as well as he looks he’ll settle
them all.

Australia’s rep. (Stan. Rowley) for British
championships and World’s Olympian games at Paris,
next July, left Melbourne, by P. and O. boat Australian,
on Tuesday. He'll figure in races up to 300yds., and can
only start in one (100 metres) at the Olympian games.
Rowley is a native of Young (N.S.W.), and was born 23
years ago ; he stands sft. 10in., and scales, now, 11.4.
His triumphs began at school, in '9O. He got home
second, by a yard, in N.S.W. Championship of ’95;
winner Cartwright’s time was 9 3-5. Since then, Rowley
has been top-notch sprinter of the continent, and gathered
in several local and interprovincial events at 220yds. and
100yds. In ’9B he journeyed to M.L., and downed that
province’s cracks—Smith and Patrick—over 100yds., dead-
heating Patrick in a 250yds. event. Australasian records
to his credit are : 120yds. in 12sec. (two years back),
220yds. in 22 1-6, «.nd 100yds. in 9 9-10 last season. ’Tis
said that Rowley’s stride averages'Bft. Bin. when at his
top. Standing to his credit are even times for five of eight
100yds. races. Several handicaps and scratcli events, other
than championships, have also gone his way.
* • • »
Edwin Smith, Adelaide’s leading patron of
athletics, claims an Australian record in being President
of 80 cricket and other clubs. He has for years been the
presiding genius of Adelaide Oval, now one of the best
sports grounds in Australia and alleged to possess the
best cycle-track in the world.
Retired champion Larry Foley, aged 48, who
takes a pardonable pride in his youthful appearance and
virility, has got a severe set-hack. The other day a
venerable old gaffer from “ out-back ” with the querulous
voice of age and senile tremblings in his limbs, accosted
the stalwart Larry and mumbled out: “ Glad lam to see
you, Mr. Foley ! I can remember just as if it were yester-
day, my father, rest his soul, takin’ me when a little boy
to see you belt the srml-case out of Sandy Ross. An’your
wearin’ well, Mr. Foley, wearin’ well ! ” Lawrence sud-
denly remembered that he had an appointment and
departed like a man in a dream. This varn isn’t true, but
. that doesn’t matter.
* • • •
C. F. Scott (Ballarat) and D. M. Strickland
(E. Melb.), Harriers, have shunted pot-hunting and gone
after lucre. Scott (50yds. and 60yds. respectively) finished
second in the mile (4.27) and two miles (9.37)‘at Stawell
(Vic.) recently. Same meeting saw Strickland getaway
with the big handicap and the Eight Hour event. In
the latter connection it is amusing to hear that three
groundless protests were entered and dismissed, yet the
prize-money is withheld and all bets remain unpaid pend-
ing “ inquiries.” Statutory declarations and personal
identifications have proved no good, and now Strickland
is asked to “give the full names of his sisters, if any.”
Why not include “ his cousins and his aunts ”?

J. W. Heckenberg, winner of the axemen’s con-
test at Sydney Show, is 34 years old, 6ft. 2in. tall, weighs
14st. 101 b. ; with chest 42Ain., upper arm 16in., fore-arm
13iin., thigh 24in., calf 16in. They chop in all shapes;
but in that one for choice, it seems.
• * * •
“ S.S.” : Bike-racing as a sport is certainly
played out. I paid particular attention to the crowd
around Melb. Exhibition arena on Eight-Hour Day ; it
was a tremendous collection, and watched the programme,
through with the stolidity of Red Indians in council. Nob
the slightest indication of interest or alertness was dis-
coverable till a bike smashed and a rider rebounded off the
asphalt like a rubber ball. Then there was a visible
awakening, an eager, enraptured stir of the whole assem-
blage ; but the man arose to his feet, and walked off the
track with little assistance, and the “ sightseers ” fell sud-
denly into their former condition of lethargy—waiting, it
appeared to me, for the next “ bust up ” ; and as Jackson
came off through the grandstand push, with his back
stripped of guernsey and hide, and his collarbone broken,
scarcely an eye followed him. Had he come off in the
ambulance stretcher with a proper regard for spectacular
effect, his accident would have been a popular success.

Griebenow, the champion cyclist of S. Africa,
mentioned by Argus war-correspondent M‘Donald as
fighting in the Boer ranks, was the man whom our Bill
Martin wanted to beat, and Bill actually went to Africa for
that purpose. But local cycling, controlled by oof-
landers 6f Johannesberg, is purely amateur (the humor of
it!) and after Bill had met the only pro., Van Heerden, he
could not prevail upon the athletic authorities to grant
an exemption to him. Griebenow, on the other hand,
declined to surrender his amateur status, so Bilmartin
had to leave without racing him.

Last round of Sydney second - grade cricket
competition was got rid of Saturday. Burwood second
and Balmain tied in a division, necessitating a decider.
Central Cumberland came out on top of the B lot hand-
somely, thus crediting the club with a double, the first 11
having won the senior-grade struggle.
A remarkable game of cricket was the final for
a £2O cup competed for in Oippgland a few years ago.
The teams were mostly young farmers, and as it was
burning-off season the leaders in the competition turned
up for the final with only three players (no substitutes
allowed). The three won the toss and ran up 108 ; then,
with one of them wicket-keeping and the other two at
point or in slips and bowling alternately, they dismissed
the opposing 11 for 10 and 14. One of the three (Mul-
doon) was described by an interprovincial expert as being
the best bowler he ever saw—fast as Richardson and able

* • * *
A reminiscence of Alec Bannerman. It was in
Melbourne years ago, and Alec was stone-walling as usual.
He had been in for half-an-hour, and had not made a run.
Presently he sent one towards the fence, and started to
run. Then someone in the middle of the crowd yelled,
“ Steady, Alec, for Heaven’s sake 1 ” This convulsed the
crowd, and even Alec smiled.

Melbourne Australasian follows the English
custom of alluding to professional cricketers without an
initial to their names. Thus “H.” Trumble’s average for
the M.C.C. in their Maoriland tour was 8.83, “Oaves
was 8.89. “H.” Graham’s batting average wasi 48,
“ Fry’s ” was 8. This silly distinction is not only snobbish,
but misleading. “Harry” Graham and “ Hughie
Trumble have got in “ expenses ” a deal more out of
cricket than Cave and Fry have earned in “ wages.”

An erstwhile all-Australian champion jowler
Is now working as a mine-trucker.
Otago Rugby Union has decided that in future
no football-team in Otago shall be allowed to travel with-
out being accompanied by a responsible officer of the
Union—this in consequence of a “ not-proven ” charge of
“ drunk and obscene ” against the crack Kaikorai team
whilst journeying by rail to Christchurch.
* * * *
The usual big Vic. contingent was absent from
the annual tourney of Sydney Lawn Tennis Club, opened
Saturday. Gaden and Bice (N.S.W. champions) beat
Goddard and Irving (Vic. champions) for interprovincial
doubles supremacy—6-3, 4-6, 6-3, 6-4. Best tennis seen in
Sydney for years was supplied in match Rice (Metrop.
champ.) and Irving (Southern Dist. champ.). Both ex-
celled themselves, which is saying a heap. Pace was
hot from the jump, and the driving and net play sur-
prised everyone. Each had it all up throughout, so they
finished the second set and the match completely
pumped. Rice won.

Only two crews (Church of England and Syd.
Grammar School) ranged up for the Championship Fours
at N.S.W. Great Public Schools’ Regatta on the Parra-

Two comers of big promise — Worboys and Tres-
gider—row a match in best-and-best boats over the
"Terrace” course (3 miles), Hunter River (N.S.W.), to-
day. Worboys, a big fellow, who, ’tis said, will take Jim
Btanbury’s place, gets 15sec. start; he is under Peter
Kemp’s mentorship, and has been with him for months.
Tressider —an undefeated man —has been schooled by
Nielsen on the Parramatta. He shows good form and has
staying powers of the first order. The result should intro-
duce another Australian champion.

From l£d. to 2d., the first Australian scare price
for bubonic or presumed bubonic rats, is less than half the
price paid in London and Dublin for rats for terrier-
killing games. When rat-killing for gate-money was a
Bydney sport half a century ago, the average price paid
for rats was 6d., the price on important events going up
to Is. apiece.
* • * *
Coolgardie, when first visited by prospectors,
was ratless. There were a few mice, which mostly fell
victims to the “ goanna.” Six months after the township
was settled the rodents put in appearance at the early
public-houses and stores. A year later, a favorite local
Sunday pastime was rat-killing matches between dogs.
When Kennedy’s hotel was burnt hundreds of rats made
frantic efforts to clear balconies and other avenues of
escape.

The cure of piles by the new “ Cannalin ”
treatment is fully described in the pamphlet,
“Piles, and How to Cure Them,” by Albert
Sharp. Copies, with testimonials, sent free to any
colony from the depfot, 257 George-street, Sydney.*
In ordering that the price of rats be raised
from 2d. to Cd. the N.S.W. Premier has acted
sensibly. The authorities presumably want to
catch all the rats in the province, and two-pence a
head affords no inducement to people to go into
rat-catching as a business. A million rats at 6d.
would only mean £25,000.
Mr. Octavius C. Beale digs up from a
German scientific paper an effective-looking means
of rat-poisoning. Small fish are cut open, smeared
inside with phosphorus - paste, and laid in the
driest parts of the rodents’ haunts. “ The plan is
most simple, and probably succeeds so well because
rats cannot fail to see the bait, whilst the phos-
phorescence of fish would be to them a natural
phenomenon.”
The Acetylene Gas Company of Austra-
lasia, Ltd., is to be congratulated on its success
at the Royal Agricultural Show, having secured no
less than twenty-five first prizes for its exhibits,
consisting of automatic, non-automatic and port-
able generators, also buggy, miner’s, stevedore’s,
bandsmen’s, fireman’s, bicycle, table, hand and
other lamps for acetylene gas. Ordinary carriage-
lamps converted into acetylene lamps; correspond-
ence invited on all outdoor and indoor lighting.*
The 15-year-old prosecutrix in a recent age-
of-consent case at Ballarat (Vic.) tripped jauntily
into the box, and, after narrating the particulars
of the case, negligently observed that she had
been seduced before she was 12, had since “kept
bouse ” several times, had had two mishaps, and
was at last about to increase the population. And
after this crowded and varied experience the law
suddenly wakes up and discovers that this tender
innocent needs the “ protection !” of the criminal
code.
Valentine Bundy, the cook of the lost
Glenelg, is a stolid person, and at the inquest
gave his evidence with the impassiveness and the
stony accuracy of a mile-post. So long as a mile-
post is dead sure of the one thing it is its business
to know —that it is exactly a mile to the next post
—it will look on at any kind of tragedy unmoved.
Bundy was a sort of mile-post. He cooked the
last meal with his usual accuracy, and washed the
pots so that they might sink clean, and then he
turned in. Being called up with the rest to bale
the sinking vessel he came on deck. “ And what
was the first thing that attracted your attention ?”
asked the court. “ Nothing,” said the stony cook.
A howling ocean and a foundering ship were things
that some cooks might have noticed, but they
didn’t catch Bundy’s eye, so to speak.
The spread of the plague goes on slowly,
but surely, and the tackling of the rat population
of the Australian seaboard becomes day by day a
more urgent necessity. The importance of the
rat-element is being publicly recognised after
weeks of futile effort by the health authorities,
and even the city fathers are beginning to under-
stand the danger. The mischief, however, has
been already done. Melbourne, Adelaide, Free-
mantle, Albany, Brisbane, Rockhampton, Auck-
land all these show infected rats, and the
development among human beings, to a greater
or less extent, according to the sanitary
conditions and the vigor of the Govern-
ment measures, is merely a matter of
time. Melbourne wharves, particularly, swarm
with dead and dying rodents, although the fact
has not been made as public as it should be.
Unless imported direct, the first Melbourne cases
will come from the river population, and then the
Sng co’s will receive a shock compared to
the present falling-off of returns will be a
mere titillation. The writer ventures to tip a
lighterman as the first candidate for Melbourne
honors. Living a filthy life and spending his
time in endeavouring to dodge the very regula-
tions that are framed to protect him, the lighter-
man forms tempting bait for an epidenr \

William Marshall, the Melbourne theatrical printer, is
in a bad way, suffering from dropsy. The Profesh will be
sorry to hear this. Marshall’s readiness to assist struggling
companies with “ tick ” for their printing has made him a
popular identity these many years, and when he got burnt
out —at the time of the Bijou fire, wasn’t it ?—there was a
big rally of actors to give their old friend a complimentary
ben.
Mrs. Dante is just now in Sydney awaiting the arrival of
her late husband’s posthumous child.
Fritz Mueller, the wondrous boy-pianist from Mel-
bourne, is to be starred in London at a concert arranged
by the enthusiastic Melba, who reckons that the little
nipper will hit the cognoscenti very hard.
Melba denies that she was born in Scotland. Declares
that she first saw light in Burnley-street, Richmond, Mel-
bourne.
Ada Reeve had at latest divorced Bert Gilbert. It was
about time the couple parted. Their constant rows and
semi-public reconciliations made the public tired.
One set of English papers insinuated that a certain
clever lady had a hand in Haddon Chambers’ brilliantly-
successful play, “ The Tyranny of Tears ” ; shortly to be
produced in Sydney by the Broughs, for the first time in
Australia, while others suggested that Oscar Wilde had
a finger in the pie. Which made Chambers Wilde m
two senses.
The unladylike conduct of the Queen in smacking Essex
in the third act of “Queen Elizabeth” is, after all, not
quite so bad as it looks. Elizabeth was 68 then and Essex
34. So she doubtless lashed out at his ear in a motherly
spirit.
“ Tim B.” : Heard lecturer Chas. Clark for the first
time (in Wellington, M.L.) the other day. Subject:
“ Macaulay,” and peroration, picturing the funeral pro-
cession in Westminster Abbey, worthy of Macaulay him-
self. One of Clark’s anecdotes of Macaulay is a well-pre-
served old friend. The historian had been accustomed to
shave himself, but one day he submitted his chin to the
hands of a barber. The operation over, Macaulay asked
“how much?" “Oh, what you usually give the man
who shaves you, sir 1 ” “ That,” replied Macaulay, with
a smile, “ would be a gash on each cheek 1 ”
Pattie Browne, at latest dates, was said to have been
engaged by Arthur Roberts for a season at London Strand.
Nat Goodwin’s luck, at a pretty low ebb when he left
these shores, has taken a turn of late. His new show,
“ When We Were Twenty-one,” with himself and scrump-
tious Maxime Elliott in the chief parts, is coining money
around America.
Frank Denton, the promising young member of Charles
Arnold’s eo., is a parson’s son, and quite new to the busi-
ness. Sons of the church nowadays take to the stage as
naturally as ducks swim. The preacher is father to the
actor, in fact, most preachers are actors.
The natural and unaffected acting of J. L. Sheridan’s
four-year-old youngster in “ When the Lamps are
Lighted ” shows that all stage kids should be the pro-
geny of the manager. The usual infant-actor is a hope-
lessly artificial youngster, who by dint of drilling and
smacking has been taught to stand under the snow and
say, “ Me will go wif mama,” in the stilted style of an
amateur Hamlet. When not speaking he stands round
obviously uncomfortable and afraid to move for fear of
Subsequent trouble. Sheridan, jun., confident in the
knowledge that the old man is bossing the show, roams
round as he pleaseß, kicks the furniture, dirties his pinny,
and behaves for all the world like a kid in real life.
Theatrical managers ought to take warning, and always
be careful to have a family for use on such occasions as
these.
“Mac : Why doesn’t somebody instruct dainty little
Dorothy Vane in the art of making-up for the stage?
People who have seen her only on the stage decline to
believe that the delightfully pretty little woman pointed
out to them in the street is the one who nightly trans-
forms herself into a gorgon before the footlights.
Of all the alleged kiddie girls billed as “ Little ” in Pan-
tomime or variety-shows, Fanny Powers is undoubtedly
the youngest and brightest. Some of the others are on
the wrong side of 17.
A well-known Yankee actor-manager tells how Irving once
said to Wilson Barrett at a professional reception : “ Hullo,
Barrett, what are you playing now?” “Hamlet, replied
Barrett loftily. “ Hamlet, eh ?” sniffed Irving. Yes ;
Hamlet. Do vou think you are the only actor who can
play Hamlet?” “Not at all,” said Irving promptly-
“ but I know you are the only actor who can’t.”
A portly Melbourne solicitor and a gentleman friend
once picnicked for several days in tents, in a sylvan
retreat, with two well-known lady-singers. The man
of law had backed bills for the principal songstress.
The ladies took all the attention and gave mere smiles
in return. And Mr. Leges had to meet the bills. The
lawyer does not always score.
“Scriptus”: Local coloring was neglected by drama-
tists and novelists up to the time of Sir Walter Scott.
Shakespeare is a notable offender, and Milton did not
escape the vice. In “As You Like It the forest of
Ardennes is stocked with roaring lion 9 and Arcadian
shepherds and shepherdesses. Although all the charac-
ters ought to have been French, Touchstone and. Audrey
are regular English villagers, and no explanation is given
of the inconsistency. In “ Catherine and Petruchio ” the
housekeeper of the Italian couple is plain Mrs. Curtis. In
“Much Ado About Nothing” we have a regular English
watchman and English magistrate in the heart of Italy,
while the lower characters refresh themselves in an ale-
house in a country where wine would be the only bever-
age. There are also jokes about a hot January as a thing
impossible in Southern Italy, where a <*old January would
be the greater wonder of the two ; and a “ February
face,” meaning showery, in a climate where even February
is more kindly than April is in England. Sir Toby Belch
and Sir Andrew Aguecheek—in “Twelfth Night are
two downright English worthies although purporting to
be citizens of Illyria. In “ A Midsummer Night’s Dream,”
Theseus is Duke of Athens before Dukes were known;
and the pagan Duke applies the term “ nun ” to Hermia’s
intention of becoming priestess of Diana. In “ Measure
for Measure ” we find Italian names, although the scene is
in Vienna. The divine William, like the old painters who
represented the Prodigal Son in point lace, had never
heard of local coloring. [There is small doubt that
Shakespeare looked upon chronological and topographical
correctness as of little importance, so long as his presen-
tations had, in his own phrase, the “ touch of nature that
makes,” &c. He was right. Impertinent accuracy of
detail would have been out of keeping with the grandeur
of his conceptions ; and “ The Winter’s Tale,” for in-
stance, suffers nothing by the attribution of a sea-coast
to Bohemia.]
A musical composer liad a composition performed at a
church not many Sundays ago, and it is said that a day or
two previous to the performance hand-bills were printed
announcing that owing to the great success of the work
it would he repeated by General Desire. A certain well-
known Australian entrepreneur did the same thing with
a fiddler’s “first and only ” concert at a city Town Hall
some years ago. During the interval the ushers rushed
around with slips announcing that the fiddler had been
induced to stay and give another concert for the sake of
Musical Culture.
A very old document lately found in the municipal
archives of Helsingors, or Elsinore (Denmark), sets forth
that certain English actors whose names are given
behaved like larrikins in that town in 1555, and destroyed
a wooden fence, to the great indignation of the Danish
Bumble of the period. Some of the names are those of
people said to have been associated with Shakespeare, so
it is suspected that Shakespeare himself may have been a
frisky hoodlum in Denmark in 1585, and have gathered
geographical information for “ Hamlet ” on the spot.

“ Lyah ”: Not generally known that the “ What, ho I—
she bumps ’’ gag, now so much in vogue in Australia,
originated with Noah when the ark first touched bottom.
Whereupon there was much joy in the menagerie. Also,
the expression will probably be the very latest ever used
by mortal man when some day a huge pugilistic comet
biffs this conceited little planet into nothingness, and
Gabriel advertises in the S.M. Hhrald for a new globe to
be taken on trial, with permanent engagement if found
sober and truthful.
Perth (W.A.) has what is proudly termed a “ Zoo.” To
get there, you cross the river in a boat, the wheel of which
is situated right astern, behind an elevated deck that pre-
vents the captain seeing an inch ahead of his nose.
Therefore, being an ingenious man, he stands wrong way
about, and takes his bearings from the shore astern.
When he bumps into a sandbank he knows he has got
halfway, and waits patiently until the tide floats him off
to do the rest of the passage. In the course of time you
land and reach a turnstile entrance, through which, on
payment of sixpence, a melancholy person, apparently
overcome with the prevailing dismal ness of the place,
passes you in to view one lion, three guinea-pigs, two
small crocodiles, half-a-dozen parrots, a small ourang-
outang, one bear, three languid emus, one mangy ostrich,
a squirrel and some goats. The latter are in a small wire-
netted enclosure, which bears a finger-board with
“ Ladies ” on it; and the writer could swear they were
gentlemen goats ! The rest of the stock consists of empty
cages, the defunct inhabitants whereof visitors are still
requested “ Not to tease.” Truly, a cheerful spot 1

“Sidelight”: Looking recently at a portrait of the
beauteous but tenuous Oora Potter in the character of
Rosalind, writer was reminded that for years she declined
the part on the ground that she was “ too womanly to
adopt male garb.” But when at last Mrs. P. did stage
“ As You Like It,” it became obvious that the thinness of
the excuse was fully equalled by the thinness of the lady’s
legs.

In a Sydney police-court dock, shoulder to
shoulder, last week, stood three drunks named
respectively Caulfield, Meagher and Norton —but
they were not the well-advertised trio.
Kalgoorlie, which offered £4OO for a coun-
cil clerk, had 157 applicants—4B from N.S.W., 42
from Vic., 20 from S.A., and 48 from Westralia
itself. Of the lot 11 were lawyers ; most of the
others were accountants or engineers. A Vic-
torian, E. E. Hawkins, got the job.
During cleansing operations in a busy
portion of Sydney last week a peculiar discovery
was made in the shape of a cartload of tobacco,
cigars, bottled beer, and cider. This little haul
was unearthed in an underground passage, where
it had evidently been placed by a smuggler some
15 years ago, as it is known that several iron bar-
ricades, not since shifted, were placed there about
that time.
N.S.W. judicial dicta : •
District Court Judge Murray {teste Solicitor R. H.
Levien, M.L.A.): “ A prisoner cannot be blamed for tak-
ing advantage of a loophole to escape from gaol.”
District Court G. C. Heydon, in the Benjamin
Bridge case:
While there is no moral indignation against a man for
escaping from gaol, still it constitutes flouting of the law
and consequently cannot be passed over.
There is not much difference between these pro-
nouncements, but the little there is should be
threshed out among jurists generally and a sound
uniform principle be affirmed. If it be ruled that
prisoners commit no offence, or only a formal one,
by effecting escape, gaoler and prisoner will realise
the relation, ana while the latter will not be a
worse prisoner the former will need to be a better
gaoler. Broadly, prisoners convicted in criminal
courts will stand in the same relation to their
gaolers that prisoners of war stand in to their
captors.
“D.C.L.,”
When applied to a man, is a high academic dis-
tinction. When applied to whisky, “D.C.L.”is
the highest distinction it can possess. “ D.C.L.”
(Black Bottle) Scotch Whisky is perfect.*
Just at present some people are in such a
state of hysterical exaltation over Jingo matters
that if you don’t pass the mustard quickly enough
they reckon it is an evidence of treason, and call
you a “pro-Boer.” Postmaster-General Watt, of
Victoria, has found that out. Apparently he re-
fused leave of absence to somebody who wanted to
go somewhere, or at least he did something to
somebody, or refused to do something—anyhow, a
silly female thought she saw in it an evidence
of “disloyalty,” and she called on Watt with a
shriek and a cane, and tried to batter him. Later
on, being interviewed, she cried out wildly
that she would die for her Queen and country.
The matter of the lady’s grievance isn’t very clear,
but it seems to be more or less connected with the
latest contingent the one that camped in the
mud and caught such a dreadful cold in the head,
and poor Watt, who spends most of his time try-
ing to find out why the Melbourne telephones
buzz so horribly, was held responsible.

Business, Robbery, etc.
The professional trade swindlers who played out
one or other of the Australias years ago, and have “ given
others a turn” in the interim, appear to be now
changing their quarters again. The stylish lady who,
when “things were booming” years ago, “landed”
almost every prominent retail trader in a big city, is back
once more. During her prior residence in that city,'she
acquired the art of renting a house at £lO a week (worth
10s. a week) from a confederate landlord, who regularly
sold-up for back rent such chattels as the lady might in-
duce tradesmen to deliver on credit. A confederate
auctioneer knocked down the goods for next to nothing,
to pre-arranged bogus-buyers and the grass-widow,
auctioneer and landlord whacked the profits realised
.when the goods were subsequently sold by the auctioneer.
The lady swindled tradesmen, at that time, and in that
way, for about £160) in seven months. As a married
woman she could not be summoned for fraud, and hubby
was always away “on his station in Maoriland” when
wanted. Now, her former debts are barred by the
Statute of Limitations, and, relying on new firms’ anxiety
for biz., and the forgetfulness of old creditors, &c., she
has a fair chance of “ducking” tradesmen once more
(Bulletin readers excepted). Verb sap. : Ware “ the
lady who comes into her inheritance three months
hence.” Beware also of the same lady when she tries to
allay your doubts (you may be mesmerised by her into
having no doubts whatever) by producing a bulky packet
of “family papers,” including an attested will in her
favor. That will has been annulled by a much later one
now in force, leaving her with her wits only, which are
keen and plentiful.
* « * •
Another artist who once came to grief through
“speculating” in station supplies, &c. (to the extent of
£5BOO in four months) which he promptly realised at a
huge loss—to the suppliers—inasmuch as they get noth-
ing in the £, is again on the job. This gentleman used to
have a free and merry way of buying (in various names) a
“going business”—no matter in what line so long as
there were saleable assets attached. He always bought
on long-dated bills, to be cancelled in one month from
date of purchase were the business or businesses found
by him to have been misrepresented. This proviso
inspired confidence. Busybodies often told him he had
“ paid too much,” &c., &c., but he always replied that he
“ could afford to drop a few hundreds.” Which was true
(in a sense) as he invariably sold out for cash at a heavy
nominal loss before the first of his own purchase-bills be-
came due. He recently bought (on bills) what was
described as a “ snug little agricultural area with com-
fortable homestead.” Elegant stationery and the com-
manding tone of his orders written on same have yielded
considerable plunder, so far.
A well-groomed pal of the aforesaid gentleman,
who also “ did time ” years ago, relies chiefly on the effect
produced by a big bundle of bank-notes (genuine and
bogus) which he carelessly hauls out when paying for a
“few little knick-nacks for my children,” who invariablv
accompany him. The initial purchases, although small,
are always 18-carat, and easily pawnable at a discount.
In paying for the goods (generally jewellery) he con-
trives to accidentally drop out of the bundle of notes a
genuine one of £5O ; allows the shopkeeper sufficient time
to pick up, appreciate its value, and to return same with
—u ria’ly—the expression of “ hoping for further patron-
age, &c. If the prospects, in his judgment, warrant it,
further and larger purchases follow later—on credit. The
(genuine) £5O note, with five genuine £1 notes, constitute
Jus stock-in-trade (all the other notes in the big bundle
are bogus). On a £55 capital he has thus secured (on
Judgment Day credit) quite £BOO of goods, that could be
readily realised for at least £350 cash. Traders, especially
jewellers, who have been “ had,” will regret to learn that
the gentleman cannot be charged with fraud, as he takes
care to make no verbal misrepresentation, but simply
rehes °P the impression created by “appearances,”
which (in this case) are, as the proverb says, “ de-
ceitful.”

A much-embarrassed Southern city man saves
his own time and that of his many pressing creditors by
pasting this notice on his outer-office door: “To solici-
tors’ clerks, creditors, and other nuisances :—Writs, sum-
monses, notices of judgment and other aggressive docu-
ments served upon J.S. (my office-bov) will be accepted as
good and proper service on myself. ‘ Printed notices to a
similar effect will be handed to servers (if desired) by the
boy named. N.B.—Kindly close the door after you ; and
be sure to mind the step 1! ”

Most of the insolvents now passing through
Melb. court are public servants, and the majority of those
are minor railway employes. In nearly every case the
schedules mention the same well-known set of money-
lenders. The debtors, as a rule, drift into court through
the good old railway habit of passing the tip along the line
when some jockey travelling with horses has blabbed the
secret of an alleged good thing. Civil service usurers
usually charge half-a-crown a month on £l, or 150 per
cent.
* • • •
Dog-ownera are fairly cornered in one Vic.
suburb. The local council employs a dog-catcher who is
paid so much a head per canine captured and destroyed
by him. The “catcher” notifies his friend “The
Inspector of Dog Licenses” for same suburb re the
removal of So-and-so’s dog, and the Inspector thence-
forward “ marks” Mr. So-and-so’s house for a new canine
arrival. When the new bow-wow arrives a “ license
notice ” is immediately served on the new owner by the
inspector, who reaps half the ss. license fee, which he
divides with his colleague (the dog-catcher). The dog
catcher had previously given a “cut” out of his
“catching” fees to the Inspector, who from his
special knowledge indicates easy captures. A sweet
arrangement!

“ X.Y.Z.” : Know a suburban landlord who
receives rent (£2 10s.) monthly—and enters up in tenant’s
book, in two amounts (£1 7s. fid. and £1 2s. fid.), thus
saving twopenny stamp on receipt.
At last a man has been found guilty of gold-
stealing in Westralia, and sentenced by the Boulder
Court. The man got three months, and deserved double
for his clumsiness. Writer hid a good deal of experience
of the W.A. mines, especially the rich ones, and has seen
direct evidence of a large amount of gold - stealing.
Knew a man who declared he would pay £lO a
week to be allowed to work in a certain mine. After
about two years of it he retired and is now
a gentleman of means in Victoria. Some gold-
buyers of ' Boulder City and Kalgoorlie are rich
because they buy gold at from £2 10s. to £3 per oz., and
ask no questions. A £4-a-week mine-hand has been
known to spend £8 a fortnight on grog and put up a
house and furnish it, piano and all, inside of three
months after getting the job. A new-chum miner
working on the Australia got away with about 30
ozs., which he sold for £BO to an 1.G.8. The latter, a
German, said he only had a few pounds in the shop,
and gave the new chum three or four sovs., telling him to
call for the balance. When he did so the 1.G.8. said :
“ You one damn fool! You steal gold from mine ; I steal
from you. Get out!” And out the new chum wrathfully,
sorrowfully went. Not a few of the big men of the West
got their first lift by gold-stealing. Oh, for the good old
days of Bayley’s, when a majority of the hands used to
steal and no one suspected anybody.

A favorite haunt of the Sydney “ confidence ”
fraternity is outside the shipping offices. Here they keep
a sharp look-out for people who have booked passages,
because a victimised intending voyager is reluctant to re-
main to prosecute. The other day one of these gentry
spotted “his man” on Howard Smith and Co.’s wharf,
Sydney, where the s.s. Koonawarra lay alongside ready to
cast-off for Westralia. The confidence person noticed a
pen of stud sheep on board, and approaching the marked
passenger, inquired how far he was going ? To Perth.
Did he understand anything about sheep. Yes. Well,
the stranger explained that he owned those sheep, that
they were valuable, and that the man he had engaged to
take care of them had not turned up, and he was greatly
worried. Would the passenger look after them for a
fiver, and see that they were landed safely ? The other
willingly consented, and just then the signal to start was
given. “ Oh, I must pay you,” cried the sharper, and he
produced a roll of notes. “ Hang it, they’re all tenners,”
he said. “ Have you got change ? ” The flat handed over
five sovereigns, and received a “ flash ” £lO-note, though
he didn’t make that little discoverv till it was too late.

Thus a Bendigo (Vic.) gold-buying Chow :
“Sposa man walka down stleet welly quick, looka my
shop winda, seeum one, two, tlee customa —go way.
Littee while come back, welly slow, findem shop empty,
lookem up stleet, down stleet —nobedee look see. Him
come in lookem tiightened, speakem welly quick—‘ Wha’
you give for gold?’ I giva that fella two poun’ ten
shillin’ one ounce. But sposa anodda man come, walkem
stlaightin, plenty customa look see, him no care ; talkem
welly loud—‘ Wha’you give for gold?’ I giva that fella
thlee-poun’-ten.”

“ S. W.” : I fancy the only cheating in pak-a-pu
is in tiie shortness of the odds. ' I calculated them once,
and found that the £74 for 10 marks ought to have been
more than ten times, that amount. The system is 80
numbers : mark any 10. At the drawing the 80 numbers
are rolled in little balls and placed in a bowl ; roused
about; divided into four equal parts. Four blocks of
wood marked 1,2, 3, 4 are then wrapped in paper and
placed by any outsider on the top of each bowl. An out-
sider then grabs a handful of “cash” and lays it on the
t idle. This is raked by fours into a box and the
remainder being 0,1, 2 and 3 decide which of the four
2>’s is the “bank.” These 20 are then called out and
clerks mark “Banks” in red ink for distribution to the
public and the agents. If your 10 are all in these 20, you
get £74 ; if 9, £4O ; 8, t’2o; 7, £3 10s. ;6, 9s. ; 5, Is. 2d. ;
4,3, 2, I—nix. In Narandera a syndicate guarantees
£3OO, and that’s all they’ll pay, beginning with the fives
and so on. The bank has been bust and closed several
times. But not for long. It bobs up again, and they give
a handsome subscription to the hospital. I can’t see any
loop-hole for manipulating the numbers, and the Chows
themselves play.

Chinese storekeepers are numerous around
Coonabarabran, Cassilis, Wee Waa, etc., and a certain
Sydney drummer, wishing to do good business amongst
them, got a Chinese merchant in Sydney to write him a
letter introducing his (the drummer’s) house. Translated,
however, the document set forth that “Messrs. Ah Cow
and Co. have in stock all kinds of goods, and supply coun-
try' storekeepers on liberal terms,” etc., etc. That bag-
man was quite surprised to find he was travelling for a
Chinese firm.

Law-courts, when straining to be specially
solemn, are often producers of light comedy. In a suit
for salvage, Brown v. the ship South Australian, defendant
paid into the N.S.W. Court £lOO last October, which sum
plaintiff accepted. Counsel for defendant was willing to
pay' costs up to time of tender which plaintiff’s counsel
was satisfied with, but plaintiff’s coutise) held it was
necessary to apply' to the Court for costs, and the other
counsel held it was not. Justice Owen, in a long judg-
ment, held that it was necessary to apply' for costs, and
ordered the defendant to pay, in addition to the costs
both sides were agreed on, the costs of the application to
the Court to decide whether it was necessary to apply to
the Court for the costs that he could have got without
any application.
• * * *
An interesting question of income-taxability
was settled in Melb. Supreme Court recently'. Three
persons formed a business partnership on the following
terms : —That the share of each partner in the profits
should be carried to his credit each year subject to cer-
tain annual drawings therefrom and the amount carried
to credit should bear interest. The death of any partner
should not dissolve the partnership, but the survivors
should manage the business without any interference
from the executors of the deceased partner. Such execu-
tors should be paid annually all interest credited to
deceased, but should not be entitled to draw any
further moneys until the expiration of the partnership
term. One of the partners died, and during 18i)s
the surviving members of the triumvirate credited
his estate with some £l4OO representing his third
share of the profits, plus accrued interest. The executors
did not include this sum in the estate’s income-tax return
but the Commissioner claimed that, inasmuch as all money
credited to the estate would be receivable by the executors
in due course, these partnership-profits ought to rank as
“ income derived from the produce of property,” and be
taxed accordingly. Justice Hood quashed the Commis-
sioner’s claim ; he held that Death had diddled the I,aw
in this case. The late partnerduring his lifetime was sub-
ject to taxation of business-profits which had been added
to his capital, hut after his death he had no capital to tax.
The dead are stone-broke, as a matter of legal fact. Neither
are the dead partner’s executors subject to income-tax
upon capital which is added to the estate, and as for
the estate it is not an actual person, living or dead, there-
fore it can’t be taxed. Nor is the widow of the dead
partner, who draws all the income of his estate, anyway
taxable as regards profits that are not yet yielding any
income towards the said estate. The spectacle of the
Commissioner impotently coveting a tirx upon a genuine
income which is nobody’s income, and which will never
be taxable at all until it gets converted into property—
this was a spectacle that no kind-hearted judge could gaze
upon unmoved. There were tears in his Honor’s eves
when he dismissed the Commissioner with costs.

Dear Sir.—l feel it my duty to write and tell you how
your remedies have cured me.
I suffered great agony with WEAK NERVES and
RHEUMATICS, and I sent for some of your ALFALINE
REMEDIES, which completely cured me, and I have felt
as well as I could wish, although it is two years ago.
also tried your ALFALINE UNIVERSAL PILLS,
whicl completely cleared my skin and purified my blood,
and I am now using your ALFALINE HAIR RESTORER,
whicA has thoroughly cleaned the scalp and made the
hair grow again, and It has gradually turned the hair
from being grey to that of its original color. I have also
recommended your Remedies for a number of different
complaints and I have never known them to fail, but to
do all that was claimed for them. I have great pleasure
to recommend these remedies to all sufferers, as I know
they can be depended on.
You are at liberty to publish this as well as my photo.
Yours truly, 0. J. A. NIELSEN.
