Rescued from Nervous Suffering.
Mr. THOMAS MUDIMAN,
The well-known Tailor, of 327 Klizaheth-
street. Sydney, states, on oath, how his
shattered nervous system was completely
cured and restored.
Statutory
Declaration.
I, THOS. MUDIMAN, Master Tailor, of 327 Elizabeth-
street, Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
hereby solemnly and sincerely declare as follows: —
Whereas I suffered severely from a very troublesome
affection of the NERVES, which affected me so much that
I was quite unable to attend to my business. I tried
several doctors and many remedies, but all of no avail.
Whereas I, as a last hope, obtained some of Herb Rass-
mussen’s Purely Herbal ALFALINE VITALITY PILLS,
which I hereby solemnly and sincerely declare COM-
PLETELY CURED ME within a few weeks, and although
this is over five years ago I have remained well ever since.
The ALFALINE PILLS also cured my wife, and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same
to be true.
Declared at Sydney this 29th day of April, 1898.
THOMAS MUDIMAN.
CHARLES COLLINS, Justice of the Peace.

Editor Bulletin. — S.M. Herald war-
correspondent, “ Banjo ” Patterson, says it is
impossible to dodge shells, because the shell
travels faster than the sound of the discharge,
while correspondent MacDonald tells how the
Ladysmith people became, during the siege, quite
expert in evading shells. Who is correct ? —S.
[Warning was given at Ladysmith the moment
the smoke of the discharge was seen.]
Ed. Bulletin. tie magisterial vagaries.
At a Sydney suburban police-court, the other day,
the presiding S.M. asked a witness whether it
was a peneral rumour that it was defendant’s dog
that frightened plaintiff’s horse and thus caused
him to bolt and kill himself. Witness said “ Yes,”
whereupon Solon expressed himself as satisfied
that said dog ivas the culprit, and sentenced the
owner accordingly. Thus was “ general rumour ”
—to wit, hearsay—allowed to outweigh the direct
testimony of four or five witnesses to the con-
trary. How’s that for the law of evidence !
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
A Westralian paper, published on a rival
goldfield, calls Coolgardie “The Beggar City,”
and goes for it in this fashion ;
, Like a decayed lady in her tattered finery, the boom
city of Coolgardie vainly endeavors to keep up the appear-
ance of respectability on an empty pocket and with no
visible means of support. Not long ago a deputation of
big bugs from Wildcatopolis, consisting of the flower and
aristocracy of the place, waited on a long-suffering Pre-
mier to air their grievances and ventilate their wants.
When Sir John goes up to Coolgardie, he wears a placard
on his coat-tails, “ Ask and ye shall receive.” There is no
trouble about the asking, for this particular electorate has
been spoon-fed for a long, long time at public expense.
The deputation brought up that very smellful transaction,
the Great International Exhibition that Coolgardie made
of itself last year, and, as usual, wanted more money to
pay off old debts, They want to turn the Exhibition
Building into a School of Mines, and the tax-
payer is to put up £3500 the first year and
£l5OO per annum afterwards to keep it going.
The Council also want £lOOO refunded that they
put up as a bluff for the show so that the
Ministry could straddle the blind and go some
better. A cannery for potted wild cat would suit the
surroundings better, for what Coolgardie don’t know
about mines and their flotation is hardly worth knowing.
The whole thing was a bluff. The town council offered to
put up £3OOO if John would give £5OOO. John promised
it, and, as he himself told the deputation, he finds
the council only put up £lOOO, and now wants it back.
The cast-iron cheek of Coolgardie would do credit to a
book fiend. Kalgoorlie asks for next to nothing. It goes
on its own bottom, while Coolgardie clutches wildly at
the Premier’s coat-tails to prevent itself slipping down
into the abyss of financial and commercial oblivion that
yawns beneath its feet. And three and a half millions are
being squandered to take the mountain to Mohammed
since Mohammed Won’t come to the mountain. A high
price for a drink of water indeed 1
Chiefly Concerning Pipes.
In toe not very long- ago there was only one pipe, and
meerschaum was the material of which it was made. But
all that has passed like the baseless fabric of a drink-
begotten dream. The pipe of the present day is the Briar
pipe—that is the only true pipe, and no other. The fan-
tastic superstition was that when the smoker mouthed his
meerschaum he drew his vaporised tobacco through solidi-
fied foam of the sea, whereas it was merely a prosaic,
chalky substance, found somewhere in Asia Minor, which
substance was very easily broken and a source of perpetual
worry. The pipe that has ousted the crumbling meer-
schaum is made of the root of the Bruy ire shrub, which
grows chiefly along the shores of the Mediterranean. The
roots are dug up, boiled, dried, and cut into square
blocks and shipped to various parts of the world. The
chief centre of the pipe-making industry was a town some-
where in the Jura Alps, in France, but of recent years an
enterprising English firm has started a factory in London,
and it is from that factory that the world-famous
'B N
BB
pipes, the true and only pipes, the real
successors and superiors of the dead and
gone meerschaum, come. And the man
who wishes to be truly happy in his
> moke has only to buy a pipe bearing
the cabalistic sign aforementioned, and
the words “own make,” to be certain
that there is nothing in the world or the
harem of the Sultan of Turkey that is
better. Originally, amber was the material chiefly used
for the mouthpieces of the best pipes, and it is still re-
garded by the old school as the material “ par excellence,”
but it is brittle, and liable to get broken in the pocket
when the smoker ceases to puff and takes to hugging girls
under the stars, or by the blushing lights of the deserted
parlor, whence all but the lovers have discreetly retired.
So the co., having hugged and suffered, adopted the vul-
canite mouthpiece, which, when made by hand of sheet
rubber, is as elegant as, and more pleasant to the touch,
than amber, and practically unbreakable, unless the owner
falls under a steam-roller, in which case the breaking of
the mouthpiece doesn’t matter. When the smoker has
got his briar, he should only half-fill it and smoke it
indoors till it has got nicely crusted. The crust should
not be removed with the knife, but with a properly-con-
structed bowl-scraper, which will take it out without
injuring the seasoned skin of the wood. The bowl-scrapers
are inexpensive, and the difference the use of them makes
in the flavor and seasoning of the pipe is enormous. Tiie
Bulletin will now proceed to light up its own crusty
volcano, and bless the seed of the man who started that
fact ory in London.*

Simeon Cadden’s Obituary
Poem.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Simeon was dead. The exigencies of the hilarious
Yuletide, when man is imbued with festive spirit,
and moved to “shout "from dawn till midnight,
and overrate the cubic capacity of his brother
combined with the wear and tear of earlier
wassail on a constitution enfeebled by damp gut-
ters and chronic “dyspepsy”-had proved too
many for the husband and father, and he was
gone into the illimitable “over there.” He came
home one night late in the old year, with afurtive
slinking stride ; his eyes were red and wild, his
body was bent as if shrinking from an invisible
horror, his hands fluttered aimlessly before him
and his scalp twitched weirdly. The night was
wet and windy; Simeon was followed into the
warm room by a rush of cold air and ram, and his
relatives wondered much at his awed appearance.
When he endeavored to hide himself behind a
chair, as if unconscious of their presence, they
became fearful and questioned him . 10 s
coming? Why don’t you shut the door?” they
queried, anxiously. Mr. Cadden peered stoni y
between the railings of the chair. Hush ! e
said in a whisper; then, gazing with terrified ex-
pectancy out into the night and intensifying t le
mystery, he murmured: “What’s the good of
shutting the door? They’ll come down the
chimney ! ”
Simeon was put to bed and tended with all
care. For some time his old father remained by
his side, to ward off the ring-tailed wallabies, the
grinning diprotodon, the blue and yellow ichthyo-
saurus, the rampant megatherium, and the many
other children of his sons pleasant fancy. If left
alone for a moment he crawled under his couch, en-
deavored to crowd up the chimney, or armed him-
self with fire-irons and coursed pre-historic monsters
throughout the house and into the street, howling
the while with inhuman ferocity. On the morn-
ing of the third day he died. “ Dyspepsia, ” the
doctor said, with the gravity peculiar to doctors
plain, unvarnished dyspepsia. Mr. Cadden was
upholstered in a pretentious coffin, bedecked with
flowers and enthroned on a parlor table; and the
family settled down to its great grief, llie house
was darkened, and tears were in the air, and the
sound of muffled footfalls and half-stifled sobbing
mingled with faint, far-off shrieks of merriment
coming from the backyard, where the juvenile
Caddens, unimpressed by the solemnity of the
occasion, were taking advantage of the general
neglect to drown the domestic feline in a pie-dish.
It was at this moving moment that Simeon’s
ancient parent called a convention of the house of
Cadden to meet about the remains of the late
Cadden and devise and build a lofty obituary
poem, to commemorate, in words that burn, the
virtues of the deceased, and apprise the world that
his relatives were left lamenting at the prospect of
never meeting his like again.
The idea was received with every manifestation
of approval. The Caddens felt that they would
be wanting in their duty towards the defunct if
they failed to rise to a lofty pitch of poetry, and
duly advertise their sorrow, and assert the in-
dubitable conviction that Simeon was sporting on
the Golden Shore, endowed with the orthodox
Celestial attributes. Indeed, the sentiment of the
age seems to regard the omission of the usual verses
af an act of indecency, and to look upon,the un-
sung deceased as one likely to meet with suspicion
and^superciliousness amongst the better m
Paradise. Simeon was not a poetic subject a-Dante
might have found inspiration for his muse m those,
visions of uncouth preadamite monsters previously
mentioned, but to an amateur, Mr. Cadden pre-
sented no points to inspire the flow of smooth and
musical numbers. He had been a small yeUow
man, with vacillating knees, thin red whiskers a
nose highly inflamed at the base and azure at the
apex, and he had watery blue eyes and receding
chin. Among his other assets were an apologetic
manner and an obtrusive air of having slept last
night in the dog-kennel. His morals he held sub-
servient to all other considerations, and nobody
seemed to consider him of the smallest account till
he died, and was no longer in a position to repudi-
ate the responsibility. . , ...
The committee appointed to draw up the obitu-
ary poem for the Evhnnoose consisted of nine :
Grandfather Cadden, the widow her eldest son,
and two spinster daughters, and two delegates
from a branch ; Uncle Caleb, a gentleman m the
bacon and cheese interest, and Aunt Aureola, an
acid female of strong character. . '
The council ranged itself with sepulchral gravity
about the table and solemnly set to work to grind
out the sad refrain. The eldest son, Ephraim, as
clerk of the court, sat at the head of the board,
armed with paper, pen and ink ready to take down
the testimony as it was offered. Ihe others drie
their tears, and fixing their eyes on space began
to think with back-breaking intensity, Aunt
Aureola alone displaying little enthusiasm, and
manifesting by her demeanor and an occasional
aggravating sniff some contempt for the under-
taking. The amateurs were a long time getting
their dirge under way. Opening lines like Kest,
dear father, rest,” and “ Affection sore long time
he bore,” and “He’s not dead but gone before,
were alternately presented m the form of motions,
were seconded, then elaborately and. somewhat
warmly discussed by the assemblage, and final y
lost on the vote because of their lack oforiginali v
and poetic fire. At length, however, Uncle Caleb
made a bold bid for fame with the following
gem:— , ,
“ Farewell, dear Simeon, thy days are done,
We’ll mourn you till our race is run.
Over on the shiny shore „
Some day we’ll meet you at the door.
This was favorably received by all concerned, and
was carried with acclamation— the idea of meeting
the lamented Cadden at the door of the shiny
shore being regarded as a peculiarly happy e^
After a severe mental struggle, lasting over half-
an-hour, three more lines were concocted and
carried :
“ He came home so gladly,
He came to us in the night,
But, oh, Death came so sadly
Here the procession got bogged again. The
chief contributors to the above were Ephraim, his
elder sister Agatha, and their respected grand-
sire ; but even this combination of intellect could
progress no further, and for a considerable time
the council sat writhing in the throes of composi-
tion, thinking with unprecedented industry. Sud-
denly Aunt Aureola sat up with a jerk and
stated: —
“ And took him when he was tight.”
The family convention started, aghast, and
gazed at the mterjector with pained surprise; but
Aunt Aureola beamed complacently—she thought
she had done remarkably well, and was only un-
deceived when the widow fell into strong hysterics,
and Uncle Caleb, with the air of an expert, cast
severe aspersions on her poetical powers, and
sternly censured her for her lack of feeling. Even
then she only sniffed more fiercely, and said she
didn’t care; it was true, anyhow —a sentiment
that moved Uncle Caleb to deliver a brief review
of the popular obituary poem, in which he lucidly
demonstrated that truth and poetry were incom-
patible, and convicted Aunt Aureola of sacrilege
and entire absence of divine afflatus. That lady,
however, was not convinced of her unworthiness,
so she froze up into a state of stony disdain, and
greeted every suggestion that followed with a
running commentary of snorts. When business
was resumed the widow added her mite to the
collection, concluding the verse with—
“ And cruelly took away his life.”
The foundation of the next stanza was laid by
grandfather Cadden:
“ How hard we thought it was to part,”
and Miss Sophronia, the second daughter, grace-
fully chipped in with —
“ Prom one we loved so dear,”
after which another long wait ensued, whilst
the council wrestled grimly with the Muses.
Grandfather sat with his elbows on the table and
his jaws clutched fiercely in his hands, and gazed
fixodly on Time and Space. The widow
assumed an attitude of pensive contemplation,
and sought inspiration in the chandelier. Uncle
Caleb sprawled over the furniture with his hands
in his liair digging viciously at his scalp, and
struggled and perspired in all the agonies of com-
position. The two daughters gnawed their finger-
nails and clawed at their fringes. A.unt Aureola
sat erect and spiteful. She was the first to speak;
she simpered :
How hard we thought it was to part
From one we loved so dear,
But; now its pleasant to our heart,
For Simeon’s off the beer.
The convention merely vouchsafed a glance of
pained indignation, and continued to ruminate and
agonise. The elder son eventually supplied the
deficiency, thusly
But now he’s gone to realms of rest
We’ll try not to fret because he s not here.
These lines were not adopted without some dis-
cussion, Miss Sophronia contending that the
lines in poetry should be all the same length, con-
sequently “We’ll try not to fret because hes not
here ” was a misfit, and altogether out of step with
the foregoing remarks. In proof pf which the
lines were measured with a bit of string and com-
pared—a mechaAical criterion that eloquently
affirmed Sophronia’s judgment. However, the
sentence in dispute was written close, and subse-
quently passed on the voices.
For five hours the committee continued its ardu-
ous labors, at the end of which time the following
additional result had been conferred upon Austra-
lian literature :
He was a husband good and true,
A better father we couldn’t find,
And a brother, oh, most true —
On this earth there is too few.
Long he was with pain oppressed,
Now he’s gone to eternal rest.
We miss him here, we miss him there,
We miss our Simeon everywhere.”
This was regarded as a stanza redolent of all
the characteristics of the popular obituary poem,
and a brilliant effort of versification. It was
attained despite the gross materialism of Aunt
Aureola, who would persist in interjecting vulgar,
debasing facts bearing on the moral weaknesses of
the deceased. How long the council would have
remained under the influence of the divine afflatus
is difficult to imagine had not grandfather Cadden
suddenly called the members back to prosaic
realism with a timely warning that the obituary
was running into money.
It is a sad commentary on the greed of news-
paper proprietors that post-mortem prosody must
be measured by pecuniary considerations. A
rough calculation revealed the fact that Simeon s
obituary-poem had already exceeded the pro-
scribed inches. So sections were jointed together,
and Ephraim assumed a declamatory attitude and
read to the assembly the results of its united
efforts, to the immense satisfaction of all, e j c< ? e P t "
ing only Aunt Aureola, whose feelings had been
harrowed by the indiscriminate rejection of her
suggestions. When the reading was concluded,
that excitable female bounced up like a spring-doll
and, gesticulating fiercely, screamed :
“ I don’t care ! It’s a pack of rubbish and lies,
and I’ll write some obituary poetry myself,
and tell how Simeon was always trying to get
into hotels on the blessed Sabbath, and how he
pawned my boots and umbrella to bet on a dog-
fight, and how he was a lazy, shiftless swiper so
I will! and I’ll pay for it to go into the papers
myself, and be independent of the lot of you!
Gone to realms of rest, has he ? Don’t believe it,
and I won’t have my name put to such a wicked
pack of lies ! A husband true and kind was he f
A better father you could not find, eli. Hah !
You know he was a miserable sot, and it 11 be
money in your pockets, now lie’s gone, if you only
save his Police-court fines. If you’d put in those
things I composed it’d have lit up your paltry
lines, and made them interesting, and a lesson to
the unregenerate, but I don’t care. 11l write
some verses myself, and tell about him going to
bed in his boots, and let the publio know just
what he was! There!” And Aunt Aureola
bounced out in a great state of agitation, leaving
the bereaved widow and her spinster daughters in
violent hysterics among the overturned furniture.
Simeon Cadden’s obituary poem appeared m
due time over the names of his mourning widow,
Cecilia Cadden ; his loving daughters, Sophroma
and Agatha Cadden ; his lamenting son, Ephraim
Cadden ; his fond father, Jabez Cadden ; and Ins
affectionate brother, Caleb Cadden. Auno
Aureola was severely omitted.

The Lonely Road.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
P"ea, I have smiled as men may smile whose
hearts within them die ;
sTea, I have heard the Voices call, and turned and
said “Good-bye !”
Good-bye, my love, good-bye !
White powdered worlds wane dim above; the
pregnant East grows gray—
Tired, trembling hands upon the rein; tired eyes
to see the day.
Last eve your twining arms still claimed and held
my soul in fee,
To-day the cruel miles spread far, so far ’tween
thee and me;
My love, my love, good-bye !
Oh, we have lived our little hour—our little hour
is dead;
The skies that arch another day bum grandly
overhead.
Now nor’ward, nor’ward winds the track —how
heavy falls the goad !
For I shall think and dream of thee along the
lonely road—
My love, dear love, good-bye !
Yea, when our camp-fires red the night beyond
the plain’s extreme,
Then shall I read among the coals, and dream, and
dream, and dream. . .
And ne’er above still sleeping trees shall climb a
great.white moon,
And ne’er across the Bush shall fall soft shades of
afternoon,
And never through their mid-day haze the blue
hills sfniling be . . .
But will their tender glories call my Fancies back
to thee ?
So not good-bye !
Nay, not good-bye ! So short is Life, so fair is
Love, so dear ;
And theirs shall surely be the Earth who hope and
nothing fear.
No, not good-bye !
For they must wait in ways obscure, who wait the
Living Light ;
But they shall see the Sun uprise who watch
through all the Night.
N.S.W.
E. J. BRADY.
Mrs. Flynn’s Sofy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Her name was Mrs. Flynn, and she lived in
Somebody’s Lane. Her home was one of those
sun-dried cottages that boast a plot of coarse
grass and rank red geraniums, showing dusty
leaves above the decrepit watering-pots and bat-
tered kerosene tins in which they are ensconced.
She was a small, blackish woman ; her skin har-
monised with the musty dark skirt, the hem
whereof was continually drawn into her flapping
shoe-lieel as into some inevitable vortex. She
also belonged to the class of the Husband-Beaten.
A writer once remarked that there is no definite
type of wife-beater, but that there is a distinct
type of woman that always manages to get beaten,
be her lord Lancelot or Bill Sikes. Some day,
when magistrates are educated in psychology,
women who get thrashed by their husbands will
be arrested for the crime, while their unfortunate
victims receive the well-merited sympathy of an
enlightened public.
_ This, however, is a digression. Mrs. Flynn’s
little weakness is merely mentioned to show why,
when Patrick Flynn died, Mrs. Flynn grieved for
him with an exceeding great grief.
There were six other Flynns left; three married
and settled, the other three still at home. As
they had grown up decent-living and steady, and
had likewise not the merit of being dead, Mrs.
Flynn cared very little about them. With a cer-
tain class of women (and men) deadness is a great
merit. All Mrs. Flynn’s love was for the de-
parted, and her only consolation was to gather
together the sacred relics of the defunct (a pair of
moleskins, a flannel shirt or two, and so on) and
weep over them whenever any audience was avail-
.able.
When she had wept sufficiently she would place
them on top of the worn-out chest before her
parlor-window, said chest becoming henceforth a
:shrine.
But the two girls, when they left school, began
to use the parlor as a sewing- room. Hence a
vividly-worded objection to father’s old clayey
trousers asserting themselves as if by way of
advertisement below their neat sign—“Miss
Flynnes, Dressmakers; Unrivalled for Fit and
Style.” At this undutiful conduct Mrs. Flynn
wept, and managed to stow one or two of the
sacred shirts in her own crowded wooden box.
But for the rest of the relics there was no abiding-
place. They were chivvied about from one
squalid bedroom to another, and the culmination
was reached one day when Mrs. Flynn found her-
self just in time to save them from the old-clo’
woman bribed by her unfilial offspring to take
them away, as it were, by accident.
This was too much. Mrs. Flynn saw that strong
forces were arrayed against her treasures, and that
to defeat these forces she must in a sense succumb
to them. As she sat in the kitchen, brooding over
her wrongs, her eye fell on the settle—which, to be
genteel, she generally referred to as her Sofy, and
a brilliant idea flashed into her brain.
It was an old settle which had been re-covered
some half dozen times, parts of the early coverings
showing through various rents in the last dusty
layer of cretonne (bought cheap from a second-
hand-clothes shop). On this seat the defunct Pat-
rick had often sat quaffing his evening beer ; from
it he had arisen to give her her daily correction,
and on it he had rested from his labors when that
duty was duly accomplished. Hence the Sofy was
hallowed by associations that were tender both
mentally and physically. The holes in the cover-
ings showed that much of the stuffing had fallen
away. What better could she do than fill up this
vacant lot with the relics ? They would be safe,
and two hallowed objects of her affections would
be indissolubly bound together.
When the girls returned from a Manly outing
that evening they expressed entire approval of
the innovation. The Sofy looked plump and
smooth beneath its irregular lines of new' white
stitching, and father’s trousers certainly made it
feel a lot more comfortable.
Some years passed, and the family prospered.
Plush photos took the place of almanacs, and a
cursing cockatoo in a green cage aroused the envy
and admiration of the neighborhood. Even the
kitchen was subject to renovation, but one article
of furniture remained untouched—a very old very
tired, very dingy-looking settle. This ancient
piece of furniture was a sore vexation to whichever
Muss Flynn had to have her boy in the kitchen
while the other was “ fitting-on ” some of the local
aristocracy m the parlor.
-Mrs. 1 lynn heard of the plague’s appear-
ance in the city she had no idea that the visitation
was to have anything to do with her Sofy: in
fact, she dismissed the matter with shrill scorn,
lo her mind, there must always be a certain
amount of illness in the world ; “an’ if we’ave
got this ’ere Plygue, why, it means we won’t ’ave
no typhoid or infiuenzar, that’s all.” The reported
deaths of several victims left her philosophy un-
touched, and she did jiot become uneasy till
rumors reached her of what Guv’ment was going to
do in the way of cleansing the city. This, to
Somebody s Lane, was a long way worse than the
“Plygue.'’
1 hen .Tuliar burst in with the glad tidings that
when the Plygue reached any district, Gov-
ernment sprinkled all your furniture with some-
thin k, and if anythink was awful bad, they burnt
it and gave you a nice new one in its place. At
this, the inhabitants of Somebody’s Lane arose
and thoughtfully endeavored to give their belong-
mgs a plaguey expression, and Mrs. Flynn sat
still with fear at her heart. The “gurrls”’ glad
expectations of a new Sofy that would do for
the parlor found no echo in her soul. Inwardly
she saw her beloved Sofy dragged out and
sacrilegiously disinfected, and perhaps burned,
t hat was, if the Plygue came to the district
which the Sofy inhabited. The following
morning a case was reported two streets off from
Somebody’s Lane !
That evening the youngest Flynn boy, assisted
by one of the Flanagan tribe, might have been
seen stealing down the Lane, carrying the Sofy
between them. Half-an-hour later it was safe in
the backyard of a married daughter who lived in
a still uninfected water-suburb.
When the married daughter’s husband came
home and stumbled over the Sofy, he cursed
brightly three or four times and promptly refused
it house-room. That night it rained heavily, the
moisture penetrating to the clay-soiled relics
entombed in the couch. A belated fowl that had
fallen victim to a lock-out perched with muddy
feet on its outposts ; and later a cat came and ate
its bubonic rat on the sodden shelter of its dubious
cushions. In the morning, when the husband had
gone to work, the married daughter dragged the
disrespected relic into the kitchen, and hanging
the children’s washed pinnies over its microby
back, hospitably dried the lot before the fire
together.
Put by the next day Mrs. Flynn heard that the
Plygue was in this suburb; so the Sofy did another
moonlight flitting, and put up at the other end of
the city with a married son, whose wife, not tak-
ing kindly to the visitor, propped it up against
the coal-cellar and offered it to the dog as a habi-
tation and a home. The dog, however, preferred
the cold, but sanitary, earth. Two days later,
pursued by the relentless Plygue, it once more
fled’ via Flanagan and Flynn, on this occasion to
the other married daughter’s, a mile or two dis-
tant. The Sofy was now in such a state that the
second husband had to be carefully kept in
ignorance of its arrival.
For the next three weeks Mrs. Flynn’s Sofy
dodged the Plygue round Sydney, every now and
then, as things looked better, returning for a spell
to its ancestral home. Every morning Mrs.
Flynn, growing more unkempt each day from
anxiety, spelt her way through the plague-news,
calculating, with feverish brow, the safest resi-
dence for her beloved article of furniture.
But by this time the patience of the children-in-
law had given out, and they refused to provide
lodging for the settle any longer. They alleged-
science having now penetrated to the proletariat
that it would soon be a matter of board as well
as lodging, as the number of plague-germs the
Sofy must have accumulated in its many wander-
ings would soon be clamoring for human diet.
Mrs. Flynn wept and pondered, and, having
come to the conclusion that the Sofy for its
health’s sake could not stay at home, suddenly
remembered an old pal of the deceased who lived
in a safe district. He and the defunct had often
drunk much beer together, and they had generally
beaten their wives extra hard on the sameday (Satur-
day). These things, Mrs. Flynn knew, always con-
stituted a strong bond of affection between men.
Besides, Riley was a sensible man, and had openly
scoffed at the Plygue when last she saw him, -a
fortnight ago.
That evening after dusk, to escape the vigilance
pf the inspectors, the beloved chattel set out on
its last journey. The Flanagan boy had been
forbidden to have any further transactions with
the Flynns’ Sofy, so Mrs. Flynn had to take one
end of it herself.
Riley was in when she arrived; he was sober ;
he was sitting in his kitchen studying the evening
paper with a serious countenance. When Mrs.
Flynn told him the Sofy was honoring his
verandah, and would ’e keep it there till after the
Plygue and not let the kids touch it, he said
“ Certingly.”
At her urgent request he went out and had a
look at the Sofy. But, though she gave him per-
mission to touch it, he edged away and said he
would rather not. He alleged that lie felt it was
too sacred for that.
Mrs. Flynn returned home, her anxiety now
completely at rest. Butphe Sofy had been with
her now three days running,- and the vacancy in
the kitchen struck to her very soul. Bv next
morning she could bear it no longer, and, having
left the cat to house-keep, she went off to enjoy a
glimpse of her Sofy on Riley’s verandah.
The day was warm and invigorating, and she
felt as if all hei troubles 'tvere over.
Turning the corner of the street she saw a
crowd round Riley’s gate. Riley himself was
standing inside talking to an official-looking par-
son with a note-book. On the plot of garden lay
her Sofy, a thin blue cloud of smoke rising from
its earth-stained cretonne seat.
With a shriek she tore up to the open gate, and
• before either could prevent her, flung herself on to
the smoking settle, and tearing something from its
bulging cover, sprang back. Then with a loud
cry of grief as she saw the other relics seized by
the flames, she fled into Riley’s house.
Riley ran to the back-door while the official
guarded the front, and Riley’s wife and children
poured out of the house to say that Mrs. Flynn
had locked herself in one of the bedrooms and
wouldn’t come out.
“ She’ll be hiding them somewhere, and trusting
to your good-nature to keep ’em from me, but you
won’t,” shouted the official.
“ Not me ! I don’t want no Plygue here.”
A little later Mrs. Flynn came out quietly by
the front. There was no p vreel in her hand, so
they rushed in to search the c limnays and various
stowing-places of the house. Meanwhile, as Mrs.
Flynn across the road raised her floppy skirt to
negotiate the curbstone, a musical and explanatory
murmur arose from the crowd—
“ She’s got ’em on ! ”

Mr. It. CRAWFORD.
Don’t Neglect.
The man in the picture is R. Crawford, of Smea-
ton-road, Clunes, Victoria. He is seventy-five
years of age. He looks and talks like a vigorous
man. He IS vigorous for his age.
The title of this article was suggested by a con-
versation recently held with Mr. Crawford, in
which he talked of kidney trouble, and its effects
on the back.. He gave an account of his own
experience with this trouble which will bear
repetition for the benefit it may sring to others
who have found themselves in a similar condition.
“An aching pain in the small of my back was a
perpetual torment to me for years,” said Mr.
Crawford. “My back was so bad I could not rise
after stooping, except with the greatest difficulty.
I had other signs of kidney disease, as the kidney
secretions were unnatural and my sleep was un-
settled. I want to say to you that I have obtained
relief by the use of Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills,
which cleared up the kidney secretions and im-
proved my back ; and I want to recommend this
medicine as a good kidney medicine not only to
any old people of my age, but to any and all who
have any signs of kidney trouble. You are at
liberty to publish these facts if you wish. They
are true and may help others.”
Now Mr. Crawford is an old man. He suffered
from kidney trouble, as many old people do. He
was relieved by Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills,
and his life prolonged.
Is there not food for reflection in Mr. Craw-
ford’s statement both for other aged people and
any and all who have any form of kidney troubles ?
Bear in mind that kidney disease attacks the
young as well as the old, and that backache is one
of the first signs of its approach.

“While living at Broken Hill I was subject to lumbago and rheumatism,
having swollen joints which prevented me from working or going about any.
I tried a great many remedies and put out a great many pounds trying to get
rid of my complaints, but to no good.
“As soon as I put my foot on the ground I suffered terrible pain, so I was
obliged to stay in bed for many months.
“ One doctor told me I had the dropsy, and another told me I would have to
lose my leg.
“ But, having heard of Ayer’s Sarsaparilla, I decided to try it, and now I am
thankful to say it has completely restored me to health.
“ I am now 72 years old, have a splendid appetite, and work hard every
day.”
Mr. John F. Jones, of Cobar, New South Wales, sends us this testimonial
with the above portrait of himself. It is the experience of thousands of others
who have had impurities removed from their, system by Ayer’s Sarsaparilla.
Such? testimonies have given to this remedy the name of
“The World’s Greatest Family Medicine.”
Sick people may be cured if they will only take the right medicine. The
blood must first be made pure, no matter what the disease. With pure blood
nature can go ahead and complete the cure.
